المرصد السادس - المقصد الثاني : المعرف تجب معرفته رسماً، ولا بد فيه أي في المعرف (من مميز مساو للمعرف (فإن كان) المميز (ذاتياً سمي) المعرف (حداً دا وإلا رسما وعلى التقديرين فإن ذكر فيه تمام الذاتي المشترك بينه وبين غيره المسمى بالجنس القريب فتام) إما حد تام مركب من الجنس والفصل القريبين وإما رسم تام مركب من الخاصة والجنس القريب ( وإلا فناقص) إما حد ناقص سواء كان بالفصل وحده أو مع الجنس البعيد ، أو العرض العام عند من يجوز أخذه في الحد ، وإما رسم ناقص سواء كان بالخاصة وحدها، أو مع الجنس قوله : ( ولا بد فيه من مميز مسار إلخ) إما مغايراً له بالذات كما في التعريف بالمركب، أو بالاعتبار كما في التعريف بالمفرد، فهو من حيث إنه معرف ظرف له من حيث كونه مميزاً مساوياً، وقد يقال : الكلام على حذف المضاف أي في حصول المعرف أو شأنه . قوله : ( فإن كان المميز إلخ ) وإذا اجتمع المميزان يسمى رسماً اكمل من الحد، وهو خارج عن القسمين لأن القسم المميز الواحد وإدخاله في القسم الثاني بأن يراد في القسم الأول إن كان المميز ذاتيا فقط غير صحيح، لأنه حصر القسم الثاني في الرسم التام المركب من الجنس القريب، والخاصة والرسم الناقض المنقسم إلى ما يكون بالخاصة ،وحدها، أو بالخاصة والجنس البعيد أو العرض العام، والرسم الأكمل ليس شيئاً منهما . موقوفة على تصوره بوجه يمتاز عن جميع ما عداه، والتوفيق بين كلاميه مشكل لأن التعريف من قبيل الطلب فيلزم أن يمتاز المطلوب التصوري قبل التعريف عن جميع ما عداه ومن لم يعرف ما هذه بعد أن المثلث من الأشكال المضلعة كيف يقال : إنه تصور المثلث بوجه يمتاز عن جميع . عداه ولا شك أن التعريف على الوجه الذي صور إنما يتأتى بالنسبة إلى من علم أن الدائرة ليست بمضلعة، وعلم أن شكلاً من الأشكال يقال له المثلث ولم يعرف أنه غير الدائرة أو عينها، أو علم مجملا أنه غيرها وطلب أن يتصور بوجه مخصوص يمتاز به عنها، ولا شك أنه في الحالة لم يتصوره بوجه يمتاز عن جميع. ما عداه فليتأمل. قوله ولا بد فيه من مميز وظاهر العبارة يشعر بلزوم جزئية المميز مع جواز التعريف بالمفرد، وعلى تقدير وجوب كونه مركباً، لا يلزم أن يكون المميز المساوي جزءاً له، بل يجوز أن يحصل التمييز التام من المجموع كما في تعريف الخفاش الطائر الولود، فقيل : ما ذكره بناء على الأعم الأغلب وقيل المراد في شأن المعرف. قوله : ( فإن كان ذاتياً سمي (حداً) أي إن كان المميز ذاتياً فقط، فالمركب من جميع الذاتيات والعرضيات مندرج في قوله، وإلا سمي رسماً على ما صرحوا من أنه رسم تام، لكنه أكمل من الحد التام. قوله : ( أو العرض العام عند من يجوز أخذه في الحد المركب من الفصل القريب، والعرض العام رسم ناقص على ما يستفاد من كلام المطالع وحد ناقص على ما ذكره الشارح ها هنا، وهو الموافق لما صرح به الرازي في شرح المطالع، حيث أبطل كلام مصنفه بأن الفصل وحده إذا أفاد
The Sixth Observation - The Second Objective: The Definer One Must Know. As a sketch (rasman), and it is necessary in it, i.e., in the definer, (of a distinguisher equal to the definer (if the) distinguisher (is essential, it is called) the definer (a limit; otherwise a sketch. And in both cases, if the entire common essential between it and others, called the proximate genus, is mentioned, then it is complete), either a complete limit composed of the proximate genus and differentia, or a complete sketch composed of the property (al-khāṣṣa) and the proximate genus. (Otherwise it is incomplete), either an incomplete limit, whether by the differentia alone or with the remote genus, or the general accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm) according to those who permit its inclusion in the limit, or an incomplete sketch, whether by the property alone or with the genus. His statement: (And it is necessary in it of a distinguisher equal [musāw], etc.) either being other than it in essence, as in definition by a composite, or by consideration, as in definition by a simple term. So it, inasmuch as it is a definer, is a container for it inasmuch as it is an equal distinguisher. And it may be said: the speech is with the deletion of the genitive, i.e., in the attainment of the definer or its affair. His statement: (If the distinguisher, etc.) And if the two distinguishers are combined, it is called a sketch more complete (akmal) than the limit, and it is outside the two divisions, because the division is [based on] a single distinguisher, and its inclusion in the second division by intending in the first division that the distinguisher is only essential is not correct, because it restricts the second division to the complete sketch composed of the proximate genus and the property, and the incomplete sketch divided into what is by the property alone or by the property and the remote genus or the general accident, and the more complete sketch is not one of them. It [the definition] depends on its conception in a respect distinguished from everything other than it, and the reconciliation between his two statements is problematic, because the definition is of the type of request, so it follows that the requested conceptual thing must be distinguished before definition from everything other than it. For someone who does not know that the triangle is among polygonal shapes, how is it said that it is a conception of the triangle in a respect distinguished from everything other than it? There is no doubt that the definition in the way it is presented only applies to one who knows that the circle is not polygonal, and knows that a shape among shapes is called the triangle and does not know whether it is other than the circle or the same, or knows in general that it is other than it and asks to conceive it in a specific respect in which it is distinguished from it. And there is no doubt that in this case he has not conceived it in a respect distinguished from everything other than it. So ponder. His statement: (And it is necessary in it of a distinguisher) and the apparent wording suggests the necessity of the distinguisher being a part, while permitting definition by a simple term. And if it is assumed that it must be composite, it does not necessarily follow that the equal distinguisher is a part of it; rather, it is permissible for complete distinction to be obtained from the whole, as in the definition of 'the flying, viviparous bat'. So it was said: his mention is based on the more common, and it was said: the intended meaning is about the affair of the definer. His statement: (If it is essential, it is called a limit), i.e., if the distinguisher is only essential, then the composite of all essential and accidental attributes is included in his statement, otherwise it is called a sketch, as they have stated that it is a complete sketch, but it is more complete than the complete limit. His statement: (Or the general accident according to those who permit its inclusion in the limit) is a composite of the proximate differentia and the general accident, it is an incomplete sketch according to what is understood from the words of al-Maṭāliʿ and an incomplete limit according to what the commentator mentioned here, which is consistent with what al-Rāzī stated in Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ, where he invalidated the words of its author by stating that the differentia alone, if it benefits...
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of definitions in logic. It begins by restating the second objective: the definer must be known. The author then provides a systematic division. Definitions are either a limit (ḥadd) when the distinguishing mark (mumayyiz) is essential (dhātī), or a sketch (rasm) otherwise. Both can be complete (tāmm) or incomplete (nāqiṣ). A complete limit consists of the proximate genus and the differentia; a complete sketch consists of the property (khāṣṣa) and the proximate genus. An incomplete limit may be the differentia alone, or with the remote genus, or with the general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm) if that is allowed in a limit. An incomplete sketch may be the property alone or with the genus. The author then discusses the condition that the distinguisher must be equal to the defined, clarifying that it can be essentially different or different by consideration. He introduces a special case: if both essential and accidental distinguishers are combined, the result is a sketch more complete (rasm akmal) than a limit, which falls outside the binary classification. The passage then discusses a problem: a definition depends on a prior conception of the thing in a way that distinguishes it from everything else, which creates a circularity. The example of the triangle and circle is revisited: for someone who knows that a circle is not polygonal but does not know whether the triangle is the circle or not, defining it as a polygonal shape does not fully distinguish it unless additional knowledge is present. Finally, the author explains that the need for a distinguisher does not always imply it must be a part; sometimes the whole composite distinguishes, as in the definition of a bat as 'flying and viviparous'. The passage ends by discussing the limit/sketch distinction further, citing al-Rāzī.

Key Terms
الحد (ḥadd)
الرسم (rasm)
المميز (mumayyiz)
الذاتي (dhātī)
التام (tāmm)
الناقص (nāqiṣ)
الجنس القريب (al-jins al-qarīb)
الفصل (faṣl)
الخاصة (khāṣṣa)
العرض العام (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a limit (ḥadd) from a sketch (rasm)?
A) The limit is complete.
B) The distinguisher is essential.
C) The limit includes the proximate genus.
D) The sketch is always incomplete.

Key Takeaway
The first step in understanding definitions is to distinguish between essential (limit) and accidental (sketch) distinguishers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a term you use often in your faith, like 'tawakkul' or 'sabr.' According to this lesson, is your understanding of it a limit (essential) or a sketch (accidental)? Is it complete or incomplete? What does that reveal about the depth of your knowledge, and what step can you take today to move closer to a complete limit?
?
The lesson warns that an incomplete definition may rely on a single differentia or property. In your own understanding of Allah's attribute 'Ar-Rahman,' do you define it by an essential quality (e.g., His mercy as part of His essence) or by an accidental one (e.g., 'the one who shows mercy')? How might an incomplete sketch lead you to underestimate His mercy?
?
Consider the phrase 'Islam is the religion of peace.' Is that definition a limit or a sketch? Complete or incomplete? If it is incomplete, what essential component (genus or differentia) is missing? How does accepting an incomplete definition affect your daily practice and your call to others?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
The More Complete Sketch (al-Rasm al-Akmal)
Al-Sanusi discusses the rasm akmal as a third type that combines essential and accidental distinguishers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unique status of the more complete sketch (rasm akmal) as a third type of definition.

Core Idea
A definition combining essential and accidental distinguishers is a rasm akmal, more informative than a limit.

Al-Sanusi introduces an interesting exception to the binary division of definitions. When both essential and accidental distinguishers are combined, the resulting definition is called a more complete sketch (rasm akmal), which is actually superior to a limit. This lesson explores that concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the combination of distinguishers
This passage is short but important. Read it carefully to understand the new concept of rasm akmal.
2
Identify why it is called a sketch and not a limit
The combination of two distinguishers results in a sketch, but it is more complete than a limit. Consider why it cannot be called a limit.
A limit requires a single essential distinguisher.
3
Understand why it is outside the two divisions
The passage explains that the binary division (limit vs sketch) is based on a single distinguisher. The rasm akmal does not fit because it uses two.
4
Reflect on the implications
This shows that the classification of definitions is more nuanced. Why might the author highlight this exception?
It demonstrates logical precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Sixth Observation - The Second Objective: The Definer One Must Know. As a sketch (rasman), and it is necessary in it, i.e., in the definer, (of a distinguisher equal to the definer (if the) distinguisher (is essential, it is called) the definer (a limit; otherwise a sketch. And in both cases, if the entire common essential between it and others, called the proximate genus, is mentioned, then it is complete), either a complete limit composed of the proximate genus and differentia, or a complete sketch composed of the property (al-khāṣṣa) and the proximate genus. His statement: (And it is necessary in it of a distinguisher equal [musāw], etc.) either being other than it in essence, as in definition by a composite, or by consideration, as in definition by a simple term. So it, inasmuch as it is a definer, is a container for it inasmuch as it is an equal distinguisher. And it may be said: the speech is with the deletion of the genitive, i.e., in the attainment of the definer or its affair. It [the definition] depends on its conception in a respect distinguished from everything other than it, and the reconciliation between his two statements is problematic, because the definition is of the type of request, so it follows that the requested conceptual thing must be distinguished before definition from everything other than it. For someone who does not know that the triangle is among polygonal shapes, how is it said that it is a conception of the triangle in a respect distinguished from everything other than it? There is no doubt that the definition in the way it is presented only applies to one who knows that the circle is not polygonal, and knows that a shape among shapes is called the triangle and does not know whether it is other than the circle or the same, or knows in general that it is other than it and asks to conceive it in a specific respect in which it is distinguished from it. His statement: (And it is necessary in it of a distinguisher) and the apparent wording suggests the necessity of the distinguisher being a part, while permitting definition by a simple term. And if it is assumed that it must be composite, it does not necessarily follow that the equal distinguisher is a part of it; rather, it is permissible for complete distinction to be obtained from the whole, as in the definition of 'the flying, viviparous bat'. So it was said: his mention is based on the more common, and it was said: the intended meaning is about the affair of the definer. Commentary
This passage presents a classification of definitions in logic. It begins by restating the second objective: the definer must be known. The author then provides a systematic division. Definitions are either a limit (ḥadd) when the distinguishing mark (mumayyiz) is essential (dhātī), or a sketch (rasm) otherwise. Both can be complete (tāmm) or incomplete (nāqiṣ). A complete limit consists of the proximate genus and the differentia; a complete sketch consists of the property (khāṣṣa) and the proximate genus. An incomplete limit may be the differentia alone, or with the remote genus, or with the general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm) if that is allowed in a limit. An incomplete sketch may be the property alone or with the genus. The author then discusses the condition that the distinguisher must be equal to the defined, clarifying that it can be essentially different or different by consideration. He introduces a special case: if both essential and accidental distinguishers are combined, the result is a sketch more complete (rasm akmal) than a limit, which falls outside the binary classification. The passage then discusses a problem: a definition depends on a prior conception of the thing in a way that distinguishes it from everything else, which creates a circularity. The example of the triangle and circle is revisited: for someone who knows that a circle is not polygonal but does not know whether the triangle is the circle or not, defining it as a polygonal shape does not fully distinguish it unless additional knowledge is present. Finally, the author explains that the need for a distinguisher does not always imply it must be a part; sometimes the whole composite distinguishes, as in the definition of a bat as 'flying and viviparous'. The passage ends by discussing the limit/sketch distinction further, citing al-Rāzī.

Key Terms
الرسم الأكمل (al-rasm al-akmal)
المميزان (al-mumayyizān)
الحد (ḥadd)
الرسم (rasm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All definitions are either limit or sketch without exception.
Correction: There is a third type: the more complete sketch (rasm akmal) which combines essential and accidental distinguishers.

Checkpoint
What is the name given to a definition that combines both essential and accidental distinguishers?
A) Complete limit
B) Incomplete limit
C) More complete sketch (rasm akmal)
D) Incomplete sketch

Key Takeaway
A definition that includes both essential and accidental features is a rasm akmal, the most informative type of definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe your own identity or faith, do you rely on a single label (like 'Muslim') or do you combine multiple essential and accidental descriptions? How might a more complete sketch (rasm akmal) deepen your self-understanding and witness to others?
?
This lesson shows that the binary classification of definitions fails to capture the more complete sketch. In your own thinking, are there areas where you settle for oversimplified binaries (good/bad, true/false) instead of seeking a more nuanced, complete picture? What is the cost of that incomplete sketch in your relationships or decisions?
?
Al-Sanusi argues that a definition combining essential and accidental features is more informative. Is there a person or situation in your life right now that you have defined too narrowly? What would change if you added an 'accidental' feature to your understanding? Will you commit to doing that today?

What Is Kalam?

The Problem of Prior Conception in Definition
Al-Sanusi investigates the circularity that a definition requires prior distinguishing knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circularity problem in definitions: a definition presupposes a prior conception of the thing being defined.

Core Idea
A definition cannot fully distinguish unless you already have some knowledge of the thing defined.

Al-Sanusi raises a deep epistemological issue: to define something, you must already have a conception that distinguishes it from everything else. But if you already know it in that way, why define it? This lesson examines this dilemma through the example of the triangle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the dependency of definition
This passage explores the problem that a definition relies on a prior distinguishing conception. Read carefully and note the triangle example.
The passage uses the example of defining a triangle as a polygonal shape.
2
Follow the triangle example
Understand the scenario: someone knows that a circle is not polygonal but does not know if the triangle is the circle or not. The definition as polygonal does not fully distinguish for that person.
This illustrates that definition presupposes some prior knowledge.
3
Consider the discussion on the distinguisher as part or whole
After the triangle example, the author discusses whether the distinguisher must be a part of the definition. He notes that the whole composite can serve as distinguisher, as in the bat example.
4
Reflect on the implications
Take a moment to think about the circularity: if a definition cannot provide entirely new knowledge, what is its purpose?
The author does not resolve the problem here; it remains a philosophical challenge.

The Sixth Observation - The Second Objective: The Definer One Must Know. As a sketch (rasman), and it is necessary in it, i.e., in the definer, (of a distinguisher equal to the definer (if the) distinguisher (is essential, it is called) the definer (a limit; otherwise a sketch. And in both cases, if the entire common essential between it and others, called the proximate genus, is mentioned, then it is complete), either a complete limit composed of the proximate genus and differentia, or a complete sketch composed of the property (al-khāṣṣa) and the proximate genus. (Otherwise it is incomplete), either an incomplete limit, whether by the differentia alone or with the remote genus, or the general accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm) according to those who permit its inclusion in the limit, or an incomplete sketch, whether by the property alone or with the genus. His statement: (And it is necessary in it of a distinguisher equal [musāw], etc.) either being other than it in essence, as in definition by a composite, or by consideration, as in definition by a simple term. So it, inasmuch as it is a definer, is a container for it inasmuch as it is an equal distinguisher. And it may be said: the speech is with the deletion of the genitive, i.e., in the attainment of the definer or its affair. His statement: (If the distinguisher, etc.) And if the two distinguishers are combined, it is called a sketch more complete (akmal) than the limit, and it is outside the two divisions, because the division is [based on] a single distinguisher, and its inclusion in the second division by intending in the first division that the distinguisher is only essential is not correct, because it restricts the second division to the complete sketch composed of the proximate genus and the property, and the incomplete sketch divided into what is by the property alone or by the property and the remote genus or the general accident, and the more complete sketch is not one of them. It [the definition] depends on its conception in a respect distinguished from everything other than it, and the reconciliation between his two statements is problematic, because the definition is of the type of request, so it follows that the requested conceptual thing must be distinguished before definition from everything other than it. For someone who does not know that the triangle is among polygonal shapes, how is it said that it is a conception of the triangle in a respect distinguished from everything other than it? There is no doubt that the definition in the way it is presented only applies to one who knows that the circle is not polygonal, and knows that a shape among shapes is called the triangle and does not know whether it is other than the circle or the same, or knows in general that it is other than it and asks to conceive it in a specific respect in which it is distinguished from it. And there is no doubt that in this case he has not conceived it in a respect distinguished from everything other than it. So ponder. His statement: (And it is necessary in it of a distinguisher) and the apparent wording suggests the necessity of the distinguisher being a part, while permitting definition by a simple term. And if it is assumed that it must be composite, it does not necessarily follow that the equal distinguisher is a part of it; rather, it is permissible for complete distinction to be obtained from the whole, as in the definition of 'the flying, viviparous bat'. So it was said: his mention is based on the more common, and it was said: the intended meaning is about the affair of the definer. His statement: (If it is essential, it is called a limit), i.e., if the distinguisher is only essential, then the composite of all essential and accidental attributes is included in his statement, otherwise it is called a sketch, as they have stated that it is a complete sketch, but it is more complete than the complete limit. His statement: (Or the general accident according to those who permit its inclusion in the limit) is a composite of the proximate differentia and the general accident, it is an incomplete sketch according to what is understood from the words of al-Maṭāliʿ and an incomplete limit according to what the commentator mentioned here, which is consistent with what al-Rāzī stated in Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ, where he invalidated the words of its author by stating that the differentia alone, if it benefits...
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of definitions in logic. It begins by restating the second objective: the definer must be known. The author then provides a systematic division. Definitions are either a limit (ḥadd) when the distinguishing mark (mumayyiz) is essential (dhātī), or a sketch (rasm) otherwise. Both can be complete (tāmm) or incomplete (nāqiṣ). A complete limit consists of the proximate genus and the differentia; a complete sketch consists of the property (khāṣṣa) and the proximate genus. An incomplete limit may be the differentia alone, or with the remote genus, or with the general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm) if that is allowed in a limit. An incomplete sketch may be the property alone or with the genus. The author then discusses the condition that the distinguisher must be equal to the defined, clarifying that it can be essentially different or different by consideration. He introduces a special case: if both essential and accidental distinguishers are combined, the result is a sketch more complete (rasm akmal) than a limit, which falls outside the binary classification. The passage then discusses a problem: a definition depends on a prior conception of the thing in a way that distinguishes it from everything else, which creates a circularity. The example of the triangle and circle is revisited: for someone who knows that a circle is not polygonal but does not know whether the triangle is the circle or not, defining it as a polygonal shape does not fully distinguish it unless additional knowledge is present. Finally, the author explains that the need for a distinguisher does not always imply it must be a part; sometimes the whole composite distinguishes, as in the definition of a bat as 'flying and viviparous'. The passage ends by discussing the limit/sketch distinction further, citing al-Rāzī.

Key Terms
التعريف (al-taʿrīf)
التصور (taṣawwur)
المميز (mumayyiz)
الجزء (juzʾ)
الكل (kull)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A definition can provide complete new knowledge without any prior conception.
Correction: A definition can only clarify and distinguish a concept that is already known in a general way; it presupposes prior knowledge.

Checkpoint
In the triangle example, why does the definition as a polygonal shape fail for someone who does not know that the circle is not polygonal?
A) Because the definition is incomplete.
B) Because the person lacks the prior knowledge that triangles are not circles.
C) Because the definition uses the wrong genus.
D) Because the distinguisher is not essential.

Key Takeaway
A definition cannot provide complete novel knowledge; it always relies on some prior conception of the thing defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attempt to understand a new Islamic concept, do you first check your prior assumptions about it, or do you expect a textbook definition to instantly clarify everything? How has that habit affected your depth of understanding?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Allah's names or attributes. Is it built on a prior conception that you never critically examined? What if that prior conception is incomplete or inaccurate—would you be willing to revisit it?
?
This lesson shows that definitions are not magic; they rely on what you already know. In your own learning, have you ever mistaken a definition for full understanding? What concrete step will you take next time you encounter a new theological term to ensure you build genuine comprehension, not just memorization?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

The 'What' Inquiry Culminates in Tasdiq
Explains how the 'what' inquiry leads to affirmation and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the 'what' inquiry in kalam leads to affirmation (tasdiq) and how it differs from pure conceptual inquiries.

Core Idea
The 'what' inquiry culminates in affirming a posited meaning, not just conceptual understanding.

This lesson uncovers a subtle point in kalam methodology. When scholars ask 'what is X?', they are not simply seeking a definition; their inquiry leads to an affirmation of a posited meaning, which is actually a compound existential question. The commentator clarifies this by defending the base text against al-Dawwani's objection and distinguishing linguistic from scientific methods.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the full translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the two main claims: the culmination in tasdiq and the defense against al-Dawwani.
2
Understand the culmination claim
The commentator says the 'what' inquiry culminates in tasdiq, meaning affirmation of the positing (waḍ‘). Grasp why this makes it part of the compound existential inquiry (هل المركبة).
The example 'what is al-ghadanfar?' shows how asking 'what' brings the meaning to mind after knowing the word.
3
See how the objection is refuted
Al-Dawwani objected that the 'what' inquiry is purely conceptual. The commentator replies that even a verbal definition, as a consequence, suffices to include it in the 'what' inquiry. Then note the distinction between linguistic and scientific methods.
The commentator quotes al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi to clarify the two senses of 'what' inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فمآله إلى التصديق) أي التصديق بالوضع فهو بالحقيقة من مطالب هل المركبة، وإن كان يسأل عنه بما نظرا إلى استلزامه لإحضار المعنى بعد العلم بالوضع، فيقال : ما الغضنفر فاندفع ما قاله المحقق الدواني من أن تعليلهم لتقدم مطلب ما الاسمية على جميع المطالب، بأنه ما لم يفهم معنى اللفظ لم يكن التصديق بوجوده ولا طلب حقيقته ولا التصديق بهليته المركبة، إنما يتم إذا كان التعريف اللفظي داخلا في مطلب ما، فيكون من مطلب ما فيكون من المطالب التصورية لأن إفادته معنى اللفظ بالتبع كاف لدخوله في مطلب ما ولا يتوقف على كونه من مطالبه حقيقة .
قوله : (وهو طريقة أهل اللغة وخارج إلخ ) قال الشارح في حواشي العضدي، وقد أشار بعض المحققين إلى الفرق وأن أحدهما يناسب المباحث اللغوية والآخر العلمية، وكتب في حاشية الحواشي هو المحقق الطوسي، حيث شرح كلام الرئيس قد يطلب بما ماهية ذات الشي. وقد يطلب ماهية مفهوم الاسم المستعمل، إنما لم يقل مفهوم الاسم لأن السؤال بذلك يصير لغوياً، بل هو السائل عن تفصيل ما دل عليه الاسم إجمالاً.
His saying: (its culmination is tasdiq) i.e., affirmation of the positing, so it is in reality from the inquiries of compound هل, even though it is asked about by 'what' due to its entailment of bringing the meaning to mind after knowledge of the positing, so it is said: 'what is al-ghadanfar?'. Thus is repelled what al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani said: that their reasoning for prioritizing the inquiry of 'what is the name' over all inquiries, namely that unless the meaning of the word is understood there is no affirmation of its existence, nor seeking its reality, nor affirmation of its simple هل, is only complete if the verbal definition is included in the 'what' inquiry, so it becomes from the 'what' inquiry and thus from the conceptual inquiries, because its conveying the meaning of the word as a consequence is sufficient for its inclusion in the 'what' inquiry and does not depend on it being among its inquiries in reality. His saying: (it is the method of the linguists and outside etc.) The commentator said in the footnotes of al-‘Adudi: and some verifiers have pointed to the difference, that one of them suits linguistic inquiries and the other scientific inquiries. And he wrote in the margin of the footnotes: he is al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi, where he explained the statement of the Chief: sometimes the whatness of the thing itself is sought, and sometimes the whatness of the meaning of the used name is sought; he only did not say 'the meaning of the name' because asking by that becomes linguistic, rather he is the asker of the detail of what the name indicates in summary.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and defense of a statement from the base text regarding the 'what' inquiry (mā). The commentator first clarifies that when the base text says 'its culmination is tasdiq,' it means affirmation of the positing (al-waḍ‘), so it is actually a compound هل inquiry, but it is still asked as 'what' because understanding the word's meaning is a prerequisite for questioning existence. He then refutes al-Dawwani's objection that the 'what' inquiry is purely conceptual: the verbal definition, though it provides a concept, does so as a consequence, which is sufficient to make it part of the 'what' inquiry. Next, the commentator explains the phrase 'it is the method of the linguists' by quoting a reference from the margins of another work, noting that al-Tusi distinguished between seeking the essence of the thing itself and seeking the meaning of the name — the latter being linguistic, the former scientific.

التصديق
هل المركبة
الوضع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'what' inquiry is purely conceptual and does not involve affirmation.
Correction: The 'what' inquiry culminates in tasdiq (affirmation) of the positing, making it a compound existential question.

Checkpoint

B) It ends in affirmation of the positing
C) It ends in a definition
D) It ends in a linguistic investigation

Key Takeaway
The 'what' inquiry in kalam is not merely conceptual; it leads to an affirmation of a posited meaning, placing it within the compound existential inquiry.

This lesson shows that even a 'what' question can lead to tasdiq — so what would it mean for you to treat your deepest questions as invitations to affirm a reality rather than just to understand?

What Is Kalam?

Types of Definition: Nominal and Real
Sanusi divides real definitions into nominal and real, with subcategories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how real definitions are divided into nominal (according to name) and real (according to reality) and their subcategories of limit and drawing.

Core Idea
Definitions clarify either concepts or realities, each via limits or drawings.

This passage from a logic work classifies definitions. It distinguishes definitions that clarify concepts (nominal) from those that describe existing realities (real). Within each, there are two methods: limit (defining by essential parts) and drawing (defining by accidents). Recognizing this classification helps in analyzing definitions used in arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the opening statement that real definition aims to obtain concepts not yet obtained.
2
Identify the two main types
The passage divides real definition into two types: nominal (تعريف بحسب الاسم) and real (تعريف بحسب الحقيقة). Observe how each is defined.
3
Learn the subcategories
Within each type, there are two subcategories: limit (حد) which gives the essence by parts, and drawing (رسم) which gives accidents. Note the examples of genus.
4
Reflect on the practical use
Think about why a nominal definition is used for concepts not known to exist externally, versus a real definition for existing things.
This classification is foundational for evaluating definitions in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن التعريف الحقيقي الذي يقصد به تحصيل ما ليس بحاصل من التصورات، ينقسم إلى قسمين أحدهما ما يقصد به تصور مفهومات غير معلومة الوجود في الخارج، ويسمى تعريفا بحسب الاسم، فإذا علم مثلاً مفهوم الجنس إجمالاً، وأريد تصوره بوجه أكمل فإن حصل نفس مفهومه بأجزائه كان ذلك حداً له اسمياً، وإن ذكر في تعريف عوارضه كان رسماً له اسمياً، والثاني ما يقصد به تصور حقائق موجودة، ويسمى تعريفاً بحسب الحقيقة إما حداً أو رسماً، وكلا هذين القسمين لا يتجه عليه منع، لأن المتصدي لهما بمنزلة نقاش ينقش لك في ذهنك صورة مفهوم أو موجود، فإنه إذا قال: مثلاً الإنسان حيوان ناطق لم يقصد به أن يحكم على الإنسان بكونه حيواناً ناطقاً، وإلا لكان مصدقاً لا مصوراً أي مفيداً للتصديق لا التصور، بل أراد بذكر الإنسان أن يتوجه ذهنك إلى ما عرفته بوجه ما ثم شرح في تصويره بوجه أكمل، فلا الحد والمحدود حكم حتى يمنع، يصح أن يقال: لا نسلم أن الإنسان حيوان ناطق، فإن ذلك يجري مجرى أن يقال : للكاتب لا نسلم كتابتك، نعم يصح أن يقال : لا نسلم أن هذا حد للإنسان أو أن الحيوان جنس له، أو أن الناطق فصل له إلى غير ذلك، فإن هذه الدعاوي صادرة عنه ضمناً وقابلة للمنع، فإذا أريد دفعه صعب جداً في الحقائق الموجودة وكان خرط القتاد دونه وإن سهل في المفهومات الاعتبارية، وكذا يتجه على الحد النقض والمعارضة فإذا قيل : مثلاً العلم ما
And know that the real definition by which is intended to obtain what is not yet obtained from conceptions is divided into two types: one is that by which is intended to conceive concepts whose existence in the external is not known, and it is called definition according to the name. So if, for example, the concept of genus is known in summary, and one wants to conceive it in a more complete manner, then if its very concept is obtained with its parts, that is a nominal limit (ḥadd ismī), and if in the definition its accidents are mentioned, then it is a nominal drawing (rasm ismī). The second type is that by which is intended to conceive existing realities, and it is called definition according to reality, either as a limit (ḥadd) or a drawing (rasm). And neither of these two types is open to objection (man‘), because the one who undertakes them is like an engraver who portrays for you in your mind the image of a concept or an existent. For when he says, for example, 'man is a rational animal,' he does not intend to judge that man is rational animal — otherwise it would be an affirmation (tasdīq) not a conception (taṣawwur), i.e., providing affirmation not conception. Rather, by mentioning 'man' he intends to direct your mind to what you know in some way, then he explains in portraying it in a more complete manner. So neither the limit nor the limited is a judgment so as to be objected to by saying 'we do not accept that man is a rational animal,' because that is like saying to a writer 'we do not accept that you are writing.' Yes, it is valid to say: 'we do not accept that this is a limit of man' or 'that animal is its genus' or 'that rational is its differentia' etc., because these claims issue from him implicitly and are open to objection. If one wants to repudiate this, it is very difficult in the case of existing realities, and is like 'pulling the qatād branches' (i.e., extremely difficult), though it is easy in conceptual entities. And likewise, the limit is subject to contradiction (naqḍ) and opposition (mu‘āraḍa), so if one says, for example, 'knowledge is...'
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies definitions. It first divides real definition into two types: nominal (according to name) and real (according to reality). Nominal definitions deal with concepts whose external existence is unknown; they can be either a nominal limit (by essential parts) or a nominal drawing (by accidents). Real definitions deal with known existents and can be limit or drawing. The commentator then argues that such definitions are not subject to direct objection like 'we do not accept', because they are not judgments but portrayals. However, one can object to the claim that something is a proper definition (e.g., 'this is not the correct limit'). The passage highlights that defending a real definition for existing things is extremely difficult, unlike for conceptual entities. Finally, it notes that limits are also open to naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa, though these terms apply to logical arguments.

Key Terms
تعريف حقيقي
حد اسمي
رسم اسمي
حد
رسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Definitions always refer to real external things.
Correction: Nominal definitions deal with concepts that may not exist externally.

Checkpoint
What is a nominal limit (حد اسمي)?
A) Definition by accidents
B) Definition by essential parts of a concept
C) Definition of an existing reality
D) Definition by metaphor

Key Takeaway
Real definitions are of two types: nominal and real, each with limit and drawing forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many times have you settled for a vague summary understanding of a core Islamic belief (like 'tawhid means oneness') without pressing into its precise elements? What would it cost you to leave it at that level?
?
The passage distinguishes between knowing a concept in summary and conceiving it more completely. Pick one concept from your aqeedah that you know only summarily. What concrete step will you take this week to move from summary to detailed conception using the categories of limit or drawing?
?
Nominal definitions allow us to reason about concepts independently of whether they exist externally. How much of your current understanding of Allah's names is merely nominal—a mental label—and how much is a real definition that transforms how you relate to Him in worship? What would change if you sought a real definition of just one name?

Definitions as Portrayals, Not Judgments
Sanusi explains that definitions are portrayals, not judgments, and how objections must be framed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why definitions cannot be rejected by denying the predicate, but only by challenging the definitional claim itself.

Core Idea
A definition is a portrayal, not a judgment, so objection is to the definition, not the content.

This passage explains that definitions are like engravings in the mind, not statements of fact. When someone defines 'man' as 'rational animal', they are not asserting a proposition but helping you conceive the meaning. Thus, objections must target the claim that this is the correct definition, not the content itself. This distinction is important for dialectical debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the analogy
The passage compares the definer to an engraver who creates a mental image. Understand that this analogy illustrates that definitions are not judgments.
2
Distinguish judgment from conception
The author distinguishes between tasdiq (affirmation) and tasawwur (conception). Definitions aim at conception, not judgment.
3
Learn the correct way to object
Objection should be directed to the definitional claim itself (e.g., 'this is not the limit of man'), not to the content of the definition.
Example: saying 'we do not accept that man is rational animal' is like telling a writer 'we do not accept that you are writing'.
4
Reflect on the difficulty of real definitions
The passage notes that defending a real definition of an existing thing is extremely difficult, unlike for conceptual entities.
This highlights the challenge in theological arguments where definitions of divine attributes are involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن التعريف الحقيقي الذي يقصد به تحصيل ما ليس بحاصل من التصورات، ينقسم إلى قسمين أحدهما ما يقصد به تصور مفهومات غير معلومة الوجود في الخارج، ويسمى تعريفا بحسب الاسم، فإذا علم مثلاً مفهوم الجنس إجمالاً، وأريد تصوره بوجه أكمل فإن حصل نفس مفهومه بأجزائه كان ذلك حداً له اسمياً، وإن ذكر في تعريف عوارضه كان رسماً له اسمياً، والثاني ما يقصد به تصور حقائق موجودة، ويسمى تعريفاً بحسب الحقيقة إما حداً أو رسماً، وكلا هذين القسمين لا يتجه عليه منع، لأن المتصدي لهما بمنزلة نقاش ينقش لك في ذهنك صورة مفهوم أو موجود، فإنه إذا قال: مثلاً الإنسان حيوان ناطق لم يقصد به أن يحكم على الإنسان بكونه حيواناً ناطقاً، وإلا لكان مصدقاً لا مصوراً أي مفيداً للتصديق لا التصور، بل أراد بذكر الإنسان أن يتوجه ذهنك إلى ما عرفته بوجه ما ثم شرح في تصويره بوجه أكمل، فلا الحد والمحدود حكم حتى يمنع، يصح أن يقال: لا نسلم أن الإنسان حيوان ناطق، فإن ذلك يجري مجرى أن يقال : للكاتب لا نسلم كتابتك، نعم يصح أن يقال : لا نسلم أن هذا حد للإنسان أو أن الحيوان جنس له، أو أن الناطق فصل له إلى غير ذلك، فإن هذه الدعاوي صادرة عنه ضمناً وقابلة للمنع، فإذا أريد دفعه صعب جداً في الحقائق الموجودة وكان خرط القتاد دونه وإن سهل في المفهومات الاعتبارية، وكذا يتجه على الحد النقض والمعارضة فإذا قيل : مثلاً العلم ما
And know that the real definition by which is intended to obtain what is not yet obtained from conceptions is divided into two types: one is that by which is intended to conceive concepts whose existence in the external is not known, and it is called definition according to the name. So if, for example, the concept of genus is known in summary, and one wants to conceive it in a more complete manner, then if its very concept is obtained with its parts, that is a nominal limit (ḥadd ismī), and if in the definition its accidents are mentioned, then it is a nominal drawing (rasm ismī). The second type is that by which is intended to conceive existing realities, and it is called definition according to reality, either as a limit (ḥadd) or a drawing (rasm). And neither of these two types is open to objection (man‘), because the one who undertakes them is like an engraver who portrays for you in your mind the image of a concept or an existent. For when he says, for example, 'man is a rational animal,' he does not intend to judge that man is rational animal — otherwise it would be an affirmation (tasdīq) not a conception (taṣawwur), i.e., providing affirmation not conception. Rather, by mentioning 'man' he intends to direct your mind to what you know in some way, then he explains in portraying it in a more complete manner. So neither the limit nor the limited is a judgment so as to be objected to by saying 'we do not accept that man is a rational animal,' because that is like saying to a writer 'we do not accept that you are writing.' Yes, it is valid to say: 'we do not accept that this is a limit of man' or 'that animal is its genus' or 'that rational is its differentia' etc., because these claims issue from him implicitly and are open to objection. If one wants to repudiate this, it is very difficult in the case of existing realities, and is like 'pulling the qatād branches' (i.e., extremely difficult), though it is easy in conceptual entities. And likewise, the limit is subject to contradiction (naqḍ) and opposition (mu‘āraḍa), so if one says, for example, 'knowledge is...'
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies definitions. It first divides real definition into two types: nominal (according to name) and real (according to reality). Nominal definitions deal with concepts whose external existence is unknown; they can be either a nominal limit (by essential parts) or a nominal drawing (by accidents). Real definitions deal with known existents and can be limit or drawing. The commentator then argues that such definitions are not subject to direct objection like 'we do not accept', because they are not judgments but portrayals. However, one can object to the claim that something is a proper definition (e.g., 'this is not the correct limit'). The passage highlights that defending a real definition for existing things is extremely difficult, unlike for conceptual entities. Finally, it notes that limits are also open to naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa, though these terms apply to logical arguments.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can object to a definition by simply saying 'I don't accept that'.
Correction: Objection must be to the definitional claim, not the content.

Checkpoint
How should one properly object to a definition according to this passage?
A) Say 'we do not accept that man is rational animal'
B) Say 'we do not accept that this is the definition of man'
C) Say 'this definition is false'
D) Say 'I don't understand'

Key Takeaway
Definitions are portrayals, so objection must target the definitional claim, not the predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone’s definition of a concept (like ‘justice’ or ‘faith’) simply because you disagreed with its content, without examining whether the definition itself is correctly structured? How would Sanusi’s insight change that interaction?
?
When you describe your own identity as a Muslim, do you treat that description as a claim to be proven or as a portrayal of what you understand? What would it mean for your conversations with others if you saw it as a portrayal?
?
Think of a definition you hold dear, such as ‘success.’ Could you defend its genus and differentia? If not, is your definition truly serving you, or is it just a comfortable label? What step will you take today to refine it?

Definitions Are Not Judgments: Why They Cannot Be Rejected
Al-Ījī explains that definitions are not judgments and thus immune to rejection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why definitions are considered depictions of concepts rather than judgments, and therefore cannot be rejected as false, but only challenged for correctness.

Core Idea
Definitions are like mental engravings, not rulings to be accepted or rejected.

In classical Islamic logic, a definition is not a proposition that can be true or false. It is a tool for clarifying a concept by describing its essence. This distinction is crucial because it determines how we critique definitions. The author explains that definitions are immune to outright rejection but are subject to challenges about their accuracy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author says definitions are not judgments and thus not subject to rejection.
The passage uses the analogy of an engraver.
2
Understand the analogy of the engraver
The definer is like an engraver painting an image in your mind. When he says 'Man is a rational animal', he is not judging but depicting.
3
Distinguish between rejection and challenge
One can challenge that the given genus or differentia is incorrect, but cannot say the definition is false as a statement.
The passage gives the example of saying to a writer 'I do not accept your writing'.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for logical discussions: it separates the role of definitions from propositions.
This connects to the larger framework of al-Ījī's work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثاني : المعرف تجب معرفته الحقيقة إما حداً أو رسماً، وكلا هذين القسمين لا يتجه عليه منع، لأن المتصدي لهما بمنزلة نقاش ينقش لك في ذهنك صورة مفهوم أو موجود، فإنه إذا قال: مثلاً الإنسان حيوان ناطق لم يقصد به أن يحكم على الإنسان بكونه حيواناً ناطقاً، وإلا لكان مصدقاً لا مصوراً أي مفيداً للتصديق لا التصور، بل أراد بذكر الإنسان أن يتوجه ذهنك إلى ما عرفته بوجه ما ثم شرح في تصويره بوجه أكمل، فلا الحد والمحدود حكم حتى يمنع، يصح أن يقال: لا نسلم أن الإنسان حيوان ناطق، فإن ذلك يجري مجرى أن يقال : للكاتب لا نسلم كتابتك، نعم يصح أن يقال : لا نسلم أن هذا حد للإنسان أو أن الحيوان جنس له، أو أن الناطق فصل له إلى غير ذلك، فإن هذه الدعاوي صادرة عنه ضمناً وقابلة للمنع، فإذا أريد دفعه صعب جداً في الحقائق الموجودة وكان خرط القتاد دونه وإن سهل في المفهومات الاعتبارية، وكذا يتجه على الحد النقض والمعارضة فإذا قيل : مثلاً العلم ما يصح من الموصوف به أحكام الفعل يقال : هذا منقوض بالعلم بالواجبات والمستحيلات، فإن سلم اتحاد وجود العلم المتعلق بهما فقد اعترف ببطلان حده وفساد نقشه وإلا فلا ويقال أيضاً هذا معارض بأنه الاعتقاد المقتضي لسكون النفس، فإن سلم الحد الثاني بطل حده وإلا فلا إذ لا تعاند بين مفهومي هذين الحدين، بل كل منهما مفهوم على حدة، أما إذا قيل : الإنسان حيوان ناطق، وأريد أن هذا مدلوله لغة أو اصطلاحاً كان هذا تعريفاً لفظياً، وحكماً قابلاً للمنع الذي يدفع بمجرد نقل أو وجه استعمال ) ثم إنه يقدم في التعريف الأعم) لكونه أظهر عند العقل فتقديمه أولى ولأن الأخص قيد له مخصص ،إياه فكان تقديمه عليه أنسب وما يقال : من أنه واجب في الحد التام محصل لجزئه الصوري، حتى إذا أخر الجنس فيه كان حداً ناقصاً، فليس بشيء إذ ليس للحد التام جزء خارج عن أجزاء الماهية
The Sixth Observatory - The Second Objective: The Definition Must Be Known. Reality is either a definition (ḥadd) or a description (rasm), and both of these two categories are not subject to rejection (manʿ), because the one who undertakes them is like an engraver who engraves in your mind an image of a concept or an existent. For instance, when he says: 'Man is a rational animal,' he does not intend by it to judge that man is a rational animal—otherwise he would be giving a judgment (muṣaddiq), not a depiction (muṣawwir), i.e., providing affirmation (taṣdīq), not conceptualization (taṣawwur)—rather, by mentioning man he intends that your mind turn to what you already know in some way, and then he explains its depiction in a more complete way. So neither the definition nor the defined is a judgment that can be rejected. It is correct to say: 'We do not accept that man is a rational animal,' for that is analogous to saying to a writer: 'We do not accept your writing.' Yes, it is correct to say: 'We do not accept that this is the definition of man,' or 'that animal is its genus,' or 'that rational is its differentia,' etc., because these claims are implied by him and are subject to rejection. If one wants to defend it, it is very difficult in real existents—and it is harder than stripping the leaves of the qatād tree—while it is easy in conceptual matters. Likewise, objection (naqḍ) and counterexample (muʿāraḍa) can be directed at the definition. For example, if one says: 'Knowledge is that which renders the one described with it capable of action,' it is said: 'This is refuted by knowledge of obligations and impossibilities, for if the unity of the existence of knowledge related to them is conceded, then one has acknowledged the invalidity of its definition and the corruption of its depiction; otherwise, no.' And it is also said: 'This is countered by [the definition that it is] a belief that necessitates tranquility of the soul.' If the second definition is conceded, then the first definition is invalidated; otherwise, no, because there is no incompatibility between the concepts of these two definitions; rather, each is a concept taken separately. As for when one says: 'Man is a rational animal,' and intends that this is its linguistic or conventional sense, this is a verbal definition (taʿrīf lafẓī) and a judgment subject to rejection, which is repelled merely by transmission or manner of usage. Then the more general term is presented before the specific in definitions because it is more apparent to the intellect, so its presentation is preferable, and because the specific is a restriction and specification of it, so its presentation before it is more appropriate. And what is said—that it is necessary in the complete definition (al-ḥadd al-tāmm) to actualize its formal part, so that if the genus is delayed therein, it would be an incomplete definition (ḥadd nāqiṣ)—is nothing, for the complete definition has no part outside the parts of the essence.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about definitions (ḥadd and rasm) in the context of the subject of knowledge. The author argues that definitions are not subject to rejection (manʿ) because they are not judgments (aḥkām) but rather depictions (ṣuwar) of concepts. The person providing a definition is like an engraver painting an image in the mind; when he says 'Man is a rational animal,' he is not judging that man is that, but rather directing your attention to the concept and explaining it more fully. Therefore, one cannot reject the definition as false, just as one cannot say to a writer 'I do not accept your writing' as a rejection of his act of writing. However, one can object that the given definition is not correct: e.g., that animal is not the genus of man, or rational is not the differentia. These claims are implied by the definer and can be challenged. Defending such claims for real existents is very difficult, likened to stripping the leaves of the qatād tree (a proverbial impossible task), while it is easy for conceptual matters. The author then introduces objection (naqd) and counterexample (muʿāraḍa) as tools against definitions. He gives an example: defining knowledge as 'that which renders the one described with it capable of action' is refuted by knowledge of obligations and impossibilities, because if the existence of that knowledge is the same as the knowledge of the possible, then the definition is flawed; otherwise it is not. Another example: defining knowledge as 'belief that necessitates tranquility of the soul' is countered; if accepted, it invalidates the first definition. The key point is that there is no incompatibility between the concepts of the two definitions; they are separate concepts. Then the author distinguishes between real definition (ḥaqīqī) and verbal definition (lafẓī). If one says 'Man is a rational animal' intending the linguistic or technical meaning, it is a verbal definition and a judgment subject to rejection by citation or usage. Finally, he discusses the order of terms in a definition: the more general term should come first because it is more apparent to the intellect and because the specific restricts it. He refutes the claim that delaying the genus makes the definition incomplete, arguing that the complete definition has no part outside the parts of the essence (genus and differentia).

Key Terms
حد
رسم
منع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A definition can be rejected as false, like a proposition.
Correction: A definition is a depiction, not a judgment; it cannot be false, only inaccurate or incorrect in its components.

Checkpoint
Why is it incorrect to say 'We do not accept that man is a rational animal' as a rejection of a definition?
A) Because the definition is a judgment and cannot be rejected.
B) Because the definition is a depiction of a concept, not a judgment, so rejecting it as false is like rejecting a writer's act of writing.
C) Because the term 'man' is ambiguous.
D) Because rational animal is not the correct definition.

Key Takeaway
Definitions are mental depictions, not propositions, so they are immune to rejection but open to challenge on their correctness.

Objection and Counterexample to Definitions
Al-Ījī describes how definitions are subject to objection and counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how definitions can be criticized through objection (naqḍ) and counterexample (muʿāraḍa), and why these challenges do not show the definition to be false but rather inadequate or incorrect.

Core Idea
Definitions can be challenged by showing they fail to cover cases or conflict with other acceptable definitions.

While definitions are not judgments, they can still be evaluated. The author introduces two tools: objection and counterexample. Objection shows that the definition fails to include something it should; counterexample proposes an alternative definition that conflicts. These critiques do not treat the definition as a proposition but test its adequacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on objection and counterexample
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the specific examples given for 'knowledge'.
2
Understand the example of objection
The definition 'knowledge is that which renders the one capable of action' is objected to by noting that knowledge of obligations and impossibilities does not render one capable.
The objection asks: if the same knowledge exists, then the definition is flawed.
3
Understand the counterexample
Another definition 'knowledge is belief that necessitates tranquility of the soul' is presented as a counter. If accepted, it invalidates the first definition.
The key point: both definitions are separate concepts; they are not incompatible, only a choice is made.
4
Reflect on the methodology
These tools are used to refine definitions without rejecting them as false claims.
Notice the careful conditional language: 'if conceded, then... otherwise no.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Sixth Observatory - The Second Objective: The Definition Must Be Known. Reality is either a definition (ḥadd) or a description (rasm), and both of these two categories are not subject to rejection (manʿ), because the one who undertakes them is like an engraver who engraves in your mind an image of a concept or an existent. For instance, when he says: 'Man is a rational animal,' he does not intend by it to judge that man is a rational animal—otherwise he would be giving a judgment (muṣaddiq), not a depiction (muṣawwir), i.e., providing affirmation (taṣdīq), not conceptualization (taṣawwur)—rather, by mentioning man he intends that your mind turn to what you already know in some way, and then he explains its depiction in a more complete way. So neither the definition nor the defined is a judgment that can be rejected. It is correct to say: 'We do not accept that man is a rational animal,' for that is analogous to saying to a writer: 'We do not accept your writing.' Yes, it is correct to say: 'We do not accept that this is the definition of man,' or 'that animal is its genus,' or 'that rational is its differentia,' etc., because these claims are implied by him and are subject to rejection. If one wants to defend it, it is very difficult in real existents—and it is harder than stripping the leaves of the qatād tree—while it is easy in conceptual matters. Likewise, objection (naqḍ) and counterexample (muʿāraḍa) can be directed at the definition. This passage is a discussion about definitions (ḥadd and rasm) in the context of the subject of knowledge. The author argues that definitions are not subject to rejection (manʿ) because they are not judgments (aḥkām) but rather depictions (ṣuwar) of concepts. The person providing a definition is like an engraver painting an image in the mind; when he says 'Man is a rational animal,' he is not judging that man is that, but rather directing your attention to the concept and explaining it more fully. Therefore, one cannot reject the definition as false, just as one cannot say to a writer 'I do not accept your writing' as a rejection of his act of writing. However, one can object that the given definition is not correct: e.g., that animal is not the genus of man, or rational is not the differentia. These claims are implied by the definer and can be challenged. Defending such claims for real existents is very difficult, likened to stripping the leaves of the qatād tree (a proverbial impossible task), while it is easy for conceptual matters. The author then introduces objection (naqd) and counterexample (muʿāraḍa) as tools against definitions. He gives an example: defining knowledge as 'that which renders the one described with it capable of action' is refuted by knowledge of obligations and impossibilities, because if the existence of that knowledge is the same as the knowledge of the possible, then the definition is flawed; otherwise it is not. Another example: defining knowledge as 'belief that necessitates tranquility of the soul' is countered; if accepted, it invalidates the first definition. The key point is that there is no incompatibility between the concepts of the two definitions; they are separate concepts. Then the author distinguishes between real definition (ḥaqīqī) and verbal definition (lafẓī). If one says 'Man is a rational animal' intending the linguistic or technical meaning, it is a verbal definition and a judgment subject to rejection by citation or usage. Finally, he discusses the order of terms in a definition: the more general term should come first because it is more apparent to the intellect and because the specific restricts it. He refutes the claim that delaying the genus makes the definition incomplete, arguing that the complete definition has no part outside the parts of the essence (genus and differentia).

نقض
معارضة
حد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Objection and counterexample show that a definition is false like a proposition.
Correction: They show that the definition is inadequate or that another definition is preferable, not that it is false.

Checkpoint
What is the effect of the counterexample definition of knowledge as 'belief that necessitates tranquility' if accepted?
A) It confirms the first definition.
B) It invalidates the first definition.
C) It shows the first definition is false.
D) It reconciles the two definitions.

Key Takeaway
Objection and counterexample challenge definitions for adequacy, not for truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define your own beliefs (about God, knowledge, truth), do you hold them as absolute and beyond challenge? What would it cost you to admit that your definition might be incomplete or could be improved?
?
The passage shows that definitions can be challenged without being false. Is there a belief you cling to that, if you honestly examined it, you would find it inadequate? What would it look like to refine it instead of defending it?
?
If your understanding of a core aqeedah concept (like the attributes of Allah) were shown to be open to a counterexample or a better definition, would you be willing to reconsider? How would you humble yourself before the truth?

Verbal Definitions and Order of Terms
Al-Ījī distinguishes verbal from real definitions and discusses the order of terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real and verbal definitions, and the reasons for presenting the more general term first in a definition.

Core Idea
A verbal definition reports linguistic usage, while a real definition describes essence; the more general term is placed first for clarity.

The final part of this passage distinguishes between definitions that clarify the meaning of a word (verbal) and those that describe the essence of a thing (real). It also addresses a subtle point about the order of genus and differentia in a complete definition, arguing against the claim that delaying the genus makes it incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on verbal definitions
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that when one says 'Man is a rational animal' as a verbal definition, it is a judgment subject to rejection based on language.
2
Understand the rule for ordering terms
The author states that the more general term should come first because it is more apparent to the intellect and because the specific term restricts it.
3
Examine the refutation of the opposing view
The author dismisses the claim that delaying the genus makes the definition incomplete (ḥadd nāqiṣ), arguing that the complete definition has no part outside the essence.
This is a technical point about the nature of complete definitions.
4
Reflect on the implications for real vs verbal definitions
Understand that real definitions are not judgments, but verbal definitions are judgments about language. The order rule applies to real definitions.

المرصد السادس - المقصد الثاني : المعرف تجب معرفته الحقيقة إما حداً أو رسماً، وكلا هذين القسمين لا يتجه عليه منع، لأن المتصدي لهما بمنزلة نقاش ينقش لك في ذهنك صورة مفهوم أو موجود، فإنه إذا قال: مثلاً الإنسان حيوان ناطق لم يقصد به أن يحكم على الإنسان بكونه حيواناً ناطقاً، وإلا لكان مصدقاً لا مصوراً أي مفيداً للتصديق لا التصور، بل أراد بذكر الإنسان أن يتوجه ذهنك إلى ما عرفته بوجه ما ثم شرح في تصويره بوجه أكمل، فلا الحد والمحدود حكم حتى يمنع، يصح أن يقال: لا نسلم أن الإنسان حيوان ناطق، فإن ذلك يجري مجرى أن يقال : للكاتب لا نسلم كتابتك، نعم يصح أن يقال : لا نسلم أن هذا حد للإنسان أو أن الحيوان جنس له، أو أن الناطق فصل له إلى غير ذلك، فإن هذه الدعاوي صادرة عنه ضمناً وقابلة للمنع، فإذا أريد دفعه صعب جداً في الحقائق الموجودة وكان خرط القتاد دونه وإن سهل في المفهومات الاعتبارية، وكذا يتجه على الحد النقض والمعارضة فإذا قيل : مثلاً العلم ما يصح من الموصوف به أحكام الفعل يقال : هذا منقوض بالعلم بالواجبات والمستحيلات، فإن سلم اتحاد وجود العلم المتعلق بهما فقد اعترف ببطلان حده وفساد نقشه وإلا فلا ويقال أيضاً هذا معارض بأنه الاعتقاد المقتضي لسكون النفس، فإن سلم الحد الثاني بطل حده وإلا فلا إذ لا تعاند بين مفهومي هذين الحدين، بل كل منهما مفهوم على حدة، أما إذا قيل : الإنسان حيوان ناطق، وأريد أن هذا مدلوله لغة أو اصطلاحاً كان هذا تعريفاً لفظياً، وحكماً قابلاً للمنع الذي يدفع بمجرد نقل أو وجه استعمال ) ثم إنه يقدم في التعريف الأعم) لكونه أظهر عند العقل فتقديمه أولى ولأن الأخص قيد له مخصص ،إياه فكان تقديمه عليه أنسب وما يقال : من أنه واجب في الحد التام محصل لجزئه الصوري، حتى إذا أخر الجنس فيه كان حداً ناقصاً، فليس بشيء إذ ليس للحد التام جزء خارج عن أجزاء الماهية
The Sixth Observatory - The Second Objective: The Definition Must Be Known. Reality is either a definition (ḥadd) or a description (rasm), and both of these two categories are not subject to rejection (manʿ), because the one who undertakes them is like an engraver who engraves in your mind an image of a concept or an existent. For instance, when he says: 'Man is a rational animal,' he does not intend by it to judge that man is a rational animal—otherwise he would be giving a judgment (muṣaddiq), not a depiction (muṣawwir), i.e., providing affirmation (taṣdīq), not conceptualization (taṣawwur)—rather, by mentioning man he intends that your mind turn to what you already know in some way, and then he explains its depiction in a more complete way. So neither the definition nor the defined is a judgment that can be rejected. It is correct to say: 'We do not accept that man is a rational animal,' for that is analogous to saying to a writer: 'We do not accept your writing.' Yes, it is correct to say: 'We do not accept that this is the definition of man,' or 'that animal is its genus,' or 'that rational is its differentia,' etc., because these claims are implied by him and are subject to rejection. If one wants to defend it, it is very difficult in real existents—and it is harder than stripping the leaves of the qatād tree—while it is easy in conceptual matters. Likewise, objection (naqḍ) and counterexample (muʿāraḍa) can be directed at the definition. For example, if one says: 'Knowledge is that which renders the one described with it capable of action,' it is said: 'This is refuted by knowledge of obligations and impossibilities, for if the unity of the existence of knowledge related to them is conceded, then one has acknowledged the invalidity of its definition and the corruption of its depiction; otherwise, no.' And it is also said: 'This is countered by [the definition that it is] a belief that necessitates tranquility of the soul.' If the second definition is conceded, then the first definition is invalidated; otherwise, no, because there is no incompatibility between the concepts of these two definitions; rather, each is a concept taken separately. As for when one says: 'Man is a rational animal,' and intends that this is its linguistic or conventional sense, this is a verbal definition (taʿrīf lafẓī) and a judgment subject to rejection, which is repelled merely by transmission or manner of usage. Then the more general term is presented before the specific in definitions because it is more apparent to the intellect, so its presentation is preferable, and because the specific is a restriction and specification of it, so its presentation before it is more appropriate. And what is said—that it is necessary in the complete definition (al-ḥadd al-tāmm) to actualize its formal part, so that if the genus is delayed therein, it would be an incomplete definition (ḥadd nāqiṣ)—is nothing, for the complete definition has no part outside the parts of the essence.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about definitions (ḥadd and rasm) in the context of the subject of knowledge. The author argues that definitions are not subject to rejection (manʿ) because they are not judgments (aḥkām) but rather depictions (ṣuwar) of concepts. However, one can object that the given definition is not correct: e.g., that animal is not the genus of man, or rational is not the differentia. These claims are implied by the definer and can be challenged. Defending such claims for real existents is very difficult, likened to stripping the leaves of the qatād tree (a proverbial impossible task), while it is easy for conceptual matters. The author then introduces objection (naqd) and counterexample (muʿāraḍa) as tools against definitions. He gives an example: defining knowledge as 'that which renders the one described with it capable of action' is refuted by knowledge of obligations and impossibilities, because if the existence of that knowledge is the same as the knowledge of the possible, then the definition is flawed; otherwise it is not. Another example: defining knowledge as 'belief that necessitates tranquility of the soul' is countered; if accepted, it invalidates the first definition. The key point is that there is no incompatibility between the concepts of the two definitions; they are separate concepts. If one says 'Man is a rational animal' intending the linguistic or technical meaning, it is a verbal definition and a judgment subject to rejection by citation or usage. Finally, he discusses the order of terms in a definition: the more general term should come first because it is more apparent to the intellect and because the specific restricts it. He refutes the claim that delaying the genus makes the definition incomplete, arguing that the complete definition has no part outside the parts of the essence (genus and differentia).
