قوله : (بل المنحاز في الخارج بماهية وهوية إلخ ليس المراد أن الموجود في الخارج منحاز بماهية وهوية تنضم إليها في التحقق الخارجي بخلاف الموجود الذهني كما يتبادر من سياق كلامه، إذ لا انضمام في الخارج فإن الهوية متحدة مع الماهية في التحقق الخارجي سيأتي، ولذا لم يصرح بالانضمام فيه بل المراد أن الموجود الخارجي ينحاز في تحققه الخارجي بماهية وهوية ابتداء حصولها في ذلك التحقق بخلاف الجزئيات المرتسمة في الحواس فإن ابتداء حصول هوياتها ليس في هذا التحقق بل كانت متحققة في تحققها الخارجي، فارتسم المجموع في الحس ولك أن تكتفي بالمغايرة الاعتبارية في اعتبار الانضمام الخارجي، فإن قلت : هذا الجواب لا يتم في المخيلات الصرفة كمربع مجنح ،بمربعين فإن شخصيته بحسب الذهن فقط قلت : لا شخصية ولا هوية هناك فإن الشيء إذا لم يرتسم في الخيال من طرق الحواس لا يكون إلا كلياً، وهو ظاهر بالتأمل الصادق، فإن قلت : يصدق على تلك الجزئيات أنها منحازة بماهية وهوية تنضم إليها في تحققها الذهني وهي التشخص الذهني العارض في الذهن، فالسؤال باق قلت : قد سبق أن المراد بالهوية هو التشخص الخارجي سواء حصل في الذهن أيضاً أم لا، وإلا فالماهية الكلية الذهنية أيضاً منحازة بماهية وهوية على أنه قد يدعي أن ذلك التشخص انحاز في الذهن لا على وجه ينضم فيه تشخص إلى ماهيته والفرق ظاهر بالتأمل الصادق، فيصدق عليه أنه منحاز عن غيره بحقيقته، فإن الحقيقة تطلق على ما يتناول الجزئيات أيضاً، وكل ذلك تعسف والأظهر أن يقال : الموجود إما أن يكون وجوده أصلاً، يترتب عليه آثاره ويظهر منه أحكامه فهو الموجود الخارجي والعيني أولا، وهو الموجود الذهني والظلي ) والموجود في الخارج إما أن لا يقبل العدم لذاته وهو قوله : ( والفرق ظاهر بالتأمل (الصادق لا بد من التأمل الصادق حتى لا تلتبس الجزئيات المدركة وإدراكاتها وخلاصة الفرق أن ماله تحقق في الجملة إن انحاز بالحقيقة الجزئية أو الكلية، والتشخص العارض له في هذا التحقق فهو الموجود الخارجي وإن انحاز بالحقيقة فقط أي من غير انضمام التشخص إليه في هذا التحقق فهو الموجود الذهني، سواء كان منحازاً بالحقيقة فقط أو بالحقيقة والهوية معاً لكنه في غير هذا التحقق . قوله (تعسف) لا تعسف فيه إلا تعميم الهوية وتخصيص الانحياز بكونه في ذلك التحقق، وأنت خبير بأنهم يرتكبون لتصحيح المقاصد ما قوله : (أصيلاً) أي ذا أصل وعرق . هو أبعد هذا . من قوله : (يترتب عليه آثاره سواء كان ذلك الترتب في الذهن أو في خارجه فيشمل الكيفيات النفسانية التي تترتب عليها آثارها في الذهن كالعلم والمراد بالآثار الآثار المطلوبة منه أي التي يطلب كل أحد تلك الآثار منه ، والأحكام المعلوم اتصافه بها لكل أحد كالإحراق والاشتعال والطبخ من النار، فلا يرد أن الموجود الذهني أيضا له آثار يترتب عليها وهي المعقولات الثانية لأن المراد عدم ترتب تلك الآثار عليه لا عدم ترتب الآثار المختصة مطلقاً كما يشير إليه الشارح في بحث الوجود الذهني، ولا حاجة إلى التزام أن الآثار الذهنية مشتركة في الموجودات الذهنية، ولا إلى أن المراد كونه فاعلاً لتلك الآثار فإن كل ذلك دعوى لا طريق إلى إثباتها . قوله : ( والظلي) تشبيهاً له بالظل في كونه تابعاً للآخر. الذهني مشترك بين الماهية الشخصية هذه والنوعية الكلية فلا اعتبار له في ذلك الانحياز وإن كان محل نظر. قوله : ( فإن الحقيقة تطلق على ما يتناول الجزئيات) فإن قلت : هذا مسلم لكن الحقيقة التي حكم عليها بانحياز الموجود بها هي الكلية قلت : لا نسلم ذلك إذ لا ضرورة في أصل التقسيم داعية إلى تقييدها بالكلية، بل المراد أن كل ماله تحقق ما منحاز عن غيره لما يصدق عليه الحقيقة مع قطع النظر عن كليتها وجزئيتها فإن انحاز بما يصدق عليه الهوية أيضا فخارجي، وإلا فذهني وأما ما ذكره الشارح أولا من أن الموجود الذهني لا ينحاز عن غيره إلا بحسب الماهية الكلية فهو سوق الكلام على الظاهر المتبادر ، والجواب عدول عن الظاهر وتعميم للحقيقة فتأمل. قوله : ( وكل ذلك تعسف ألا يرى إلى ما ارتكبنا لتصحيحه من التكلفات مع أن الكلام بعد محل تأمل .
His statement: 'Rather, that which is located in external existence with an essence and identity...' It is not meant that the external existent is located with an essence and identity that are subsumed under it in external realization, unlike the mental existent, as would be understood from the context of his speech, because there is no subsumption in external existence, for identity is identical with essence in external realization, as will come. Therefore he did not explicitly mention subsumption in it. Rather, the intended meaning is that the external existent is located in its external realization with an essence and identity whose acquisition begins in that realization, unlike the particulars impressed in the senses, for the beginning of the acquisition of their identities is not in this realization; rather, they were already realized in their external realization, and then the whole was impressed in the sense. And you may suffice with the conceptual difference in considering external subsumption. If you say: This answer does not hold for pure imaginaries, such as a winged square with two squares [or: with two wings?], for its individuality is only according to the mind. I say: There is no individuality nor identity there, for if a thing is not impressed in the imagination through the senses, it is only universal. That is clear by honest reflection. If you say: It is true of those particulars that they are located with an essence and identity subsumed under them in their mental realization, which is the mental individuation accidentally attaching in the mind, so the question remains. I say: It has already been said that the intended meaning of 'identity' is external individuation, whether it also occurs in the mind or not. Otherwise, the universal mental essence is also located with an essence and identity, and it may be claimed that that individuation is located in the mind not in a way that individuation is subsumed under its essence. And the difference is clear by honest reflection. Thus it is verified that it is located from others by its reality, for 'reality' is applied to what includes particulars as well. And all of that is strained. The most apparent is to say: Existence is either such that its existence is original (aṣīl), producing its effects and manifesting its judgments, so it is external and concrete existence, or not, and it is mental and shadowy (ẓillī) existence. And the external existent is that which does not admit non-existence by itself, and it is... his statement: 'And the difference is clear by honest reflection,' one must have honest reflection so that the perceived particulars are not confused with their perceptions. The summary of the difference is: Whatever has some realization, if it is located by the particular or universal reality, and the individuation accidentally attaching to it in this realization, then it is an external existent. If it is located by the reality only, i.e., without the attachment of individuation to it in this realization, then it is a mental existent, whether it is located by the reality only or by the reality and identity together but in other than this realization. His statement: 'Strained' — there is no strain except in generalizing identity and specifying location as being in that realization, and you are aware that they commit to correct objectives what is farther than this. His statement: 'Original (aṣīl)' i.e., having an origin and root. His statement: 'producing its effects' — whether that production occurs in the mind or outside it, thus it includes psychic qualities that produce their effects in the mind, like knowledge. The intended meaning of 'effects' are the effects sought from it, i.e., those effects that everyone seeks from it, and the judgments known to be attributed to it for everyone, like burning, ignition, and cooking from fire. So the objection that mental existence also has effects that follow from it, namely the secondary intelligibles, does not apply, because what is meant is the non-occurrence of those effects upon it, not the absolute non-occurrence of specific effects, as the commentator indicates in the discussion of mental existence. There is no need to commit to the claim that mental effects are shared among mental existents, nor to the claim that the intended meaning is that it is the agent of those effects, for all that is a claim with no way to prove it. His statement: 'shadowy (ẓillī)' likening it to the shadow in being dependent on another. The mental is common between this personal essence and the universal species, so it is not considered in that location, even if it is subject to scrutiny. His statement: 'For indeed reality is applied to what includes particulars' — if you say: This is granted, but the reality by which the existent is judged to be located is the universal reality. I say: We do not grant that, for there is no necessity in the original division that would restrict it to universality. Rather, the intended meaning is that everything that has some realization is located from others by that which is true of it as reality, disregarding its universality or particularity. If it is located by that which is also true of it as identity, then it is external; otherwise, it is mental. As for what the commentator mentioned first, that the mental existent is not located from others except in terms of the universal essence, that is a presentation of the discourse according to the apparent understanding, and the answer is a departure from the apparent and a generalization of reality, so reflect. His statement: 'And all of that is strained'—do you not see what we have committed to correct it in terms of effort, even though the discourse is still subject to scrutiny?
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage contains a series of objections and answers regarding the location of particulars, culminating in a definition of external existence as original (aṣīl) and mental existence as shadowy (ẓillī). The author first clarifies that 'being located' in external existence means that the essence and identity begin in that realization, unlike sensory particulars whose identities precede. He then addresses an objection about pure imaginaries like a winged square, denying they have individuality. Another objection about mental individuation is answered by restricting 'identity' to external individuation. The author then declares this approach strained and offers a preferred definition: existence is either original, producing its effects, or shadowy. He then elaborates on the difference between external and mental existence in terms of location by reality alone or with individuation. He also clarifies that the effects of external existence are the sought-after effects like fire's burning, while mental existence has its own derivative effects. The passage ends with a discussion of the term 'reality' and a final note of strain.

Key Terms
أصيل
ظلي
آثار
موجود خارجي
موجود ذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental existence also produces the same sought-after effects as external existence.
Correction: Mental existence only produces secondary intelligibles, not the sought-after effects like fire burning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes external existence from mental existence?
A) External existence is located in space.
B) External existence is original and produces sought-after effects.
C) External existence is universal.
D) External existence is abstract.

Key Takeaway
External existence is original and produces its sought-after effects, while mental existence is shadowy and derivative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, which 'realities' are you treating as original external existences—producing real effects on your soul and actions—and which are merely mental constructs that you allow to dominate your thoughts? How can you realign your priorities with what is truly original?
?
This lesson teaches that mental existence is like a shadow, dependent on external existence. Consider your worries, fears, and desires—are they based on external realities or mental shadows? What would it mean for your peace of mind to let go of the shadows and focus on the original?
?
The author emphasizes that the effects of external existence are sought after by everyone (e.g., fire's burning). What ultimate, sought-after effects are you living for? Are you content with mere mental satisfactions (intellectual pride, theoretical knowledge) when what is required is original, transforming effects on your character and afterlife?

Distinguishing External and Mental Existence
Al-Iji defines external and mental existence and clarifies the criterion of distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji distinguishes external existence from mental existence based on the presence of individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) in the realization.

Core Idea
External existence includes individuation; mental existence does not.

Al-Iji now presents a foundational distinction in metaphysics: the difference between things that exist in the external world and those that exist only in the mind. His criterion is not about location but about whether individuation is part of the thing's realization. Let's read his precise formulation and see why he rejects a common alternative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Then read the translation. Notice that al-Iji first rejects a certain view as 'far-fetched' before giving his own definition.
The passage includes both the original matn and commentary interwoven.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where al-Iji defines external existence as 'having its existence as an origin, its effects follow' and mental existence as 'otherwise'. Then find the more detailed criterion involving 'reality' and 'individuation'.
The key distinction is whether individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) is joined to the reality in the same realization.
3
Understand the role of 'individuation'
External existence is set apart by both reality (ḥaqīqa) and the individuation that occurs in that realization. Mental existence is set apart by reality alone. Reflect on examples to solidify this.
Individuation is what makes a thing particular and distinct from others.
4
Consider the objection and response
Al-Iji addresses the objection that mental existents also have effects. He clarifies that the relevant effects are those sought from the thing itself, not secondary intelligibles. This strengthens the definition.
This part shows the sophistication of the distinction.

النار، فلا يرد أن الموجود الذهني أيضا له آثار يترتب عليها وهي المعقولات الثانية لأن المراد عدم ترتب تلك الآثار عليه لا عدم ترتب الآثار المختصة مطلقاً كما يشير إليه الشارح في بحث الوجود الذهني، ولا قوله : ( فإن الحقيقة تطلق على ما يتناول الجزئيات) فإن قلت : هذا مسلم لكن الحقيقة التي حكم عليها بانحياز الموجود بها هي الكلية قلت : لا نسلم ذلك إذ لا ضرورة في أصل التقسيم داعية إلى تقييدها بالكلية، بل المراد أن كل ماله تحقق ما منحاز عن غيره لما يصدق عليه الحقيقة مع قطع النظر عن كليتها وجزئيتها فإن انحاز بما يصدق عليه الهوية أيضا فخارجي، وإلا فذهني وأما ما ذكره الشارح أولا من أن الموجود الذهني لا ينحاز عن غيره إلا بحسب الماهية الكلية فهو سوق الكلام على الظاهر المتبادر، والجواب عدول عن الظاهر وتعميم للحقيقة فتأمل. قوله : ( وكل ذلك تعسف ألا يرى إلى ما ارتكبنا لتصحيحه من التكلفات مع أن الكلام بعد محل تأمل .
The second position: On universal matters. It is set apart in the mind not in a way that individuation is joined to its quiddity, and the difference is clear by sincere reflection, so it is true of it that it is set apart from others by its reality, for reality is applied to what encompasses particulars as well, and all of that is far-fetched. The most apparent is to say: Existence either has its existence as an origin, its effects follow from it and its rulings appear from it, so it is external and concrete existence, otherwise it is mental and shadow existence. His saying: (And the difference is clear by sincere reflection) Sincere reflection is necessary so that perceived particulars and their perceptions are not confused. The summary of the difference is that whatever has some realization, if it is set apart by partial or universal reality, and the individuation accidental to it in this realization, then it is external existence. If it is set apart by reality only, i.e., without the addition of individuation to it in this realization, then it is mental existence, whether it is set apart by reality only or by both reality and identity, but not in this realization. His saying (far-fetched): There is no far-fetchedness in it except generalizing identity and specifying being set apart as being in that realization, and you know that they commit to correct the goals what is further than this. His saying: (as an origin) i.e., having a root and lineage. His saying: (its effects follow from it) Whether that following is in the mind or outside it, so it includes psychic qualities whose effects follow from them in the mind, like knowledge. By effects are meant the effects sought from it, i.e., those effects that everyone seeks from it, and the rulings known to be attributed to it by everyone, like burning, igniting, and cooking from fire. So it is not objected that mental existence also has effects that follow from it, which are secondary intelligibles, because what is meant is the non-following of those effects from it, not the absolute non-following of specific effects, as the commentator points out in the discussion of mental existence. There is no need to commit to mental effects being shared among mental existents, nor to the meaning that it is the agent of those effects, for all that is a claim with no way to prove it. His saying: (and shadow) likening it to a shadow in being a follower of the other. His saying: (for reality is applied to what encompasses particulars) If you say: This is accepted, but the reality by which the existent is judged to be set apart is universal. I say: We do not accept that, for there is no necessity in the original division that calls for restricting it to universality. Rather, the meaning is that whatever has some realization is set apart from others by what is true of it as reality, regardless of its universality or particularity. If it is set apart by what is true of it as identity as well, then it is external; otherwise, it is mental. As for what the commentator mentioned first, that mental existence is not set apart from others except by universal quiddity, that is following the apparent understanding. The answer is turning away from the apparent and generalizing reality, so reflect. His saying: (and all that is far-fetched) Do you not see what we undertook to correct it in terms of complications, even though the speech is still subject to reflection.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes external existence (al-wujūd al-khārijī) and mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī). It begins by stating the second position on universal matters. The author explains that an existing thing is set apart (munḥāz) in the mind not by having individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) joined to its quiddity (māhiyya). The difference between external and mental existence becomes clear through sincere reflection: something is truly said to be set apart from others by its reality (ḥaqīqa). However, reality can also apply to particulars. After critiquing a previous view as far-fetched (ta‘assuf), the author gives the more apparent definition: existence that is an origin (aṣīl), with effects following from it and rulings appearing from it, is external (khārijī) and concrete (‘aynī); otherwise it is mental (dhihnī) and shadow (ẓillī). The commentary then elaborates on the criterion: if something has realization and is set apart by its reality (whether partial or universal) along with the individuation that occurs in that realization, it is external; if it is set apart by its reality only without that individuation, it is mental. Further comments address the notion of 'origin' (aṣīl) and that effects can follow in the mind as well. The passage defends against the objection that mental existents also have effects, clarifying that the intended effects are those sought from the thing itself. It also rebuts the claim that the reality used for distinction must be universal, arguing that the division does not require restricting to universality.

الوجود الذهني
الماهية

Misconception: Some might think that mental existence is set apart only by universal quiddity.
Correction: Al-Iji argues that the division does not require restricting to universality; the reality used for distinction can be either universal or particular.

Checkpoint

A) External existence is in the mind, mental existence is outside.
B) External existence is set apart by both reality and individuation, mental existence by reality alone.
C) External existence has no effects, mental existence has effects.
D) External existence is universal, mental existence is particular.

Key Takeaway
External existence is distinguished by the inclusion of individuation in its realization, whereas mental existence lacks that individuation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine a future event or a past memory, you often react emotionally as if it were present. How can the criterion of individuation—that external existence includes a specific 'thisness'—help you realize that such mental images lack that individuation and therefore do not demand the same response as external reality? What would it mean for you to stop treating your mental constructs as if they were external facts?
?
The passage says external existence has effects that are sought from it. Consider a fear you have: is it truly external, with real effects you seek to avoid? Or is it a mental existence, where the fear itself is only in your mind? How does recognizing its mental nature change your relationship to that fear? Will you challenge it today as a mental construct rather than an external threat?
?
You often mistake universal concepts (like 'justice' or 'success') as having the same fixed reality as concrete things. This lesson teaches that mental existence lacks individuation. Is your idea of 'success' an external truth or a mental construct without a specific 'thisness'? How might your life change if you stopped treating it as an external object and instead saw it as a mental category that you can redefine?

Substance and Accident in Islamic Metaphysics
Al-Iji classifies possible existence into substance and accident, with a safeguard clause excluding form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji classifies possible existence into substance and accident, and why form is considered a substance despite inhering in matter.

Core Idea
Not everything that inheres is an accident—form is a substance because matter depends on it.

In this passage from al-Mawaqif, al-Iji lays out the foundational classification of all possible entities into substance and accident. The definitions hinge on whether something exists in a subject (mawḍū‘) or not. However, a subtle case—form (ṣūra)—appears to inhere in matter but is actually a substance. Al-Iji explains why, refining our understanding of the categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read carefully to get the author's own words. Note the structure: definition of accident, then substance, then a safeguard clause.
The passage begins with a continuation from the previous lesson.
2
Distinguish between accident and substance
Accident exists in a subject (locus that sustains it). Substance does not exist in a subject. Identify the two definitions.
The term 'subject' (mawḍū‘) is key.
3
Examine the exception: form
Form exists in matter, but matter is not a subject; rather, matter is constituted by form. So form is a substance despite inhering.
This is a crucial point against simplistic definitions.
4
Reflect on the broader implications
Understanding this classification helps in later discussions about God's attributes (which are accidents) and the nature of the world.
This is a building block for kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(And it), i.e., the possible for itself, (either exists in a subject, i.e., in a locus that sustains that locus (what inheres in it, and that is the accident, or) does not exist in a subject (and that is the substance), whether it does not exist in a locus, or exists in a locus that is not a subject. (So our saying: sustains what inheres in it is a safeguard against form because it exists in a locus, which is matter, but it, i.e., that locus which is matter, is not constitutive of what inheres in it, namely form) (for matter is constituted by form according to them) as you will know, (so form is a substance) despite its being inherent in a locus. (So the locus is more general than matter) because the term locus applies to the subject as well. (And the inherent
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage classifies possible existence (mumkin li-dhātihī) into accident ('araḍ) and substance (jawhar). An accident is that which exists in a subject (mawḍū‘), i.e., a locus (maḥall) that sustains it. A substance exists not in a subject, whether it has no locus at all, or it has a locus that is not a subject. The phrase 'our saying: sustains what inheres in it' excludes form (ṣūra), because form exists in a locus (matter, mādda), but matter is not constitutive of form; rather, matter is constituted by form. Hence form is considered a substance despite inhering in a locus. Thus the term 'locus' is more general than 'matter', applying also to the subject. The passage establishes the technical definitions used in kalam and falsafa.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anything that inheres in something else is an accident.
Correction: Form inheres in matter but is a substance because matter is not a subject; rather, form constitutes matter.

Checkpoint
Why is form considered a substance even though it inheres in matter?

B) Because matter is constituted by form, not vice versa.
C) Because form is universal.
D) Because form is an accident.

Key Takeaway
Possible existence is either a substance or an accident; form is a substance because it does not depend on a subject but rather the subject depends on it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider something you consider a defining trait of yours (patience, anger, generosity). Is it a substance that sustains your actions, or an accident that merely inheres in you based on circumstances? Would removing that trait change your essence? How does that distinction push you to cultivate what is truly substantial in your character for the Hereafter?
?
The passage shows that matter is constituted by form, not the other way around. What is the 'matter' of your daily life—your time, wealth, energy—and what 'form' (purpose, intention) gives it meaning? Are you letting the accidents of the world constitute your core, or are you letting your core substance shape the accidents?

The Necessary and the Possible: Definitions and the 'By Itself' Qualification
Sanusi defines the necessary and possible and explains the 'by itself' qualification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the necessary and possible in Islamic theology, particularly the significance of the phrase 'by itself' (lidhatihi) as a key qualification.

Core Idea
The necessary and possible are defined by accepting non-existence; 'by itself' prevents confusion with external causes.

This lesson introduces two foundational concepts in Islamic theology: the necessary (al-wajib) and the possible (al-mumkin). The passage from Sanusi carefully defines these terms with the crucial qualification 'by itself' to distinguish them from what may be necessary through another cause. Understanding this distinction is essential for later discussions on God's attributes and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses very precise, technical language.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where Sanusi defines the necessary by itself and the possible by itself. Notice how each definition hinges on 'acceptance of non-existence'.
3
Examine the restriction on the necessary
Sanusi says the phrase 'by itself' (lidhatihi) applied to the necessary is a safeguard against the necessary by another. Understand what 'necessary by another' means and why it must be excluded.
4
Examine the restriction on the possible
For the possible, 'by itself' is not a safeguard since there is no possible by another. Instead, it is for conformity and to show possibility is an essential attribute.
The lesson here is that possibility cannot be caused by another without contradiction.
5
Reflect on 'no possible by another'
Sanusi explains that if something were possible by another, it would be necessary or impossible in itself, which is impossible. This establishes that possibility is always an intrinsic property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الثاني : في الأمور العامة الواجب لذاته أو يقبله وهو الممكن لذاته فتقييد الواجب بقوله : لذاته احتراز عن الواجب بغيره وتقييد الممكن بذلك ليس احترازاً عن شيء إذ لا ممكن بالغير، بل هو رعاية للموافقة وإظهار لكون الإمكان مقتضى الذات كالوجوب. قوله: ( لذاته) قيد للنفي لا للمنفي أعني قبول العدم احتراز عن الممكن الموجود، فإن عدم قبوله العدم لغيره أعني العلة. قوله: (أو يقبله أي العدم أو العدم لذاته رعاية للموافقة إذ لا قبول للعدم بمعنى الصلاحية له لغيره وإن كان بمعنى الاتصاف لغيره . قوله: (إذ لا ممكن بالغير أي بسبب الغير وإلا لكان في ذاته واجباً أو ممتنعاً، فيلزم الانقلاب وأما الممكن بالقياس إلى الغير ،فمتحقق كالواجب تعالى فإنه ممكن بالقياس إلى ما سواه إذ لا يقتضي شيء منه وجود الواجب ولا عدمه.
The Second Stand: On Universal Matters. The necessary by itself or that which accepts non-existence, and it is the possible by itself. So restricting the necessary by His saying 'by itself' is a safeguard against the necessary by another, and restricting the possible by that is not a safeguard against anything for there is no possible by another, rather it is for the sake of conformity and to show that possibility is a requirement of the essence like necessity. His saying: ('by itself') is a restriction on the negation not on the negated, meaning acceptance of non-existence, as a safeguard against the existing possible, for its non-acceptance of non-existence is due to another, meaning the cause. His saying: ('or it accepts it') meaning non-existence or non-existence by itself for the sake of conformity, since there is no acceptance of non-existence in the sense of suitability for it due to another, even if it is in the sense of attribution due to another. His saying: ('for there is no possible by another') meaning due to another, otherwise it would be in itself necessary or impossible, leading to conversion. As for the possible in relation to another, it is realized, like the Exalted Necessary, for it is possible in relation to what is other than it, since nothing from it necessitates existence or non-existence of the Necessary.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines the necessary (al-wajib) and the possible (al-mumkin) with their fundamental qualifications. It begins by stating that the necessary is that which by itself does not accept non-existence, and the possible is that which by itself accepts non-existence. The restriction 'by itself' (li-dhatihi) applied to the necessary guards against the necessary by another (al-wajib bi-ghayrihi), which is something that becomes necessary through something else. For the possible, the same restriction is not a guard because there is no possible by another—anything that is possible by another would actually be necessary or impossible in itself, causing a contradiction. Rather, the restriction is for conformity of expression and to show that possibility, like necessity, is a requirement of the essence. The commentary then explains each phrase: 'by itself' restricts the negation of accepting non-existence, not the negated, to exclude the existing possible which temporarily does not accept non-existence due to an external cause. The phrase 'or it accepts it' is explained as referring to non-existence by itself, again for conformity, since acceptance of non-existence in the sense of suitability is not due to another, though attribution may be. Finally, 'no possible by another' is explained: if something were possible by another, it would be necessary or impossible in itself, an impossible conversion; but something can be possible relative to another, like God being possible relative to what is other than Him, since no other thing necessitates His existence or non-existence.

Key Terms
الواجب لذاته
الممكن لذاته
الواجب بغيره
الممكن بالغير
قبول العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is a category of 'possible by another' analogous to 'necessary by another'.
Correction: The passage clearly states there is no possible by another; if something were possible by another, it would be necessary or impossible in itself, which is contradictory.

Checkpoint
Why is the phrase 'by itself' applied to the possible not a safeguard against another category?
A) Because there is no possible by another.
B) Because the possible always exists.
C) Because the possible is equivalent to the necessary.
D) Because the possible does not accept non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The categories of necessary and possible are defined by their essence, not by external causes, and the phrase 'by itself' is crucial to avoid logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that only God is necessary by Himself, what created things do you treat as indispensable? In what ways do you act as if your job, relationships, or health are necessary for your existence, rather than merely possible? How would your heart’s attachment change if you saw them as possible in themselves?
?
The passage says the possible accepts non-existence. Do you live with a genuine awareness that you could cease to exist at any moment? If you fully internalised that you are contingent—kept in existence only by God’s will—what would you do differently today that you have been putting off?
?
You are possible by yourself, necessary only by God’s will. Does your life reflect that your existence is a gift, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? What would it look like to thank God for every breath as if it were your last?

The Definition of Existence: Self-Evidence or Acquired?
Presenting three opinions on whether existence is self-evident or acquired and stating the chosen view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether existence is self-evident or acquired, and why the chosen position is self-evident yet demonstrable.

Core Idea
Existence is self-evident, but its self-evidence can still be proven.

In Islamic theology, the concept of existence is foundational. Scholars debated whether it can be defined or is immediately known. This passage from Sanusi presents three views and argues that existence is self-evident, yet even that can be demonstrated through reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Then read the English translation. Notice the three opinions introduced by 'قيل' and the author's chosen view.
2
Identify the three opinions
The passage lists three views: existence is self-evident, existence is acquired, or neither. Understand each position and how they differ.
Self-evident (بديهي) means immediate knowledge; acquired (كسبي) requires reasoning.
3
Understand the chosen view and its proof
The author selects the first opinion: existence is self-evident. Then he explains that even self-evidence can be proven, because it is a quality external to the concept.
This shows that self-evidence does not preclude demonstration.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some may think self-evident things cannot be argued for. This passage corrects that: the self-evidence itself can be shown through proof.
This is key for understanding how kalam treats foundational concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول : في الوجود والعدم فيه مقاصد ] المقصد الأول : في تعريفه ] يتصور أي تعريف الوجود ( فقيل إنه بديهي) تصوره فلا يجوز حينئذ أن يعرف إلا تعريفاً لفظياً وقيل : هو كسبي فلا بد حينئذ من تعريفه وقيل: لا أصلاً لا بداهة ولا كسباً والمختار أنه بديهي (الوجوه) وهذه الوجوه إما استدلالات كما هو الظاهر منها فإن بداهة التصور صفة خارجة عنه فجاز أن تكون مطلوبة له بالبرهان .
The first observation: on existence and non-existence. It contains aims: the first aim: on its definition. It is conceived, i.e., the definition of existence. So it was said: it is self-evident, so its conception cannot be defined except verbally. And it was said: it is acquired, so it must be defined. And it was said: not at all—neither self-evident nor acquired. And the chosen view is that it is self-evident. The proofs: these proofs are either arguments, as is apparent from them, because the self-evidence of the conception is a quality external to it, so it is possible that it is sought by demonstration.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage classifies the possible stances on the definition of existence. It begins by defining the aim: to investigate whether existence can be defined. It presents three opinions: (1) existence is self-evident (بديهي), so it cannot be defined except verbally—that is, by clarifying the term, not by giving a real definition. (2) existence is acquired (كسبي), meaning it requires definition. (3) existence is neither self-evident nor acquired. The author then states the chosen view: it is self-evident. The passage then provides the proof: the self-evidence of a concept is a quality external to the concept itself, so it can be proven by demonstration. This shows that even though existence is self-evident, we can still provide arguments for its self-evidence. The commentary that follows (though not included here) elaborates on this reasoning. The passage thus establishes the foundational status of the concept of existence in kalam.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is self-evident, it cannot be proven or argued for.
Correction: The passage explains that self-evidence is a quality external to the concept, so it can be demonstrated through proof (برهان). Even self-evident truths can be shown to be self-evident.

Checkpoint

A) It is acquired.
B) It is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The concept of existence is self-evident, but its self-evidence can be demonstrated through rational proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Part-Whole Argument for Self-Evidence of Existence
Sanusi presents the part-whole argument for self-evidence of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of existence is considered self-evident through the part-whole argument and why even self-evident matters may need clarification for some minds.

Core Idea
Existence is self-evident because it is part of every determinate thing, and even children conceive it.

The author begins by affirming that the judgment of self-evidence is itself self-evident, but acknowledges that some minds need assistance. He then gives the first argument: existence is a constituent part of all determinate concepts, and because the part is self-evident, existence is self-evident. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with attention to structure
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the overall flow. Notice the opening statement about self-evidence and the need for warning, then the core argument.
The passage uses parentheses for clarifications; read them as part of the sentence.
2
Identify the key premises
Find the two main premises: 1) Existence is a part of every qualified thing. 2) Even mentally deficient persons conceive of existence. Then see how the conclusion follows.
The argument is: part of a self-evident concept is self-evident; existence is such a part; therefore existence is self-evident.
3
Understand the role of deficient minds
The author uses the example of the mentally deficient and children to show that the concept of existence is innate, not acquired through effort.
This counters the idea that existence is a learned concept.
4
Reflect on the counterfactual
The passage says if existence were acquired, the whole concept would also be non-self-evident. Understand why this strengthens the argument.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if the part needed definition, the whole would too, contradicting our intuition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم
تنبيهات بناء على ما قيل من أن الحكم ببداهة تصوره بديهي أيضاً، لكن قد يحتاج في الأمور البديهية إلى تنبيه بالنسبة إلى الأذهان القاصرة . [ الأول] أنه جزء وجودي ) لأن المطلق جزء من المقيد بالضرورة ) وهو متصور بالبديهة ) لأن من لا يقدر على الكسب حتى البله والصبيان يتصور وجوده قطعاً ) وجزء المتصور بالبديهة بديهي ) لو كان كسبياً محتاجاً إلى تعريف لكان ذلك المتصور أيضاً محتاجاً إلى ذلك التعريف فلا يكون بديهياً ( وعلى التنزل ) أي تنزلنا عن كون وجودي متصوراً بالبديهة وقلنا إن تصوره كسبي ( فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل أي طريق موصل (يلزم من وجوده (وجوده أي من وجود ذلك الدليل وجود المدلول الذي هو تصور وجودي ويكون وجوده أي وجود ذلك الدليل (ضرورياً دفعاً للتسلسل) أو الدور اللازم من كون العلم بوجود كل دليل مستفادا من دليل آخر ) وبه يتم الدليل على بداهة تصور الوجود، فإنه إذا كان وجود ذلك الدليل متصوراً بالبديهة كان الوجود المطلق الذي هو جزء من وجوده بديهياً أيضاً
قوله : ( إلى الأذهان (القاصرة أي التي لا تقدر على تصور أطرافها على ما هو مناط الحكم.
قوله : ( محتاجاً إلى ذلك التعريف باحتياج هو نفس احتياج الجزء في نفس الأمر وإن كان مغايراً له من حيث المفهوم والإضافة إلى الجزء والكل لا باحتياج ذاتي مغاير لاحتياج الجزء حتى يرد المنع بأنا لا نسلم ثبوت احتياج آخر للكل، وتبعي حتى يرد أنه لا يستلزم نظرية الكل لأن النظري ما يحتاج إلى النظر بالذات لا بالتبع.
قوله : فلا بد من الانتهاء (إلخ أي لا بد من اكتسابه بدليل ضروري أو الانتهاء إليه إلا أنه حذف الأول لظهوره اختصاراً .
قوله: (يلزم من وجوده (وجوده أي من العلم بوجوده إذ لو لم يكن موجوداً في الواقع كيف يستلزم وجود المدلول في الواقع؟ أعني كونه متحققاً فيه ولو لم يكن معلوماً وجوده كيف يمكن الاستدلال به هكذا ينبغي أن يحرر هذا المقام ليطابق ما سيأتي في الجواب . قوله : ( ويكون وجوده) أي العلم بوجوده.
قوله : ( ويكون وجوده ضرورياً دفعاً للتسلسل قيل : إن أراد بضرورية وجوده ضرورية البله التصديق به ففيه أن ضرورية التصديق لا تستلزم ضرورية الأطراف فإن ادعى حصوله من الصبيان حتى يلزم ضرورية أطرافه أيضاً، ذلك يمنع مع أن الكلام لا يتم حينئذ بمجرد ضرورية هذا التصديق، وإن أراد ضرورية تصور الوجود فبعيد غير لازم أجيب بأن الكلام للإمام وقد جرى هاهنا على طريقته المعروفة من الاستدلال على بداهة الأطراف ببداهة التصديق، وإن كان مزيفاً فحينئذ يمكن أن يختار كل من الشقين لكن الأول أظهر.
The First Observatory – The First Objective: On the definition of existence and non-existence. Warnings based on what was said that the judgment of the self-evidence of its concept is also self-evident, but self-evident matters may need a warning for deficient minds. [First] It is a existent part (because the absolute is part of the qualified by necessity) and it is conceived self-evidently (because one who cannot acquire, even the mentally deficient and children, definitely conceive of his existence) and the part of what is conceived self-evidently is self-evident (if it were acquired needing definition, then that [whole] would also need that definition and would not be self-evident). And on the concession – i.e., our concession that 'existence' is not conceived self-evidently and we say its conception is acquired – then we must end with a proof, i.e., a means that leads (from whose existence i.e., from the existence of that proof, follows the existence of the thing proved, which is the conception of existence, and its existence i.e., the existence of that proof is necessary to prevent regress) or the circularity necessary from knowledge of the existence of every proof being derived from another proof. And by this the proof of the self-evidence of the conception of existence is completed, because if the existence of that proof is conceived self-evidently, then absolute existence, which is a part of its existence, is also self-evident. His statement: (to deficient minds) i.e., those that cannot conceive their parts as is the basis of judgment. His statement: (needing that definition) with a need that is the same as the need of the part in reality, although it is different from it in concept and relation to part and whole, not with a separate essential need different from the need of the part so that the denial occurs that we do not affirm another need for the whole, and it is subsidiary so that it occurs that it does not entail the theoretical nature of the whole because the theoretical is what needs inquiry essentially not subsidiarily. His statement: so we must end (etc.) i.e., we must acquire it through a necessary proof or end with it, but the first was omitted for brevity due to its obviousness. His statement: (from its existence) i.e., from knowledge of its existence, because if it did not exist in reality, how could the existence of the thing proved in reality follow? I mean it being actual in it, and if its existence were not known, how could one use it as evidence? Thus this position should be formulated to match what will come in the answer. His statement: (and its existence) i.e., knowledge of its existence. His statement: (and its existence is necessary to prevent regress) It was said: If by the necessity of its existence he means the necessity of assent to it, then that entails that the necessity of assent does not entail the necessity of the parts; if he claims that it occurs from children such that the necessity of its parts also follows, that is denied, and besides the speech is not completed then by merely the necessity of this assent. And if he means the necessity of the conception of existence, it is far-fetched and not necessary. It was answered that the speech is for the Imam (al-Razi) and he has followed here his known method of inferring the self-evidence of the parts from the self-evidence of assent, even if it is weak; then one can choose either of the two branches, but the first is more apparent.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument for the self-evidence of the concept of existence. The author begins by stating that the judgment that the concept of existence is self-evident is itself self-evident, but even self-evident matters may require clarification for weaker minds. The first point is that existence is a constituent part of every determinate thing, and the concept of existence is self-evident because even those incapable of rational acquisition, such as the mentally deficient and children, definitely conceive of their own existence. Since the part of a self-evident concept must also be self-evident, existence is self-evident. If it were acquired, it would require a definition, and then the whole concept would also need that definition, making it non-self-evident. Then, conceding that the concept might be acquired, the author argues that we must end with a proof whose existence is necessary to avoid infinite regress. The proof's existence must be self-evident, otherwise we would need another proof ad infinitum. Thus, the absolute existence that is part of that proof's existence is also self-evident. The commentary clarifies the meanings: 'deficient minds' are those unable to conceive the parts correctly. The need of the whole for definition is the same as the need of the part, not an additional essential need. The necessity of ending with a proof is explained as acquiring the concept through a necessary proof or ending with it. The commentator then discusses the requirement that the proof's existence be known, and addresses an objection about the necessity of assent versus the necessity of parts, ultimately choosing the first interpretation as more apparent.

بديهة
جزء
كل
مطلق
مقيد
كسب
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-evident matters require no explanation at all.
Correction: The author shows that even self-evident matters may need clarification for minds that are deficient or unable to grasp the parts.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for existence being self-evident?
A) It is known through logical deduction.
B) It is a part of every determinate thing and even the mentally deficient conceive it.
C) It is defined in the Quran.
D) It is proven by scientific experiments.

Key Takeaway
Existence is self-evident because it is a constituent part of every determinate concept, and even those incapable of rational acquisition conceive it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is self-evident and even a child knows it, why do you sometimes act as if your existence is uncertain? What fears lie behind that?
?
When you reflect that existence is a part of every determinate thing, does that change how you see the world around you? How can you live more in light of this reality today?
?
Sanusi says self-evident matters may need clarification for weaker minds. Are you willing to admit when your own mind is weak and needs clarification? What would it take for you to be humble about your understanding of fundamental truths?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
8 min
The Regress Argument for Self-Evidence of Existence
Sanusi presents a regress argument for the self-evidence of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the regress argument that even if existence were acquired, it must ultimately rest on a self-evident proof, confirming its self-evidence.

Core Idea
If existence were acquired, an infinite regress would result, so we must end with a self-evident proof.

After conceding the possibility that the concept of existence might be acquired, the author presents a second argument based on the impossibility of infinite regress. This lesson traces that argument and its conclusion that absolute existence is self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the concessional argument
Start from 'وعلى التنزل' and read through the passage. Note the concession and then the structure of the proof.
The author is entertaining the idea that existence is not self-evident but acquired.
2
Identify the regress argument
The author argues that if existence is acquired, we need a proof. That proof itself must be known, leading to a regress unless we stop at a self-evident proof.
The proof's existence must be necessary to prevent infinite chain.
3
Understand the conclusion
If the proof's existence is self-evident, then absolute existence (part of that proof) is also self-evident. Thus, existence is self-evident after all.
This shows the original concept is self-evident, even if we assumed otherwise.
4
Examine the objection and response
The passage includes an objection about necessity of assent vs. necessity of conception. Consider how the commentator resolves it by attributing the view to Imam al-Razi.
The objection is not fully settled, but the author favors the first interpretation.

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
Knowledge of Self-Existence is Self-Evident
Al-Jurjani argues that man's knowledge of his own existence is self-evident and defends against the objection that it might be acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of one's own existence is considered a self-evident starting point in kalam reasoning and how it is defended against the objection that it might be acquired.

Core Idea
Our own existence is the clearest self-evident truth that anchors all reasoning.

Al-Jurjani opens with a fundamental epistemological building block. Before any proof can be built, we must find a starting point that does not itself require proof. He argues that our awareness of our own existence is that starting point — it is not acquired through reasoning but is directly known. This passage establishes that principle and defends it against a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the compact style of kalam reasoning.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage states that man's knowledge of his own existence is 'non-acquired' (self-evident). Understand what this means in the context of kalam: it is a premise that does not need proof.
3
Follow the part-whole argument
Al-Jurjani uses a logical relationship: existence (absolute) is a part of one's existence (restricted). Knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole, so since the whole is self-evident, the part is even more so.
This is an argument from priority.
4
Examine the objection and response
An imaginary opponent says knowledge of existence might be acquired. The author gives two responses: 1) it will be refuted later, 2) even if conceded, the regress must end in self-evident premises, so absolute existence remains self-evident.
The second response is the stronger one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإنسان بوجود نفسه غير مكتسب، والوجود جزء من وجوده والعلم بالجزء سابق على العلم بالكل، والسابق على غير المكتسب أولى بأن لا يكون مكتسباً، فإن قيل: علم الإنسان بوجوده مكتسب قلنا سنبطله في باب النفس وبتقدير التسليم لا قدح في المقصود لأنا ما لم نعرف وجود الدليل لا يمكننا أن نستدل به على وجود المدلول وليس العلم بوجود كل دليل محتاجاً إلى دليل آخر، بل لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل يكون العلم بوجوده بديهياً، فكذا العلم بالوجود المطلق
Man, with respect to his own existence, is not acquired. Existence is a part of his existence, and knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole, and what precedes the non-acquired is more deserving of not being acquired. If it is said: Man's knowledge of his existence is acquired, we say: we will refute that in the chapter on the soul. And even with conceding, there is no harm to the intended purpose because as long as we do not know the existence of the evidence, we cannot use it to infer the existence of the indicated. The knowledge of the existence of every evidence does not need another evidence. Rather, it is necessary to end at an evidence whose existence is known self-evidently. Likewise is knowledge of absolute existence.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that knowledge of one's own existence is not acquired but self-evident. It begins by stating that man's knowledge of his own existence is not acquired. The reasoning is: existence (absolute existence) is a part of his restricted existence (the fact that he exists). Knowledge of a part precedes knowledge of the whole. Since the whole (his existence) is non-acquired (self-evident), the part (absolute existence) is even more deserving of being non-acquired. An objection is raised: one might claim that man's knowledge of his existence is acquired. The author responds that this will be refuted in the chapter on the soul. Even if we concede it, the argument still stands because we must end with a self-evident proposition. Without knowing the existence of the evidence, we cannot use it to prove something else. Therefore, knowledge of absolute existence must be self-evident.

Key Terms
غير مكتسب
بديهي
الوجود المطلق
الوجود المقيد
الدليل
المدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If someone claims that knowledge of one's existence is acquired, the entire argument collapses.
Correction: The author shows that even if we grant that knowledge of one's existence is acquired, the argument still stands because the regress of evidence must end in self-evident premises, and absolute existence is that starting point.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is knowledge of absolute existence also non-acquired?
A) Because absolute existence is the same as one's own existence.
B) Because knowledge of the part (absolute existence) precedes knowledge of the whole (one's existence), and the whole is non-acquired.
C) Because the evidence for absolute existence is self-evident.
D) Because the objection that knowledge is acquired is false.

Key Takeaway
One's own existence is a self-evident truth that requires no proof and serves as the foundation for all theological reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted your own existence? How does this lesson's claim that your existence is self-evident confront those doubts? Are you willing to anchor all your theological reasoning on this unshakable truth?
?
If every chain of evidence must end in a self-evident premise, what does that mean for the certainty of your faith? Will you stop seeking proofs for foundational truths and instead start from them?
?
You rely on your own existence every moment, yet rarely ponder it. How might consciously grounding yourself in this self-evident truth change the way you approach questions about Allah, the soul, and the afterlife? What step will you take today to internalize this starting point?

Defining Existence by Two Negative Concepts
Defines 'two negative concepts' and applies them to the definition of the Necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between 'modified' (ma'dulatayn) and 'pure negation' (salibatayn) and why only the former yields a valid definition of God.

Core Idea
A definition by negation must include the subject of negation to be valid.

The author examines whether existence can be defined using purely negative concepts. He introduces the terms 'modified' and 'pure negation' to clarify when such a definition is valid—specifically, only if the negation implicitly references a positive subject. This lesson unpacks that critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author first defines 'two negative concepts' and then applies it to the definition of the Necessary.
2
Identify the two types of negation
Find where the author contrasts 'ma'dulatayn' and 'salibatayn'. Understand the difference: modified negatives imply a subject, pure negatives do not.
This is the key distinction for the validity of the definition.
3
Apply to the example
Consider the example: 'The Necessary is not a body and not a state in a body'. Work through why it works as a definition under the 'modified' interpretation but fails under 'pure negation'.
If the definition is purely negative, it does not positively identify the subject.

His statement: (two negative [concepts]) i.e., two concepts whose quiddity is pure negation without addition to anything. His statement: (is not understood, etc.) because it is the removal of the establishment of something in itself or for another. On this, where he said: 'His response: We do not accept that the reality of my existence is conceived intuitively; indeed, 'I exist' is intuitive assent, etc.' For he made in this statement the conception of my existence opposite to the assent which is 'I exist'. So if his previous statement were interpreted according to the mentioned possibility, the formulation of this response would be corrupted. And some virtuous ones have answered the mentioned problem in another way, saying: Know that the commentator has interpreted this statement of the author as being presented not for acquiring the conception of existence, so he judged that the problem arises. And it is not hidden that the author's intention here is to depict another way for the intuition of the conception of existence, its content being: Even if we concede that the conception of existence is acquired, it must end at an acquirer of necessary existence, so the required is established. Then he wanted to move from the conceptual path to the assertoric path, not in the sense that it acquires a conception of existence thereby, but insofar as it leads to a claim. So he said: 'or we say, etc.' So it is necessary here also a concept of existence intuitively known, so the required is established by this way as well. This is his statement. And you are aware that the context of speech rejects this interpretation: First, because the 'waw' in his statement 'and there is no proof' prevents it for anyone with minimal training in syntactic structure, since the correct way would be to say 'or we say: There is no proof from two negative premises'. Second, because if he intended that, it would suffice for him to say 'There is no doubt about the existence of an intuitive positive proposition'. Third, because this way then becomes an independent proof, so it should be counted as a second proof, and thus the proofs become four, not three, as the author had set. His statement: (Perhaps he intended, just as, etc.) It is said: There is no need for it, for we do not say: If it were acquired, its acquisition would be by a proof, and there is no proof from two negative premises, etc. Rather we say: If it were acquired, then knowledge of its acquiredness would be by a proof composed of two premises, one of which, due to its positivity, includes knowledge of a particular existence by intuition, and there is a discussion there, for we do not concede the implication then; because the acquiredness of something does not entail the acquiredness of the knowledge of its acquiredness; rather the closer [view] then is the intuition of this knowledge, although its acquiredness is possible, as we have established in the discussions of knowledge. His statement likewise: There is no definition by means of two negative concepts. For if you say: It is permissible to say: The Necessary is not a body and not a state in a body. I say: If the two parts of the definition are taken as modified (ma'dulatayn), then the object of the negation enters the definition and thereby the purpose is achieved. Commentary
This passage is a discussion on whether existence can be defined using two negative concepts. It begins by defining 'two negative concepts' as concepts whose quiddity is pure negation without any additional positive element. The commentary then examines an objection about the definition of the Necessary being as 'not a body and not a state in a body'. If these two parts are considered 'modified' (ma'dulatayn) — i.e., they implicitly include the subject of negation — then the definition is valid because the negation attaches to something positive. However, if taken as purely negative statements (salibatayn) without that implicit reference, the definition fails because the definiendum would be a pure negation, which is not a proper definition. The passage also revisits earlier discussions about the self-evidence of the concept of existence and the structure of proofs, reinforcing that knowledge of acquiredness can itself be intuitive.

معدولتين
سالبتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any pair of negative statements can form a valid definition of God.
Correction: The passage shows that the negatives must be 'modified' (ma'dulatayn) — i.e., they must implicitly include a positive subject — to constitute a valid definition.

Checkpoint
Under what condition can a definition using two negative terms be valid?
A) When the negatives are modified (ma'dulatayn) and include the subject of negation.
B) When the negative terms are taken as pure negations.
C) When the definition uses only one negative term.
D) When the definition is about contingent beings.

Key Takeaway
A definition by negation requires that the negatives be 'modified' to include a positive subject, otherwise it fails.

The Self-Evidence of Existence and the Number of Proofs
Analyzes the self-evidence of existence and disputes the number of proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about the self-evidence of existence, including why the number of proofs can be three or four, and how knowledge of acquiredness can be intuitive.

Core Idea
Even if existence is acquired, it must end at a Necessary Being, and the knowledge of acquiredness may be intuitive.

This passage delves into a heated discussion about whether the concept of existence is self-evident or acquired. The author explores an alternative interpretation of an earlier statement, critiques its logical structure, and addresses objections about the number of proofs. Finally, he argues that the knowledge of acquiredness itself can be intuitive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the flow from the initial problem to the alternative interpretation, then the three objections, and finally the response about knowledge of acquiredness.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main argument
The author is trying to show that even if the conception of existence is acquired, it leads to a necessary existent, thus establishing the goal. Then he critiques the way this argument is presented.
3
Understand the three objections
List the three objections raised against the alternative interpretation: (1) linguistic issue with 'waw', (2) sufficiency of a simpler statement, (3) the proof count becoming four instead of three.
These objections are based on the exact wording and logical structure.
4
Grasp the final response
The author argues that the acquiredness of something does not imply the acquiredness of the knowledge of its acquiredness; the latter can be intuitive.
This reinforces the possibility of self-evidence.

No Definition from Two Negatives: Existence is Self-Evident
Commentator argues that definitions cannot consist of two purely negative concepts, establishing existence as self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a definition cannot be composed solely of negative concepts and why existence must be a self-evident positive notion.

Core Idea
Every valid definition must ultimately rest on a positive, self-evident concept.

In this passage, the commentator defends the doctrine that the concept of existence is self-evident. He addresses objections that attempt to define existence through negation, showing that such definitions are circular or impossible. The key principle is that pure negatives cannot serve as foundations for definitions—some positive content is always presupposed. This lesson guides you through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the dense commentary style and the sequence of objections and replies.
The passage contains multiple layers: author's statement, objections, and commentator's responses.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the assertion that existence is self-evident and the principle that a definition cannot be composed of two purely negative concepts.
Look for the phrase 'لا تعريف عن مفهومين سلبيين'.
3
Trace the argument against two negatives
Follow the commentator's reasoning: if we take two negatives as parts of a definition, the definiendum is still a negation relative to a positive, so a positive concept is required.
The example given: 'The Necessary is neither corporeal nor inhering'.
4
Understand the three objections to the alternative interpretation
The commentator refutes a reinterpretation of the author's intent with three points. Note each point and why it undermines the reinterpretation.
The three points are marked by 'أما أولاً', 'أما ثانياً', 'أما ثالثاً'.

قوله : (لا يعقل إلخ ) لأنه رفع لثبوت شيء في نفسه أو لغيره .
على هذا حيث قال: وجوابه أنا لا نسلم أن وجودي حقيقته متصورة بالبديهة، نعم أنا موجود تصديق بديهي إلخ، فإنه جعل في هذا الكلام تصور وجودي مقابلا للتصديق الذي هو أنا ،موجود فلو حمل كلامه السابق على الاحتمال المذكور لاختل تقرير الجواب هذا، وقد أجاب بعض الأفاضل عن الإشكال المذكور بوجه آخر حيث قال : واعلم أن الشارح قد حمل كلام المصنف هذا على أنه مسوق لا كتساب تصور وجودي فحكم باتجاه الإشكال ولا يخفى أن مراد المصنف هاهنا تصوير طريق آخر لبداهة تصور الوجود وحاصله وإن سلمنا أن تصور الوجود كسبي لكن يجب انتهاؤه إلى كاسب وجود ضروري، فيثبت المطلوب ثم أراد أن ينتقل من طريق الموصل التصوري إلى الموصل التصديقي لا على معنى أنه يكتسب به تصور وجودي، بل من حيث أنه موصل دعوى ما فقال : أو نقول إلخ فالزم هاهنا أيضاً، وجوداً متصوراً بالبداهة فيثبت المطلوب بهذا الطريق أيضاً هذا كلامه وأنت خبير بأن سياق الكلام يأبى عن هذا التوجيه أما أولاً فلان الواو في قوله ولا دليل مانع عنه عند من له أدنى دربة في صناعة التركيب، إذ الوجه أن يقول : أونقول لا دليل عن سالبتين وأما ثانيا فلانه لو قصد ذلك لكفى أن يقول لا شك في وجود قضية بديهية موجبة، وأما ثالثاً فلأن هذا الوجه حينئذ دليل مستقل، فالوجه أن يعد دليلاً ثانياً، وتصير به الوجوه أربعة لا ثلاثة كما قرره المصنف .
قوله: (فلعله أراد كما أنه إلخ) قيل : لا حاجة إليه فإنا لا نقول : لو كان كسبياً لكان اكتسابه بدليل ولا دليل عن سالبتين إلخ، بل نقول : لو كان كسبياً لكان العلم بكسبيته بدليل مركب من مقدمتين إحداهما لإيجابها تشتمل على العلم بوجود خاص بالبداهة وفيه بحث، إذ لا نسلم الملازمة حينئذ فإن كسبية شيء لا تستلزم كسبية العلم بكسبيته، بل الأقرب حينئذ بداهة هذا العلم وإن جاز كسبيته كما حققناه في مباحث العلم.
قوله كذلك لا تعريف عن مفهومين (سلبيين فإن قلت : يجوز أن يقال : الواجب لا متحيز ولا حال في المتحيز قلت : إن اعتبر جزءا التعريف معدولتين يدخل متعلق السلب التعريف وبه يتم المطلوب، وإن أخذا سالبتين فلا شك أن المعرف هو السلب المضاف من حيث
بد في المعرف من مفهوم وجودي إما ضروري أو منته إليه، فيكون العلم بوجوده ضرورياً ، فكذا الوجود المطلق في ضمنه
The First Observatory - The First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-existence. His statement: (that the speech etc.) where he explicitly stated that the conceptualization of my existence is self-evident, so descending from that is the statement that its conceptualization is acquired. And what was said: that 'tasawwur' can be taken in the sense of knowledge absolutely, so it would mean that knowledge of my existence is self-evident, and it would be possible for two meanings like the Imām's speech — that is very far-fetched, because turning away from 'knowledge' when it occurs in the Imam's speech, which is the source of this aspect, to the word 'tasawwur' which otherwise indicates something contrary to the intended meaning, is something no rational person would dare do. His statement: (two negative [concepts]) i.e., two concepts whose quiddity is mere negation without addition to anything. His statement: (it is not intelligible etc.) because it is the removal of the affirmation of something in itself or of something else. On this basis, where he said: 'The answer is that we do not grant that the reality of my existence is conceptualized by self-evidence; rather, that I exist is a self-evident assent, etc.,' for he made in this speech the conceptualization of my existence opposite to the assent which is 'I exist.' So if his earlier speech were interpreted according to the mentioned probability, the formulation of this answer would be spoiled. And some virtuous scholars have answered the mentioned difficulty with another approach, saying: Know that the Commentator interpreted the author's speech as being directed at acquiring a conceptualization of my existence, so he judged that the difficulty arises. And it is not hidden that the author's intent here is to depict another path for the self-evidence of the conceptualization of existence. Its summary: even if we grant that the conceptualization of existence is acquired, it must ultimately terminate in an existence that is necessary, so the required is established. Then he intended to move from the conceptual conveyer to the assenting conveyer, not meaning that an existential conceptualization is acquired thereby, but insofar as it conveys a claim. So he said: 'Or we say, etc.' So then here too, an existence conceptualized by self-evidence is necessary, so the required is established by this path also. This is his speech. And you know that the context of the speech refuses this interpretation. First: because the waw in his statement 'and no proof prevents' is problematic for anyone with the least experience in the art of composition, for the correct expression is 'or we say no proof from two negatives.' Second: because if he intended that, it would suffice to say 'there is no doubt about the existence of a self-evident affirmative proposition.' Third: because this aspect would then be an independent proof, so it should be counted as a second proof, making the aspects four, not three, as the author formulated. His statement: (perhaps he intended just as etc.) It was said: There is no need for it, for we do not say: If it were acquired, its acquisition would be by a proof, and there is no proof from two negatives, etc. Rather we say: If it were acquired, the knowledge of its being acquired would be by a compound proof from two premises, one of which contains knowledge of a specific existence by self-evidence, and there is discussion therein, for we do not grant the implication then, because the acquiredness of a thing does not entail the acquiredness of the knowledge of its acquiredness; rather, the closest then is the self-evidence of this knowledge, even if its acquiredness is possible, as we have verified in the investigations of knowledge. His statement likewise: 'No definition from two negative concepts.' For if you say: It is permissible to say: The Necessary is neither corporeal nor inhering in a corporeal. I say: If the two parts of the definition are considered as modified [affirmative by negation], then the object of the negation enters the definition and the required is complete. If they are taken as two negations, then there is no doubt that the definiendum is the negated thing from the aspect that it is additional, so the positive addition enters. Then it is necessary in the definiendum [there must be] a positive concept, either necessary or terminating to it, so the knowledge of its existence is necessary. Thus the absolute existence within it [is also necessary].
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a commentary and objection-answer sequence regarding the definition of existence. The commentator begins by clarifying that the author (al-Mawaqif) explicitly states that the conceptualization (tasawwur) of one's own existence is self-evident (badīhī). A detractor might argue that 'tasawwur' could be interpreted as 'knowledge' in general, leading to the claim that knowledge of existence is self-evident. The commentator dismisses this as far-fetched, because the Imam (al-Rāzī) used 'ilm in the original text, and changing it to 'tasawwur' would distort the intended meaning. Next, the commentator explains 'salbiyyayn' as two concepts whose quiddity (māhiyya) is pure negation without any positive content. He then discusses the statement that negation is not intelligible except in relation to affirmation. The commentary then addresses an attempt by some scholars to reinterpret the author's intent as merely presenting an alternative path rather than acquiring the concept of existence. The commentator refutes this reinterpretation with three points: (1) the wording 'wa lā dalīl' should be 'aw naqūl lā dalīl'; (2) if that were intended, it would suffice to say 'there is no doubt about a self-evident affirmative proposition'; (3) it would make the argument an independent proof, contradicting the author's classification of three arguments. The commentator then addresses a further objection that the argument about acquiredness is unnecessary, and he explains the proper formulation: if existence were acquired, the knowledge of its acquiredness would require a proof, but that knowledge is itself self-evident. Finally, the passage discusses the principle that a definition cannot be composed of two purely negative concepts. Using the example of 'the Necessary is neither corporeal nor inhering', the commentator shows that if the terms are taken as modified affirmatives (the negation of a positive attribute), the definition works; if taken as pure negations, the definiendum is still the negation considered as a relative concept, which presupposes a positive concept. Therefore, any definition must ultimately rest on a positive, self-evident concept, and absolute existence (al-wujūd al-muṭlaq) is such a concept.

بديهي

سلب
ثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a definition can be composed entirely of negative concepts without involving any positive notion.
Correction: The passage shows that even when the terms are taken as pure negations, the definiendum is still a negation relative to a positive, so a positive concept must be presupposed. Hence, a valid definition cannot consist solely of negatives.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can a definition not be formed from two purely negative concepts?
A) Because negative concepts are unintelligible.
B) Because any definition requires a positive concept either necessarily or as endpoint.
C) Because the author explicitly forbids it.
D) Because negation cannot be added to other negations.

Key Takeaway
No definition can rest solely on negative concepts; a positive, self-evident concept like existence is always required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every valid definition requires a positive concept that is self-evident or terminates to one, what is the self-evident positive concept in your own life that anchors your certainty? Have you ever built your faith on mere negations—what God is not—without grounding it in the undeniable reality of existence itself? Will you commit today to rooting your understanding of God in the self-evident truth that He is, not just what He is not?
?
Do you truly believe that your own existence is self-evident? Or do you sometimes doubt it, slipping into a kind of existential skepticism that undermines your certainty about the Creator? If you cannot even be sure of your own existence, how can you be sure of His? This lesson forces you to confront that your own existence is the first evidence of the Necessary Being. Have you been avoiding this truth?
?
When you think about God, do you find yourself more comfortable describing Him by what He is not (not limited, not bodily) rather than affirming positive attributes? This lesson teaches that such an approach is ultimately incoherent without a positive concept. Are you using negations as a crutch to avoid the awe of affirming His real, positive attributes? What would change in your worship if you instead focused on His positive perfections—His power, His mercy, His self-sufficiency?
