Why Existence Cannot Be Defined by Hadd
Al-Jurjani argues against defining existence by hadd via a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining existence by hadd necessarily leads to circularity or non-existence, making such a definition impossible.

Core Idea
Defining existence by hadd forces a choice between circularity and non-existence.

Al-Jurjani presents a classic dilemma: if we define existence by parts (as in a hadd), those parts must either be existent or non-existent. Both options are impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why existence is indefinable by hadd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic and English together to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the two options presented.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities for the parts of existence: they are either qualified by existence (before the composite) or not. If they are, existence precedes itself; if not, they are non-existent.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the role of the law of non-contradiction
The commentary adds that there is no intermediary between existence and non-existence, so the parts must be one or the other. This seals the argument.
This reinforces the impossibility of hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم
( أو ) يتصف بوجود (قبل) أي قبل الوجود الذي هو المركب ( فيتقدم الشيء ) أي الوجود ) على نفسه أولا تتصف ( تلك الأجزاء ( به ) أي بالوجود فلا شك أنها تتصف بالعدم ( فالوجود محض ما ليس له وجود ) أعني تلك الأجزاء التي لم تتصف بالوجود
قوله : ( فلاشك أنها إلخ ) لعدم الواسطة بين النقيضين .
The First Observatory - The First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-existence. (Or) is qualified by existence (before), i.e., before the existence which is the composite, so the thing (i.e., existence) would precede itself. Or those parts are not qualified by it (i.e., by existence), so there is no doubt that they are qualified by non-existence. So existence is pure that which has no existence — meaning those parts that were not qualified by existence. His saying: 'There is no doubt that they, etc.' due to the absence of an intermediary between the two contradictories.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is an argument against defining existence by a hadd (definition by genus and differentia). It begins by presenting the dilemma: if we define existence by parts, those parts must either be qualified by existence or not. If they are qualified by existence before the composite, then the thing (existence) would precede itself, which is impossible (circularity). If they are not qualified by existence, then they are non-existent, making the definition meaningless. The commentary adds that there is no middle ground between existence and non-existence (no intermediary between contradictories), hence the parts must be non-existent. Thus, existence cannot be defined by hadd because its parts would either require anterior existence or be non-existent, both problematic.

Key Terms
الوجود
العدم
الحد
النقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence can be defined by hadd if we use parts that are not themselves existence, such as 'being' or 'reality'.
Correction: The passage shows that any parts of the definition must themselves be either existent or non-existent; there is no neutral third option. If they are existent, circularity results; if non-existent, the definition fails.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the problem with defining existence by a hadd (definition by genus and differentia)?
A) The parts of existence are unknowable.
B) The parts must either be existent or non-existent, leading to circularity or non-existence.
C) Existence has no parts.
D) Existence is a simple concept that cannot be analyzed.

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be defined by hadd because any definition would require its parts to be either existent (circular) or non-existent (meaningless), both of which are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often try to define your worth by your achievements and attributes. If those parts themselves are either existent (circular) or non-existent (meaningless), what does that say about your self-definition? Will you continue to build your identity on shifting sand, or seek a foundation beyond definition?
?
This passage shows that even the most basic concept—existence—escapes our attempts to pin it down. If you are honest, do you try to 'define' Allah in your mind as if He fits into categories? How does this impossibility humble you, and what does it require of you in your worship?
?
The dilemma forces you to accept that some truths are known without definition. Is there an area of your life where you resist this and insist on logical certainty before submitting? What would it mean to trust what is known through experience and revelation rather than through human categories?

Two Reasons Why Existence Cannot Be Defined by Rasm
Al-Jurjani gives two reasons why existence cannot be defined by rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two logical reasons why defining existence by rasm (description) is impossible, and why the condition of being 'more known' makes rasm inapplicable to existence.

Core Idea
Existence is so fundamental that no description can be more known than it.

Al-Jurjani explains why existence cannot be defined by rasm. He gives two clear reasons: first, rasm does not reveal essence; second, rasm requires a 'more known' term, but existence is the most known concept. This lesson unpacks these reasons and the meaning of a'raf.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: the two reasons are introduced with 'fli wajhayn'.
The passage includes commentary from the author, which clarifies the condition of being 'more known'.
2
Identify the two reasons
Look for the two reasons: first, rasm does not give knowledge of essence; second, rasm must be by something 'more known', but existence is the most known by induction.
The second reason is the focus of the commentary that follows.
3
Understand the condition of a'raf
The commentary explains that 'more known' means prior in knowledge. For rasm, the definer must be more known than the definiendum. Existence is the most general concept, so nothing is more known.
The text's negation of anything 'more known' is either metaphorical or sufficient to show impossibility.
4
Reflect on why rasm fails for existence
Consider that if no concept is more known than existence, then rasm is impossible. This shows the unique status of existence in logical definition.
This connects to broader discussions in Islamic logic about defining primary concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعريفه بالرسم فلوجهين أحدهما أن الرسم لا يفيد معرفة كنه الحقيقة والنزاع فيه لا في وجه يمكن استفادته من الرسم ( الثاني أن الرسم يجب أن يكون بالأعرف ) في شرائط المعرف ) ولا أعرف من الوجود بالاستقراء ) فإنا تتبعنا المفهومات فوجدنا الوجود أعرف من كل ما نحاول تعريفه به وأيضاً فهو أي الوجود ( أعم المفهومات
قوله : (بالأعرف أي بما هو أقدم ،معرفة، وحينئذ يظهر أنه لا يجري هذا الوجه في امتناع التحديد لأن الأجزاء تتقدم معرفتها على معرفة المحدود قطعاً، ومن ومن هذا ظهر أن اشتراط الأعرفية في مطلق المعرف إنما هو بالنظر إلى بعض أفراده .
قوله : ( أعرف إلخ) فنفي الأعرفية في المتن إما كناية عن إثبات الأعرفية كما هو المتفاهم في العرف بناء على أن المساواة قلما تتحقق بين الشيئين فهي كالمعدوم، وإما اكتفاء على ما هو المقصود فإنه إذا لم يكن أعرف منه مفهوم امتنع ) مفهوم امتنع رسمه وإن وجد ما يساويه بناء على أن شرطه الأعرفية .
And as for its definition by rasm (description), it is [impossible] for two reasons: one, that rasm does not give knowledge of the essence of the reality, and the dispute concerning it is not about something that can be derived from rasm. Second, that rasm must be by that which is more known (al-a'raf) in the conditions of the definer, and nothing is more known than existence by induction, for we have examined all concepts and found existence more known than everything by which we attempt to define it. And also, it, i.e., existence, is the most general of concepts (a'amm al-mafhumāt). His saying: 'by that which is more known' i.e., by that which is more known in terms of knowledge. And then it becomes apparent that this aspect does not apply to the impossibility of definition by limit (hadd) because the parts are necessarily prior in knowledge to the knowledge of the defined. And from this it appeared that the condition of being 'more known' in absolute definer is only considered with respect to some of its individuals. His saying: 'more known, etc.' So the negation of being 'more known' in the text is either a metonymy for affirming being 'more known' as is understood in custom, based on the fact that equality rarely occurs between two things, so it is like non-existent; or it is sufficiency for what is intended, because if there is no concept more known than it, its rasm is impossible even if there exists something equal to it, based on the condition of being 'more known'.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents two reasons why defining existence by rasm (definition by accidental properties) is insufficient. The first reason: rasm does not provide knowledge of the true essence of a thing, so it cannot resolve the dispute about the nature of existence. The second reason: rasm must be by something more known (a'raf) than the definiendum, but existence is the most known concept based on induction. The commentary explains that the condition of being 'more known' is generally required for any definer, but it is particularly relevant for rasm because in hadd the parts are naturally more known. The commentary also clarifies that the text's negation of anything 'more known' than existence is either a figurative way of affirming that existence is the most known (since equality among concepts is rare) or is sufficient for the argument: if no concept is more known, then rasm is impossible even if an equal concept exists, because the condition is that the definer must be more known. Thus, both reasons show that rasm cannot define existence.

Key Terms
الرسم
الأعرف
الاستقراء
أعم المفهومات
المعرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that rasm is easier than hadd because it uses accidental properties, but this passage shows that the condition of being 'more known' makes rasm impossible for foundational concepts like existence.
Correction: Rasm is not easier; it requires a definer that is more known. For existence, no such definer exists because existence is the most known concept.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason why definition by rasm is insufficient for existence?
A) Rasm is too long.
B) Rasm must be by something more known, but nothing is more known than existence.
C) Existence cannot be described.
D) Rasm is only for accidents.

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be defined by rasm because it does not reveal essence and because no concept is more known than existence, which the definer in rasm must be.

Existence as the Most General Concept in the Mind
Al-Jurjani refutes objections to existence as the most general concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence is considered the most general concept in terms of mental predication, despite objections based on extra-mental generality.

Core Idea
Existence is the most general concept only in the mind, not in extra-mental reality.

Al-Jurjani confronts an apparent paradox: how can existence be the most general concept if it doesn't apply to non-existents and other concepts seem broader? He refines the notion of generality by distinguishing between types of predication, showing that existence is indeed the most general in the mental realm, which suffices for definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage which presents the objection: existence is not the most general in terms of predication or actuality, and other concepts like possibility are broader.
Notice that the objection challenges both the scope and the knowability of existence.
2
Understand Al-Jurjani's answer
Al-Jurjani distinguishes derivative predication (ḥaml ightiqāqī) from other types. He argues that every concept is existent in the mind, so 'existent' applies to all concepts.
This is the key move: shifting from extra-mental to mental existence.
3
See how the answer repels objections
The answer shows that possibility and thingness, as mental concepts, are more specific than existence, even if they are broader extra-mentally. This refutes the second objection.
The passage also notes that this suffices to establish existence as more known than any definer.
4
Note the final clarification
The final line warns against confusing essential predication (where the general is part of the specific) with accidental predication.
This prevents a common logical error in understanding part-whole relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأيضاً فهو إلخ ) عطف عل قوله بالاستقراء.
قوله: (أعم المفهومات لا يخفى أن الوجود ليس أعم المفهومات حملاً إذ لا يحمل إلا على أفراده ولا تحققاً لعدم تحققه في الأمور العدمية، وأيضاً الإمكان العام لشموله المعدوم أعم منه، والشيئية تساويه والجواب أن المراد أعم المفهومات من حيث الحمل اشتقاقاً، فإن كل مفهوم موجود لكونه حاصلاً في الذهن وليس كل موجود مفهوماً لأن بعض الموجودات الخارجية غير مفهوم لنا بالفعل، وبهذا اندفع الاعتراض الثاني لأن الإمكان والشيئية من حيث حصولهما في الذهن أخص منه وإن كانا من حيث ذاتهما أعم منه أو مساوياً له وبهذا القدر يتم غرضنا وهو كونه أعرف من كل ما نحاول تعريفه ،به لأن التعريف بالشيء إنما يكون بعد حصوله في الذهن، ولا يحتاج إلى إثبات أعرفيته من كل ما سواه سواء كان مفهوما بالفعل أولا
قوله : ( والأعم جزء الأخص) منشؤه عدم الفرق بين حمل الذاتي والعرضي .
His saying: 'And also, it, etc.' is conjoined to his saying 'by induction'. His saying: 'the most general of concepts' — it is not hidden that existence is not the most general of concepts in terms of predication, for it is only predicated of its individuals, nor in terms of actuality, due to its non-existence in non-existential matters. Also, general possibility (al-imkān al-‘āmm) is more general than it because it encompasses the non-existent, and thingness (al-shay’iyya) is equal to it. The answer is that what is meant is the most general of concepts in terms of derivative predication (al-ḥaml ightiqāqan), for every concept is existent because it is present in the mind, but not every existent is a concept because some external existents are not concepts for us actually. And by this the second objection is repelled, because possibility and thingness, in terms of their occurrence in the mind, are more specific than it, even if in their essences they are more general than it or equal to it. And to this extent our purpose is achieved, which is that it is more known than everything by which we attempt to define it, because definition by something only occurs after it is obtained in the mind, and there is no need to prove its being more known than everything else, whether it is actually conceptualized or not. His saying: 'And the more general is a part of the more specific' — its source is the failure to distinguish between predication of the essential (al-dhātī) and the accidental (al-‘araḍī).
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage addresses the claim that existence is the most general concept (a‘amm al-mafhumāt). It presents an objection: existence is not the most general in terms of predication (it is only predicated of its instances) nor in terms of actuality (it is not present in non-existential matters). Also, general possibility (imkān ‘āmm) encompasses non-existent things, so it is more general, and thingness (shay’iyya) is equal to existence. The answer distinguishes between types of generality: what is meant is generality in terms of derivative predication (i.e., ‘existent’ is predicated of all concepts because concepts are mental existents). Although external existents may not be conceptualized, every concept is existent in the mind. This refutes the second objection because possibility and thingness, as mental concepts, are more specific than existence (they are subclasses), even if in their extra-mental essences they are more general or equal. The commentary concludes that this suffices to show existence is more known than any definer, since definition requires mental presence, not extra-mental generality. The final note warns against confusing essential predication (where the general is part of the specific) with accidental predication.

Key Terms
أعم المفهومات
الحمل الاشتقاقي
الإمكان العام
الشيئية
الذاتي
العرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence is the most general concept in all respects, including extra-mental reality.
Correction: The passage clarifies that existence is only the most general concept by derivative predication in the mind; it is not most general in terms of predication of individuals or actuality.

Checkpoint
How is existence considered the most general concept according to the passage?
A) In terms of extra-mental actuality.
B) In terms of predication of individuals.
C) In terms of derivative predication in the mind.
D) In terms of being predicated of non-existents.

Key Takeaway
Existence is the most general concept only in the sense that every concept in the mind is existent, not because it applies to extra-mental non-existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Jurjani says definition only occurs after a thing is in the mind. Think of a concept you are trying to understand about Allah—have you been waiting for a clear mental picture before affirming its reality? How might that be holding you back from true knowledge?
?
The passage ends with a warning about confusing essential and accidental predication. Where in your own reasoning about theological matters might you be making this mistake, and what concrete step will you take to correct it?

The Argument from Emanation: Why the More General is More Known
Al-Jurjani presents a third argument for the generality of knowledge based on emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the more general concept is more known because it has fewer conditions and obstacles to its occurrence in the soul.

Core Idea
The more general is more known because it requires fewer conditions to be realized.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a third argument for the claim that the more general is more known. He bases it on the concept of emanation (fayḍ) from the Supreme Giver, arguing that the human soul is receptive to conceptions and that the emanation of a conception depends on the gathering of conditions and removal of obstacles. Since the more general has fewer such conditions and obstacles, it occurs more frequently in the soul and is therefore more known.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall flow. Note the key terms: al-fayḍ, al-mabda' al-fayyāḍ, al-a'amm, al-akhṣṣ.
The argument is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the premises
The argument has several premises: (1) emanation from the Supreme Giver is general, (2) the human soul is receptive, (3) emanation depends on conditions and obstacles. Identify each in the text.
3
Trace the reasoning to the conclusion
Follow how the premises lead to the conclusion: whatever has fewer conditions and obstacles is closer to emanation; the general has fewer; therefore the general occurs more and is more known.
Notice the comparative logic: less condition → more emanation → more known.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument connects ontology (emanation) with epistemology (knowledge). Ask yourself why the author includes this argument and how it supports his broader point about the more general being more known.
Compare with previous arguments if you have studied them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً (فالفيض من المبدأ الفياض (عام) والنفس الإنسانية قابلة للتصورات، وإذا وجد القابل والفاعل لم يتوقف الفيض إلا على اجتماع الشرائط وارتفاع الموانع فكل ما كان شرائطه وموانعه أقل كان إلى الفيض أقرب (والأعم) لا شك أنه ( أقل شرطاً ومعانداً) من الأخص ( لأن شرط العام ومعانده شرط للخاص، ومعاند له من غير عكس كلي لأن الخاص بحسب خصوصه له شرائط، وموانع لا تعتبر في العام أصلاً فيكون اجتماع شرائطه وارتفاع موانعه أقل بالنسبة إلى الخاص، ( فيكون وقوعه في النفس وارتسامه فيها (أكثر) من وقوع الخاص وارتسامه، فيكون أعرف )
وجوابه ) أي جواب الوجه الثالث (أنا) (نختار أن تعريف الوجود بالحد فنختار أولا أن أجزاءه التي يحد بها ) وجودات قولك : فالجزء مساو للكل في )
قوله : (أنا نختار أن أجزاءه إلخ لا يخفى أن هذا الجواب إنما يتم إذا حمل الترديد المذكور بقوله : إن أجزاءه إما وجودات أو لا على أنه يطلق عليها الوجودات أو لا، إذ حينئذ يمكن أن يقال : إنها متخالفة الماهيات، فلا يلزم مساواة الجزء للكل في الحقيقة، وكذا الجواب الذي ذكره الشارح مبني على حمل الترديد المذكور على أنه يصدق عليها الوجودات أولا، فإنه حينئذ يتجه أن يقال: يجوز أن يكون صدق الوجود عليها صدقاً عرضياً، فلا يلزم المساواة المذكورة، وأما إذا حمل الترديد المذكور على أن حقيقتها إما وجودات أي وجودات مع خصوصیات اعتبرت معه على ما ،مر، فلزوم المساواة المذكورة ظاهر كما بيناه، وحينئذ يتعين الجواب باختيار الشق الثاني وهو أن أجزاءه ما ليست بوجودات كما سيجيء.
And also, the emanation from the Supreme Giver is general, and the human soul is receptive to conceptions. And when the receiver and the agent exist, the emanation does not depend except on the gathering of conditions and the removal of obstacles. So whatever has fewer conditions and obstacles is closer to emanation. And the more general (al-a‘amm) is undoubtedly less in condition and opposition than the more specific, because the condition of the general and its opposite is a condition for the specific and its opposite, but not vice versa entirely, because the specific, according to its specificity, has conditions and obstacles that are not considered in the general at all. So the gathering of its conditions and the removal of its obstacles are less relative to the specific. Therefore its occurrence in the soul and its impression therein are greater than the occurrence and impression of the specific, so it is more known. And its answer, i.e., the answer to the third aspect: 'We choose that the definition of existence by hadd, so we first choose that its parts by which it is defined are existences, you say: then the part is equal to the whole in...' His saying: 'We choose that its parts, etc.' — it is not hidden that this answer is only valid if the aforementioned disjunction in his saying 'its parts are either existences or not' is interpreted as 'they are called existences or not'. For then it can be said that they are diverse in quiddities, so the equality of the part to the whole in reality does not follow. Similarly, the answer that the commentator mentioned is based on interpreting the aforementioned disjunction as 'existence is true of them or not', for then it is directed to say that it is permissible that the truth of existence of them is an accidental truth, so the mentioned equality does not follow. But if the aforementioned disjunction is interpreted as 'their reality is either existences, i.e., existences with particularities considered with them as before', then the mentioned equality is apparent as we explained, and then the answer is determined by choosing the second disjunct, which is that its parts are not existences, as will come.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument for the claim that the more general is more known, followed by a response. The argument: the emanation (fayd) from the Supreme Giver is general, and the human soul receives conceptions. Since existence depends on conditions and obstacles, the more general (al-a‘amm) has fewer conditions and obstacles than the specific, so it occurs more frequently in the soul and is thus more known. The response (jawāb) chooses to define existence by hadd (limit), arguing that the parts used in the definition are themselves existences. However, there is a disjunction: either the parts are existences or not. If they are called existences (but are diverse in quiddities), then the part is not necessarily equal to the whole. If they are truthfully existent, it may be accidental, so equality does not follow. But if the parts are existences with their specific particularities, then equality is apparent, and the correct answer is to choose the second disjunct: the parts are not existences (i.e., they are aspects or negations). This resolves the objection.

Key Terms
الفيض
المبدأ الفياض
الأعم
الأخص
الشرائط
الموانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the argument from emanation, why is the more general more known than the more specific?
A) Because the general has more conditions, so it is more perfect.
B) Because the general has fewer conditions and obstacles, so it occurs more in the soul.
C) Because the specific is harder to imagine.
D) Because emanation only applies to general things.

Key Takeaway
The more general is more known because it requires fewer conditions to be received by the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge of Allah, do you tend to focus on detailed specifics (e.g., intricate rulings or theological nuances) before grasping the general principles that are more accessible? How might this lesson correct your learning priority? Starting today, what one general truth about your Creator will you deliberately meditate on first?
?
The argument says the general is more known because it requires fewer conditions and obstacles. Consider your own path to certain knowledge: are you adding unnecessary conditions (e.g., requiring complicated proofs or specific experiences) that prevent the emanation of clear truths into your soul? What obstacles—pride, haste, or distraction—have you placed between yourself and the simple, overwhelming signs of Allah's existence? Will you remove them today?
?
If the general occurs more in the soul and leaves a stronger impression, why do you so easily ignore the universal signs of Allah in creation (the sky, the earth, your own breath) and instead cling to rare or obscure doubts that are less known? What specific general sign have you seen today but not let impress your heart? How will you train yourself to attend to what is most certain and frequent?

Responding to the Objection: Defining Existence by Hadd
Al-Jurjani responds to the objection that definition by hadd of existence leads to part-whole equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's response to the objection that existence cannot be defined by hadd because its parts would be existences, leading to a part-whole equality problem.

Core Idea
Existence can be defined by hadd if its parts are either diverse in quiddity or only accidentally existent.

Having presented objections against defining existence by hadd, al-Jurjani now gives a response. The objection claimed that if the parts of a definition are existences, then the part would be equal to the whole, making definition impossible. Al-Jurjani responds by interpreting the disjunction: either the parts are called existences (but are diverse in quiddity) or existence is truly predicated of them (but accidentally). In both cases, the part is not equal to the whole. Alternatively, one can choose the second disjunct: that the parts are not existences at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Begin by reading the Arabic and translation of the response. Note the disjunction (tardīd) and the two interpretations.
The response is dense; focus on the logical structure.
2
Understand the objection being answered
The objection (not fully included here) was that if you define existence by hadd, the parts of the definition would themselves be existences, so the part would equal the whole. This response addresses that.
The reference to 'the third aspect' is the objection.
3
Analyze the two interpretations
Al-Jurjani gives two interpretations of the disjunction 'the parts are either existences or not.' (1) If they are called existences but have diverse quiddities, then equality does not follow. (2) If 'existence' is true of them, it could be accidental, so again no equality.
The key is that the parts are not identical in quiddity to the whole existence.
4
Identify the preferred answer
Al-Jurjani concludes that if the disjunction is interpreted as 'their reality is existence with particularities,' then equality would follow, so the correct answer is to choose the second disjunct: the parts are not existences (they are aspects or negations).
This aligns with what will come later in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the parts of a definition are existences, then the definition fails because the part equals the whole (existence).
Correction: The parts may be diverse in quiddity or only accidentally existent, so equality does not necessarily follow. Alternatively, the parts may not be existences at all.

Checkpoint
According to the response, under which interpretation does the part-equal-to-whole problem arise?
A) When the parts are called existences but have diverse quiddities.
B) When existence is accidentally true of the parts.
C) When the parts are taken as existences with their specific particularities.
D) When the parts are not existences.

Key Takeaway
Existence can be defined by hadd if its parts are understood as either diverse quiddities or accidental existents, or by choosing that the parts are not existences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological objection that seems to undermine a core belief, do you immediately assume it is unanswerable, or do you, like al-Jurjani, seek to carefully reinterpret the terms? What does your instinct say about your trust in the scholarly tradition?
?
The passage shows that even abstract concepts like 'existence' require precise definition in theology. Are you willing to invest time in understanding such precision, or do you dismiss it as academic hair-splitting? How might that affect your depth of faith?
?
Al-Jurjani resolves the objection by choosing a specific interpretation. When your own understanding of a doctrinal issue leads to a contradiction, are you humble enough to reconsider your assumptions and accept a nuanced solution, or do you cling to a simplistic view? What is at stake in that choice?

The Principle: The General is More Known
Two proofs that the general is more known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs that the general concept is more known than the specific, and why this epistemic principle matters for defining existence.

Core Idea
The general is more known because it has fewer conditions and occurs more in the mind.

Sanusi begins his discussion of existence and non-existence by establishing a fundamental epistemic principle: the general is more known than the specific. He offers two proofs—one from the part-whole relationship and another from the emanation of concepts into the human soul. This principle will be crucial for later arguments about the definition of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement and first proof
Start with the first proof: the general is a part of the specific; the part is more known than the whole because knowledge of the whole depends on knowledge of the part, not vice versa.
2
Understand the second proof from emanation
The second proof uses emanation: the general has fewer conditions and obstacles, so it is impressed in the soul more frequently, making it more known.
Notice the emphasis on conditions (شرائط) and obstacles (موانع).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Both proofs lead to the same conclusion: the general is more known than the specific. Consider why this is foundational for later arguments about defining existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم
والأعم جزء الأخص والجزء أعرف من الكل لأن العلم بالكل يتوقف على العلم بالجزء من غير عكس وأيضاً فالفيض من المبدأ الفياض عام والنفس الإنسانية قابلة للتصورات، وإذا وجد القابل والفاعل لم يتوقف الفيض إلا على اجتماع الشرائط وارتفاع الموانع فكل ما كان شرائطه وموانعه أقل كان إلى الفيض أقرب والأعم لا شك أنه أقل شرطاً ومعانداً من الأخص لأن شرط العام ومعانده شرط للخاص، ومعاند له من غير عكس كلي لأن الخاص بحسب خصوصه له شرائط، وموانع لا تعتبر في العام أصلاً فيكون اجتماع شرائطه وارتفاع موانعه أقل بالنسبة إلى الخاص، فيكون وقوعه في النفس وارتسامه فيها أكثر من وقوع الخاص وارتسامه، فيكون أعرف. وجوابه أي جواب الوجه الثالث أنا نختار أن تعريف الوجود بالحد فنختار أولا أن أجزاءه التي يحد بها وجودات قولك : فالجزء مساو للكل في الذهن أخص منه وإن كانا من حيث ذاتهما أعم منه أو مساوياً له وبهذا القدر يتم غرضنا وهو كونه أعرف من كل ما نحاول تعريفه به لأن التعريف بالشيء إنما يكون بعد حصوله في الذهن، ولا يحتاج إلى إثبات أعرفيته من كل ما سواه سواء كان مفهوماً بالفعل أولا
قوله : ( والأعم جزء الأخص) منشؤه عدم الفرق بين حمل الذاتي والعرضي .
قوله : ( وأيضاً فالفيض عام إلخ ) عطف على قوله والأعم جزء الأخص لا على قوله وأيضاً الأول لأنه لا بد في هذا الوجه من اعتبار كونه أعم المفهومات والفاء زائدة لمجرد تحسين الكلام . قوله : ( والأعم لاشك إلخ) أي الأعم من حيث عمومه وإن كان منحصراً في الخاص أقل منه شرطاً ومعانداً ضرورة اشتماله على أمر زائد على العام .
قوله : ( أنا نختار أن أجزاءه إلخ لا يخفى أن هذا الجواب إنما يتم إذا حمل الترديد المذكور بقوله : إن أجزاءه إما وجودات أو لا على أنه يطلق عليها الوجودات أو لا، إذ حينئذ يمكن أن يقال : إنها متخالفة الماهيات، فلا يلزم مساواة الجزء للكل في الحقيقة، وكذا الجواب الذي ذكره الشارح مبني على حمل الترديد المذكور على أنه يصدق عليها الوجودات أولا، فإنه حينئذ يتجه أن يقال: يجوز أن يكون صدق الوجود عليها صدقاً عرضياً، فلا يلزم المساواة المذكورة، وأما إذا حمل الترديد المذكور على أن حقيقتها إما وجودات أي وجودات مع خصوصیات اعتبرت معه على ما مر، فلزوم المساواة المذكورة ظاهر كما بيناه، وحينئذ يتعين الجواب باختيار الشق الثاني وهو أن أجزاءه ما ليست بوجودات كما سيجيء.
من العبارة إذا لم يرد معنى التفضيل لم يبق لادعاء جزئيته عما سواه وجه ولا تقريب حينئذ لقوله : والأعم جزء الأخص قلت : الأظهر أن المراد أنه أعم المفهومات التي يحاول تعريفه بها . قوله : ( وأيضاً الفيض عام الظاهر أنه دليل ثان لأعرفية الأعم معطوف على قوله والأعم جزء الأخص والجزء أعرف لا علة ثالثة لأعرفية الوجود وإن كان ظاهر العبارة يقتضيه وحمله الشارح في تحقيق الجواب عليه
تمام الماهية قلنا ممنوع فإن وجود كل شيء عندنا نفس حقيقته وهي أي حقائق الأشياء متخالفة فكذا الوجودات الواقعة أجزاء للوجود متخالفة في أنفسها ومخالفة في الحقيقة للمركب منها وقد سبقت منا الإشارة إلى أن الخلاف في كون الوجود بديهياً أو كسبياً مبني على كونه مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً، وأما على تقدير كونه نفس الحقيقة فالمناسب أن يقال بعضه بديهي وبعضه كسبي أو يقال : كله كسبي إذ ليس كنه شيء من الحقائق الموجودة بديهيا، فالأولى في الجواب : أن يقال أجزاؤه وجودات وليس يلزم من ذلك مساواة الجزء للكل في الماهية لجواز أن يكون صدق الوجود على تلك الأجزاء صدقاً عرضياً ولا استحالة في صدق الكل على أجزائه، كذلك ونختار ثانياً أن أجزاءه ليست وجودات قوله يحصل عند الاجتماع بين تلك الأجزاء أمر آخر قلنا : نعم وذلك الأمر الآخر هو المجموع من حيث هو مجموع وهو عين الوجود وإن كان كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المجموع ليس وجوداً، فيكون التركيب في الوجود نفسه لا في قابله أو فاعله ثم ما ذكرته منتقض بسائر المركبات التي علم تركيبها يقيناً إذ نطرده بعينه في السكنجبين مثلاً فنقول : إن كانت أجزاؤه سكنجبينات ساوى الجزء الكل في الماهية وإن لم تكن سكنجبينات فإن حصل عند الاجتماع أمر زائد عليها مسبب عن اجتماعها عارض لها هو السكنجبين كان التركيب في علل السكنجبين ومعروضاته لا فيه وإن لم يحصل
قوله : ( وقد سبقت منا إلخ) بقوله : وأما إذا كان مشتركاً لفظياً فليس هناك وجود مطلق متصور بديهة أو كسباً.
قوله : ( وأما على تقدير إلخ) عطف على قوله وقد سبقت وليس داخلاً تحت الإشارة، حتى يرد أنه ليس مشاراً إليه فيما سبق . :قوله فالمناسب إلخ) لا ما قاله المصنف من أنه كسبي فإنه غير مناسب على ذلك التقدير، وفيه إشارة إلى صحته بناء على جواز القول : بكون الوجود معنى مشتركاً مع القول بأن وجود كل شيء نفسه، وإلن لم يكن مذهباً لأحد، ومن هذا ظهر وجه قوله : والأولى دون أن يقول : والصواب وإنما كان جواب الشارح أولى لمناسبة القول باشتراك الوجود معنى. قوله : ( ولا استحالة إلخ) بل هو واقع فإن كل صادق على جزئه الذهني صدقاً عرضياً، كالإنسان بالنسبة إلى الحيوان .
قوله : ( فالأولى في الجواب إلخ قد نبهناك على أن لفظ المساواة مانع عن حمل الترديد السابق، على أن أجزاء الوجود إما نفس مفهوم الوجود أو لا حتى يندفع هذا الجواب نعم لو قرر ابتداء بهذا الوجه اندفع هذا الجواب وتعين اختيار أنها ليست بوجودات .
First Observatory - First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. The general is a part of the specific, and the part is more known than the whole, because knowledge of the whole depends on knowledge of the part without the reverse. Also, emanation from the Emanating Principle is general, and the human soul is receptive to concepts; when the receiver and the agent exist, emanation does not depend except on the gathering of conditions and the removal of obstacles. So everything whose conditions and obstacles are fewer is closer to emanation. The general, no doubt, is less in condition and opposition than the specific, because the condition and opposite of the general are a condition and opposite for the specific, without total reversal, because the specific by its specificity has conditions and obstacles not considered in the general at all. So the gathering of its conditions and removal of its obstacles are fewer relative to the specific, so its occurrence in the soul and its impression in it is more than the occurrence of the specific and its impression, so it is more known. Its answer, i.e. the answer to the third aspect: We choose that the definition of existence by a definition, so we first choose that its parts by which it is defined are existences. Your statement: 'So the part is equal to the whole in the mind, more specific than it, although in terms of their essences they are more general than it or equal to it.' And by this measure our purpose is achieved, which is its being more known than everything we try to define by it, because definition of a thing is only after its occurrence in the mind, and there is no need to prove its being more known than everything else, whether it is actually conceptualized or not. His statement: 'And the general is part of the specific' originates from failing to distinguish between essential and accidental predication. His statement: 'And also emanation is general, etc.' is conjoined to his statement 'and the general is part of the specific', not to his first statement 'and also', because in this aspect there must be consideration of its being the most general of concepts, and the 'fa' is superfluous merely for beautifying speech. His statement: 'And the general, no doubt, etc.' means the general in terms of its generality, although it is confined in the specific, is less in condition and opposition due to involving an additional matter over the general. His statement: 'We choose that its parts, etc.' It is not hidden that this answer is only valid if the aforementioned disjunction in his statement 'Indeed its parts are either existences or not' is interpreted as whether they are called existences or not, for then it can be said: They are diverse in quiddities, so equality of part to whole in reality does not necessarily follow. Similarly, the answer mentioned by the commentator is based on interpreting the aforementioned disjunction as whether existence is truly predicated of them or not, for then it is appropriate to say: It is possible that the predication of existence to them is accidental predication, so the mentioned equality does not necessarily follow. But if the aforementioned disjunction is interpreted as their reality being either existences, i.e., existences with particularities considered with it as previously mentioned, then the necessity of the mentioned equality is apparent as we have explained, and then the answer is determined by choosing the second alternative, which is that its parts are not existences, as will come. From the expression if the meaning of preference is not intended, no face remains for claiming its being part of what is other than it, and no approximation then for his statement: 'and the general is part of the specific'. I say: The more apparent is that the intended meaning is that it is the most general of the concepts by which he attempts to define it. His statement: 'And also the emanation is general, it appears that it is a second proof for the greater knowability of the general, conjoined to his statement 'and the general is part of the specific and the part is more known', not a third cause for the greater knowability of existence, although the apparent meaning of the expression requires it, and the commentator's interpretation in the verification of the answer is upon it. 'Completion of the quiddity' we said 'prevented'. For the existence of everything, according to us, is its very essence, and they, i.e., the realities of things, are diverse. Similarly, the existences that are parts of existence are diverse in themselves and different in reality from the composite of them. And we have previously indicated that the dispute over whether existence is intuitive or acquired is based on its being a single shared concept. But on the assumption that it is identical to essence, the appropriate thing is to say that part of it is intuitive and part acquired, or to say: all of it is acquired, since the innermost essence of none of existing realities is intuitive. So the preferable in the answer is to say: Its parts are existences, and equality of part to whole in quiddity does not necessarily follow from that, because it is possible that the predication of existence to those parts is accidental predication, and there is no impossibility in the whole being predicated of its parts in that way. And we choose, secondly, that its parts are not existences. His statement: 'An additional matter occurs upon the combination of those parts.' We said: Yes, and that additional matter is the whole insofar as it is a whole, and it is identical to existence, even if each one of the parts of that whole is not existence. So composition is in existence itself, not in its receiver or agent. Then what you mentioned is refuted by other composites whose composition is known with certainty, for we apply it exactly to sakanjabin, for example. So we say: If its parts are sakanjabins, then the part equals the whole in quiddity; if they are not sakanjabins, then if an additional matter occurs upon their combination, caused by their combination, an accident of them which is sakanjabin, then composition is in the causes and subjects of sakanjabin, not in it; and if it does not occur... His statement: 'And we have previously indicated, etc.' by his statement: 'But if it is a homonym, then there is no absolute existence conceptualized intuitively or acquired.' His statement: 'And on the assumption, etc.' is conjoined to his statement 'and we have previously indicated' and is not included under the indication, such that it could be objected that it is not referred to previously. His statement: 'So the appropriate, etc.' is not what the author said, that it is acquired, for it is not appropriate on that assumption, and there is in it an indication of its correctness based on the permissibility of saying that existence is a shared meaning along with the statement that the existence of each thing is its own essence, otherwise it would not be a doctrine for anyone. From this the justification of his statement 'and the preferable' becomes apparent, rather than saying 'and the correct', and the commentator's answer was preferable due to suitability for the statement that existence is a shared meaning. His statement: 'And there is no impossibility, etc.' Rather it does occur, for everything that is truly predicated of its mental part is accidentally predicated, like 'human' in relation to 'animal'. His statement: 'So the preferable in the answer, etc.' We have drawn your attention to the fact that the word 'equality' prevents the interpretation of the previous disjunction as the parts of existence being either the very concept of existence or not, such that this answer is repelled. Yes, if it had been formulated initially in this manner, this answer would be repelled and the choice that they are not existences would be determined.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and commentary on the definition of existence and the epistemic principle that the general is more known than the specific. It begins by stating the principle: the general is a part of the specific, and the part is more known than the whole because knowledge of the whole depends on knowledge of the part, not vice versa. A second proof is then given from emanation: the emanation from the First Principle is general, and the human soul is receptive to concepts. When the agent and receiver exist, emanation only depends on the gathering of conditions and removal of obstacles. Therefore, what has fewer conditions and obstacles is closer to emanation and thus occurs more frequently in the mind, making it more known. The general has fewer conditions and obstacles than the specific, so it is more known. The passage then shifts to an objection and response regarding the definition of existence by its parts. The objector claims that if the parts of existence are themselves existences, then each part would be equal to the whole in quiddity, undermining the claim that existence is more known. The response is to choose that the parts of existence are existences, but they are diverse in quiddities, so equality does not follow. Alternatively, one may choose that the parts are not existences. The commentary explains that the validity of the answer depends on how the disjunction is interpreted: if 'existences' means they are called existences, accidental predication allows difference; if it means they are existences with particularities, then equality follows, so the second alternative (parts not existences) must be chosen. The passage then discusses the nature of existence: whether it is intuitive or acquired. It states that the dispute is based on whether existence is a single shared concept or identical to essence. If it is a shared concept, then some aspects may be intuitive and others acquired; if it is identical to essence, then none is intuitive, and all is acquired. The preferable answer is to say the parts are existences with accidental predication, avoiding equality. The passage then addresses composition: if the whole is a composite, composition is in existence itself, not in its causes. An analogy with sakanjabin (a compound drink) is given to refute the objection: if the parts are not themselves sakanjabin, the compound is a new accidental entity, so composition is in its causes, not in the compound itself. The commentary clarifies that the author's preferred response is based on the possibility that existence is a shared meaning, and the word 'equality' prevents interpreting the disjunction in a way that would make the first answer valid. Ultimately, the choice that the parts are not existences is determined.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that the general is more known than the specific?
A) Because the general has more conditions and obstacles.
B) Because the general has fewer conditions and occurs more in the mind.
C) Because the specific is always intuitive.
D) Because the general is not conceptually distinguishable.

Key Takeaway
The general concept is more known because it is simpler and has fewer conditions, making it more readily impressed upon the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a new topic in Islam, do you gravitate toward specific details before grasping the general principles? How might this habit weaken the foundation of your understanding? Commit today to first mastering the general before diving into specifics.
?
Are you more fascinated by elaborate theological arguments for specific attributes of Allah than by the simple, overarching truth of His existence? Could this imbalance cause you to lose sight of what is most essential? Ask yourself: do I give the general (Tawheed) its due priority in my study and worship?
?
Think of an area of your life where you focus on minor, specific matters (e.g., a particular sin or ritual) while neglecting clear, general obligations (e.g., honesty, justice, prayer). How can the lesson that 'the general is more known' prompt you to realign your priorities today? What specific step will you take?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
8 min
Defining Existence: Objection and Response
Response to the objection about defining existence by parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to defining existence by its parts and the two possible responses, as well as the implications for whether existence is intuitive or acquired.

Core Idea
The parts of existence need not equal existence in essence; accidental predication saves the definition.

After establishing that the general is more known, Sanusi addresses an objection: if we define existence by its parts, and those parts are themselves existences, then each part would be equal to the whole in quiddity, undermining the claim that existence is more known. He explores two alternatives for the nature of these parts and discusses whether existence is intuitive or acquired.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection states: if the parts of existence are existences, then each part would equal the whole in quiddity, making the definition circular and contradicting the claim that existence is more known.
2
Examine the first response
The first response is that the parts are existences, but they are diverse in quiddities. The predication of 'existence' to them can be accidental, so no equality follows.
3
Examine the second response
The second response is that the parts are not existences at all. Upon combination, an additional entity (the whole) emerges, which is existence itself. Composition is in existence, not in its causes.
An analogy with sakanjabin (a compound drink) is used to illustrate this.
4
Reflect on intuitive vs. acquired
The passage also touches on whether existence is intuitive (بديهي) or acquired (كسبي). It connects this to whether existence is a single shared concept or identical to essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم
والأعم جزء الأخص والجزء أعرف من الكل لأن العلم بالكل يتوقف على العلم بالجزء من غير عكس وأيضاً فالفيض من المبدأ الفياض عام والنفس الإنسانية قابلة للتصورات، وإذا وجد القابل والفاعل لم يتوقف الفيض إلا على اجتماع الشرائط وارتفاع الموانع فكل ما كان شرائطه وموانعه أقل كان إلى الفيض أقرب والأعم لا شك أنه أقل شرطاً ومعانداً من الأخص لأن شرط العام ومعانده شرط للخاص، ومعاند له من غير عكس كلي لأن الخاص بحسب خصوصه له شرائط، وموانع لا تعتبر في العام أصلاً فيكون اجتماع شرائطه وارتفاع موانعه أقل بالنسبة إلى الخاص، فيكون وقوعه في النفس وارتسامه فيها أكثر من وقوع الخاص وارتسامه، فيكون أعرف. وجوابه أي جواب الوجه الثالث أنا نختار أن تعريف الوجود بالحد فنختار أولا أن أجزاءه التي يحد بها وجودات قولك : فالجزء مساو للكل في الذهن أخص منه وإن كانا من حيث ذاتهما أعم منه أو مساوياً له وبهذا القدر يتم غرضنا وهو كونه أعرف من كل ما نحاول تعريفه به لأن التعريف بالشيء إنما يكون بعد حصوله في الذهن، ولا يحتاج إلى إثبات أعرفيته من كل ما سواه سواء كان مفهوماً بالفعل أولا
قوله : ( والأعم جزء الأخص) منشؤه عدم الفرق بين حمل الذاتي والعرضي .
قوله : ( وأيضاً فالفيض عام إلخ ) عطف على قوله والأعم جزء الأخص لا على قوله وأيضاً الأول لأنه لا بد في هذا الوجه من اعتبار كونه أعم المفهومات والفاء زائدة لمجرد تحسين الكلام . قوله : ( والأعم لاشك إلخ) أي الأعم من حيث عمومه وإن كان منحصراً في الخاص أقل منه شرطاً ومعانداً ضرورة اشتماله على أمر زائد على العام .
قوله : ( أنا نختار أن أجزاءه إلخ لا يخفى أن هذا الجواب إنما يتم إذا حمل الترديد المذكور بقوله : إن أجزاءه إما وجودات أو لا على أنه يطلق عليها الوجودات أو لا، إذ حينئذ يمكن أن يقال : إنها متخالفة الماهيات، فلا يلزم مساواة الجزء للكل في الحقيقة، وكذا الجواب الذي ذكره الشارح مبني على حمل الترديد المذكور على أنه يصدق عليها الوجودات أولا، فإنه حينئذ يتجه أن يقال: يجوز أن يكون صدق الوجود عليها صدقاً عرضياً، فلا يلزم المساواة المذكورة، وأما إذا حمل الترديد المذكور على أن حقيقتها إما وجودات أي وجودات مع خصوصیات اعتبرت معه على ما مر، فلزوم المساواة المذكورة ظاهر كما بيناه، وحينئذ يتعين الجواب باختيار الشق الثاني وهو أن أجزاءه ما ليست بوجودات كما سيجيء.
من العبارة إذا لم يرد معنى التفضيل لم يبق لادعاء جزئيته عما سواه وجه ولا تقريب حينئذ لقوله : والأعم جزء الأخص قلت : الأظهر أن المراد أنه أعم المفهومات التي يحاول تعريفه بها . قوله : ( وأيضاً الفيض عام الظاهر أنه دليل ثان لأعرفية الأعم معطوف على قوله والأعم جزء الأخص والجزء أعرف لا علة ثالثة لأعرفية الوجود وإن كان ظاهر العبارة يقتضيه وحمله الشارح في تحقيق الجواب عليه
تمام الماهية قلنا ممنوع فإن وجود كل شيء عندنا نفس حقيقته وهي أي حقائق الأشياء متخالفة فكذا الوجودات الواقعة أجزاء للوجود متخالفة في أنفسها ومخالفة في الحقيقة للمركب منها وقد سبقت منا الإشارة إلى أن الخلاف في كون الوجود بديهياً أو كسبياً مبني على كونه مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً، وأما على تقدير كونه نفس الحقيقة فالمناسب أن يقال بعضه بديهي وبعضه كسبي أو يقال : كله كسبي إذ ليس كنه شيء من الحقائق الموجودة بديهيا، فالأولى في الجواب : أن يقال أجزاؤه وجودات وليس يلزم من ذلك مساواة الجزء للكل في الماهية لجواز أن يكون صدق الوجود على تلك الأجزاء صدقاً عرضياً ولا استحالة في صدق الكل على أجزائه، كذلك ونختار ثانياً أن أجزاءه ليست وجودات قوله يحصل عند الاجتماع بين تلك الأجزاء أمر آخر قلنا : نعم وذلك الأمر الآخر هو المجموع من حيث هو مجموع وهو عين الوجود وإن كان كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المجموع ليس وجوداً، فيكون التركيب في الوجود نفسه لا في قابله أو فاعله ثم ما ذكرته منتقض بسائر المركبات التي علم تركيبها يقيناً إذ نطرده بعينه في السكنجبين مثلاً فنقول : إن كانت أجزاؤه سكنجبينات ساوى الجزء الكل في الماهية وإن لم تكن سكنجبينات فإن حصل عند الاجتماع أمر زائد عليها مسبب عن اجتماعها عارض لها هو السكنجبين كان التركيب في علل السكنجبين ومعروضاته لا فيه وإن لم يحصل
قوله : ( وقد سبقت منا إلخ) بقوله : وأما إذا كان مشتركاً لفظياً فليس هناك وجود مطلق متصور بديهة أو كسباً.
قوله : ( وأما على تقدير إلخ) عطف على قوله وقد سبقت وليس داخلاً تحت الإشارة، حتى يرد أنه ليس مشاراً إليه فيما سبق . :قوله فالمناسب إلخ) لا ما قاله المصنف من أنه كسبي فإنه غير مناسب على ذلك التقدير، وفيه إشارة إلى صحته بناء على جواز القول : بكون الوجود معنى مشتركاً مع القول بأن وجود كل شيء نفسه، وإلن لم يكن مذهباً لأحد، ومن هذا ظهر وجه قوله : والأولى دون أن يقول : والصواب وإنما كان جواب الشارح أولى لمناسبة القول باشتراك الوجود معنى. قوله : ( ولا استحالة إلخ) بل هو واقع فإن كل صادق على جزئه الذهني صدقاً عرضياً، كالإنسان بالنسبة إلى الحيوان .
قوله : ( فالأولى في الجواب إلخ قد نبهناك على أن لفظ المساواة مانع عن حمل الترديد السابق، على أن أجزاء الوجود إما نفس مفهوم الوجود أو لا حتى يندفع هذا الجواب نعم لو قرر ابتداء بهذا الوجه اندفع هذا الجواب وتعين اختيار أنها ليست بوجودات .

So we say: If its parts are sakanjabins, then the part equals the whole in quiddity; if they are not sakanjabins, then if an additional matter occurs upon their combination, caused by their combination, an accident of them which is sakanjabin, then composition is in the causes and subjects of sakanjabin, not in it; and if it does not occur... His statement: 'And we have previously indicated, etc.' by his statement: 'But if it is a homonym, then there is no absolute existence conceptualized intuitively or acquired.' His statement: 'And on the assumption, etc.' is conjoined to his statement 'and we have previously indicated' and is not included under the indication, such that it could be objected that it is not referred to previously. His statement: 'So the appropriate, etc.' is not what the author said, that it is acquired, for it is not appropriate on that assumption, and there is in it an indication of its correctness based on the permissibility of saying that existence is a shared meaning along with the statement that the existence of each thing is its own essence, otherwise it would not be a doctrine for anyone. From this the justification of his statement 'and the preferable' becomes apparent, rather than saying 'and the correct', and the commentator's answer was preferable due to suitability for the statement that existence is a shared meaning. His statement: 'And there is no impossibility, etc.' Rather it does occur, for everything that is truly predicated of its mental part is accidentally predicated, like 'human' in relation to 'animal'. His statement: 'So the preferable in the answer, etc.' We have drawn your attention to the fact that the word 'equality' prevents the interpretation of the previous disjunction as the parts of existence being either the very concept of existence or not, such that this answer is repelled. Yes, if it had been formulated initially in this manner, this answer would be repelled and the choice that they are not existences would be determined.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and commentary on the definition of existence and the epistemic principle that the general is more known than the specific. It begins by stating the principle: the general is a part of the specific, and the part is more known than the whole because knowledge of the whole depends on knowledge of the part, not vice versa. A second proof is then given from emanation: the emanation from the First Principle is general, and the human soul is receptive to concepts. When the agent and receiver exist, emanation only depends on the gathering of conditions and removal of obstacles. Therefore, what has fewer conditions and obstacles is closer to emanation and thus occurs more frequently in the mind, making it more known. The general has fewer conditions and obstacles than the specific, so it is more known. The passage then shifts to an objection and response regarding the definition of existence by its parts. The objector claims that if the parts of existence are themselves existences, then each part would be equal to the whole in quiddity, undermining the claim that existence is more known. The response is to choose that the parts of existence are existences, but they are diverse in quiddities, so equality does not follow. Alternatively, one may choose that the parts are not existences. The commentary explains that the validity of the answer depends on how the disjunction is interpreted: if 'existences' means they are called existences, accidental predication allows difference; if it means they are existences with particularities, then equality follows, so the second alternative (parts not existences) must be chosen. The passage then discusses the nature of existence: whether it is intuitive or acquired. It states that the dispute is based on whether existence is a single shared concept or identical to essence. If it is a shared concept, then some aspects may be intuitive and others acquired; if it is identical to essence, then none is intuitive, and all is acquired. The preferable answer is to say the parts are existences with accidental predication, avoiding equality. The passage then addresses composition: if the whole is a composite, composition is in existence itself, not in its causes. An analogy with sakanjabin (a compound drink) is given to refute the objection: if the parts are not themselves sakanjabin, the compound is a new accidental entity, so composition is in its causes, not in the compound itself. The commentary clarifies that the author's preferred response is based on the possibility that existence is a shared meaning, and the word 'equality' prevents interpreting the disjunction in a way that would make the first answer valid. Ultimately, the choice that the parts are not existences is determined.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the parts of existence are existences, the part must be identical in essence to the whole.
Correction: The predication of existence to the parts may be accidental, so they can be diverse in quiddity and not equal to the whole.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one way to avoid the conclusion that the parts of existence equal the whole in quiddity?
A) Claim that the parts are not existences.
B) Claim that existence is intuitive.
C) Claim that the parts are identical to the whole.
D) Claim that the whole is not a composite.

Key Takeaway
The objection to defining existence by its parts is resolved by either allowing accidental predication of existence to diverse parts or by denying that the parts are existences themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume that something must be equal to its parts, have you ever misjudged a person because you focused on an accidental quality instead of their essence? How can the lesson's insight about accidental predication help you see others more clearly?
?
The passage argues that existence can be predicated accidentally to parts. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes treat your acts of worship as if they are the essence of your faith, rather than accidental expressions? What would change if you focused on the essence?
?
If existence is intuitive (badīhī) for some aspects, how do you know that your knowledge of Allah is intuitive? Are you willing to subject that intuition to rational scrutiny, or do you fear losing certainty? What would it cost you to do so?

Secondary Intelligibles and the Objection on Absolute Existence
Definition and defense of absolute existence as a secondary intelligible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of secondary intelligibles and why absolute existence is considered a secondary intelligible despite having the Necessary Existent as an individual in the external world.

Core Idea
A secondary intelligible has no external correspondent that is essential to it.

The passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif tackles a subtle objection: if the definition of secondary intelligibles requires that they have no external correspondent, how can absolute existence be a secondary intelligible when the Necessary Existent is an individual of it in the external world? The commentary clarifies the definition and provides a precise resolution involving the concept of essentiality and predication by analogy (tashkik).

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of secondary intelligibles
Read the opening lines where secondary intelligibles are defined as concepts that attach to things only in the mind and do not correspond to any external reality in terms of their attachment.
Note the condition that the attachment is conditioned by mental existence.
2
Identify the application to existence
See how both absolute existence and particular existence are given as examples of secondary intelligibles, and note the clarification that this does not negate God being an individual of absolute existence.
This sets up the objection.
3
Follow the objection and the rejected solutions
Read the objection that absolute existence seems to have an external correspondent (the Necessary Existent) and the two incorrect responses that are dismissed.
Understand why those responses fail.
4
Grasp the correct resolution
Study the correct answer: the external correspondent must be essential to the secondary intelligible; absolute existence is not essential to its individuals but predicated by tashkik, supported by al-Sharif's analogy with universality.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

كون الوجود متصفاً بالعدم عند الفلاسفة إنما يستقيم في الوجود المطلق، في الوجودات الخاصة للممكنات، وأما الوجود الخاص الواجبي الذي ادعوا أنه عين ذاته تعالى فهو عندهم موجود في الخارج بوجود هو نفسه فحينئذ نقول : كيف يستقيم عندهم الوجود المطلق من المعقولات الثانية، والمعقول الثاني كما سيأتي عبارة عما لا يعقل إلا عارضاً لمعقول آخر، ولم يكن في الأعيان ما يطابقه وللوجود المطلق ما يطابقه في الأعيان عندهم وهو الوجود الواجبي وهذا البحث أورده بعض المتأخرين، وقد يجاب بأن المراد بالمطابق الخارجي المنفي في المعقولات الثانية موجود خارجي، إذا جرد عن المشخصات حصل منه في الذهن ما معقولاً ثانياً على يسمى سيجيء في تحقيق كلية الكلي، ومطابقته لكثيرين وبالجملة موجود خارجي يكون المعقول الثاني ذاتياً، له والوجود المطلق ليس ذاتياً للوجودات الخاصة عند الفلاسفة، ولهذا صرحوا بأنه مقول عليها بالتشكيك، وفيه أن الشريف ذكر في حواشي التجريد أن ليس في الخارج ما يطابق الكلية كما كان للسواد المعقول ما يطابقه في الخارج، ولا شك أن السواد المطلق مقول بالتشكيك وعارض لجزئياته بالتشكيك فيفهم أن المطابق بالمعنى الأعم مما ذكر منفي عن
In the external world, and whatever has no existence is non-existent, since there is for them no intermediate between the existent and the non-existent; so the existent according to them is non-existent? and this does not necessitate the conjunction of two opposites, not in the subject of existence. His saying: (From the secondary intelligibles.) And this does not contradict that His essence – exalted is He – is an individual for it in terms of its being true of Him and its being abstracted from Him. And this is like the concept of quiddity, for it is from the secondary intelligibles, since it does not attach to the primary thing in the mind, and it is not paralleled by something in the external world in terms of its attachment, even though it applies to things in the external world. The fact that existence is described by non-existence according to the philosophers is only correct regarding absolute existence, in the particular existences of contingents. But as for the particular Necessary existence, which they claim is identical to His essence – exalted is He – it is for them existent in the external world with an existence that is itself. Then we say: How can absolute existence be from the secondary intelligibles according to them? And the secondary intelligible, as will come, is an expression for what is only conceived as an accident of another intelligible, and there is no correspondent in concrete realities; but absolute existence has a correspondent in concrete realities according to them, which is Necessary existence. And this investigation was raised by some later scholars. And it may be answered that what is meant by the external correspondent negated in secondary intelligibles is an external existent which, when stripped of individuators, yields in the mind what is a secondary intelligible... as will come in the investigation of the universality of the universal, and its correspondence to many. In short, [the condition is] an external existent of which the secondary intelligible is essential; and absolute existence is not essential to particular existences according to the philosophers, and so they stated that it is predicated of them by analogy (tashkik). And in it, al-Sharif mentioned in the margins of al-Tajrid that there is nothing in the external world that corresponds to universality, as there was for the intelligible blackness something corresponding to it in the external world; and there is no doubt that absolute blackness is predicated by analogy and is an accident of its particulars by analogy; so it is understood that the correspondent in the more general sense than what was mentioned is negated from
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the nature of secondary intelligibles, specifically addressing the objection that absolute existence cannot be a secondary intelligible because it has a real individual (the Necessary Existent) in the external world. It begins by stating that in the external world, what has no existence is non-existent, with no intermediate between existent and non-existent; thus the existent according to the philosophers is non-existent? [likely a textual issue], and this does not entail a contradiction. Then it defines secondary intelligibles as those that attach to a thing in the mind, conditioned by mental existence, and that do not, in terms of their attachment, correspond to something in the external world as a container for that attachment. Both absolute existence and particular existence, since they attach to quiddity only in the mind, have no external correspondent for their attachment, not even in the contingent or the Necessary. However, this does not negate that the essence of God is an individual for absolute existence in terms of truth and abstraction. The commentator then addresses the objection: since the Necessary Existent is identical to its existence, it is an individual for absolute existence in the external world, so absolute existence would seem to have an external correspondent, violating the definition. The commentator dismisses two proposed solutions: (1) that the definition negates correspondence to a particular, not any existent, but the Necessary existence is not a particular of absolute existence (since that would require a specifier); (2) that the Necessary existence is not an individual of absolute existence (contradicting the philosophers' explicit claim that existence has two individuals: Necessary and contingent). Instead, the correct answer is that the external correspondent negated in secondary intelligibles is an external existent that is essential to the secondary intelligible; absolute existence is not essential to particular existences but is predicated of them by analogy (tashkik). He supports this by citing al-Sharif's point that universality (a secondary intelligible) has no external correspondent, even though absolute blackness is predicated by analogy, so the correspondent in a broader sense is negated. The passage ends mid-sentence.

كلية كلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that absolute existence is a real, mind-independent entity because it applies to God, but it is a secondary intelligible that has no external reality as a particular attribute.
Correction: Absolute existence is a secondary intelligible because its external correspondent (the Necessary Existent) is not essential to it; it is predicated by analogy (tashkik) and has no external reality as an accident.

Checkpoint
Why is absolute existence considered a secondary intelligible despite the Necessary Existent being an individual of it?
A) Because absolute existence does not apply to God.
B) Because it does not correspond to an external existent that is essential to it.
C) Because the Necessary Existent is not an individual of absolute existence.
D) Because secondary intelligibles do not apply to any external things.

Key Takeaway
Absolute existence is a secondary intelligible because its external correspondent is not essential to it; it is predicated by tashkik.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that God exists, do you subconsciously imagine 'existence' as a shared property between Him and His creation? How does this lesson's teaching that absolute existence is a secondary intelligible challenge that mental picture? Can you sincerely affirm God's existence without conceptualizing it as a common attribute?
?
This lesson shows that even the philosophers' concept of absolute existence is a mental abstraction with no external reality as such. Where then is the ground of your certainty about God's existence? Are you willing to rest your faith on a reality that transcends even the most refined concepts of your mind?
?
The objection in this lesson arises because people wanted to protect the definition of secondary intelligibles. But think: Have you ever tried to protect a theological framework at the expense of the reality it points to? Is your commitment to correct conceptualizations strengthening your relationship with the Real, or becoming a substitute for it?

Defining Existence by Description (Rasm)
The commentator argues that existence can be defined by a description and responds to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the possibility of defining existence by a description (rasm) and how he addresses objections that such a definition cannot convey the essence.

Core Idea
A description can define existence if a special property reveals its quiddity.

In this passage, the commentator tackles a key objection to defining existence: that descriptions (rasm) never convey the essence. He argues that it is not necessary for a definition to convey the essence; rather, a special property might lead the mind to conceive the quiddity. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and shows how he refutes the charge of circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage contains several parenthetical interpolations from the base text and commentator.
2
Identify the main objection
The base text objects that a description (rasm) does not convey the quiddity (kunh). Notice how the commentator responds that conveying the quiddity is not necessary.
3
Understand the special property argument
The commentator argues that it is possible for a special property, when conceived, to necessitate the conception of the quiddity. This is key to his defense.
This shows that a description can lead to knowledge of the essence even if it doesn't directly define it.
4
Examine the circularity objection
The second objection claims that if existence is self-evident, then arguing that it is more known is circular. See how the commentator points out the inconsistency in the opponent's position.
5
Reflect on the induction rejection
The commentator rejects the induction that nothing can be defined by description. Consider why this matters for the possibility of defining existence.
This step ties the lesson back to the broader theological project.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقيضين جزءاً من الآخر فإن صفة الجزء ليست جزءاً من المركب، ولنا أيضاً أن نختار أن تعريف الوجود بالرسم ( قوله : الرسم لا يعرف الكنه قلنا : لا يجب تعريفه الكنه ) وإيصاله إليه ( وأما أنه لا يفيده ) أي الكنه ( شيء من الرسوم ) أصلا ( فلا لجواز أن يكون من الخواص ما تصوره موجب لتصور كنه (الحقيقة وأن يكون للوجود خاصة، كذا (قوله) في الوجه الثاني لإبطال الرسم ( لا أعرف من الوجود مصادرة فإن من يسلم كونه بديهيا ) ويدعي أنه كسبي كيف يسلم أنه لا أعرف منه ) بل يقول : كونه أعرف يتوقف على كونه بديهياً، فتتوقف مقدمة الدليل على ثبوت المدعى وما ذكرتم من الاستقراء ليس بصحيح عندنا ( قوله ) في الاستدلال ثانياً على كون الوجود أعرف لا
The two opposites are part of the other, for the attribute of the part is not a part of the composite. And we also have the option to choose that defining existence by a description (his statement: 'The description does not convey the quiddity' – we say: It is not necessary to convey the quiddity) and to arrive at it. (As for the claim that it does not benefit) i.e., the quiddity, (any of the descriptions) at all, (no, because it is possible that among the properties there is one whose mental image necessitates the mental image of the quiddity) of the reality, and that existence has a special property. Similarly, his statement in the second way to invalidate the description: ('I do not know of existence' is a circular argument; for he who concedes that it is self-evident) and claims it is acquired, how can he concede that he does not know of it?) Rather, he says: Its being more known depends on its being self-evident, so the premise of the proof depends on establishing the claim, and what you mentioned of induction is not sound according to us. (His statement) in the second inference on existence being more known – no.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a new section discussing whether existence can be defined by a description (رسم). The commentator begins by stating that the attribute of a part is not a part of the composite, responding to an earlier point. Then he says we can choose that existence is defined by a description. The base text objects that a description does not convey the quiddity (كنه). The commentator responds that it is not necessary for a definition to convey the quiddity; it can still lead to knowledge of it. As for the claim that no description benefits the quiddity at all, the commentator denies this because it is possible that some property, when conceived, necessitates the conception of the quiddity, and existence may have such a property. The commentator then addresses another objection: that if existence is self-evident, then the argument that it is more known is circular because it assumes what it tries to prove. The response is that the opponent's claim that existence is acquired is inconsistent with conceding that it is not known from anything else. The commentator also rejects the induction that nothing can be defined by a description.

Misconception: A description (rasm) can never convey the essence of a thing.
Correction: The commentator argues that a description can convey the essence if it includes a special property that, when conceived, necessitates the mental image of the quiddity.

D) He says existence has no property

Key Takeaway
The commentator argues that a description can define existence if a special property necessitates the conception of its quiddity, and the objection of circularity is refuted by exposing the opponent's inconsistency.
