How a Property Can Convey the Essence
Commentator defends possibility of property conveying essence and refutes circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's argument that a property can have a special mental relation that leads to conception of the essence, and why the objection of circularity is not necessarily valid.

Core Idea
A special property can lead the mind to the essence; circularity is not inevitable.

In this passage, the commentator addresses two challenges to the idea that a property can define an essence. First, he explains that a property can have a special relation that makes the mind conceive the quiddity. Second, he shows that the author's attempt to prove circularity is insufficient. This clarifies the epistemological role of properties in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation. Notice the two distinct comments separated by 'قوله: (بل يقول إلخ)'.
The passage is a commentary on a previous text, so it has a layered structure.
2
Understand the first comment: property and essence
Identify how the commentator explains that a property can convey the essence. Look for the key phrase 'نسبة مخصوصة' (special relation) and the analogy of passing from one opposite to another.
The mind can transition from a property to the essence via a special relation.
3
Understand the second comment: refuting circularity
Examine the second comment where the commentator departs from the author's claim. Note the term 'أضرب' (departs) and the claim that the author's argument is insufficient to prove circularity.
The commentator adds a premise to show the author's reasoning is incomplete.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why these clarifications matter. The commentator defends the possibility that a special property can lead to knowledge of the essence, and that the objection of circularity is not automatically valid.
This lesson shows how theologians handle objections about defining essences.

قوله : ( لجواز أن يكون إلخ ) بأن يكون له نسبة مخصوصة بسببها يحصل في الذهن كنه الشيء، فإن الذهن قد ينتقل من الضد إلى الضد ومجرد الاستبعاد لا ينفع.
قوله : (بل يقول إلخ) أضرب عما قاله المصنف : وضم إليه مقدمة إشارة إلى أن ما ذكره المصنف غير كاف في إثبات لزوم المصادرة.
His statement: (Because it is possible that, etc.) i.e., that it has a special relation by which the quiddity of the thing is obtained in the mind, for the mind may pass from one opposite to another, and mere improbability does not help. His statement: (Rather, he says, etc.) He departs from what the author said and appended a premise, indicating that what the author mentioned is insufficient to establish the necessity of circularity.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage combines two comments. The first comment explains the possibility that a property (خاصة) can convey the essence: the property has a special relation such that when conceived, it leads to the conception of the quiddity. The mind can transition from a property to the essence, just as it can go from one opposite to another. The mere improbability of such a relation is not a valid objection. The second comment responds to an objection of circularity: the author's argument that existence is more known is insufficient to prove circularity, because the premise that existence is self-evident is not properly established.

The True Meaning of 'More Known'
Definition and clarification of 'more known'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'more known' in kalam means priority in conception, not mere apparentness, and how this relates to the self-evidence of existence.

Core Idea
Being more known means prior in conception, not just more apparent.

The commentator begins by clarifying a key term in the circularity debate. Some had misunderstood 'more known' as 'more apparent in disclosure,' leading to wrong conclusions. This lesson explains the correct meaning and why it matters for the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'more known'
The passage states that 'more known' (أعرفية) means priority in conception (الأقدمية في التصور). Read this carefully.
This is a technical definition specific to kalam.
2
Understand the consequence if not self-evident
If existence were not self-evident, its knowledge would be prior to it in conception, leading to circularity. Notice the logical chain.
This connects the definition to the circularity argument.
3
Identify the mistaken view
The passage mentions that some erroneously thought 'more known' means more apparent in disclosure. Find this part and see how it led them into error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( يتوقف على كونه (بديهياً لأن المراد بالأعرفية الأقدمية في التصور، فلو لم يكن بديهياً كان معرفته أقدم منه في التصور، وتوهم البعض أن الأعرفية بمعنى الأظهرية في الانكشاف فمنع توقفه على البداهة فوقع فيما وقع.
قوله : (وما ذكرتم إلخ) دفع لما يرد أن قوله كيف يسلم إلخ منع لمقدمة مدللة، وذا لا يجوز بأن منعها راجع إلى منع دليله .
قوله : ( لجواز أن يكون من الخواص إلخ) وذلك لأن المعرفات والحجج معدات لفيضان المطلوب من المبدأ الفياض فيجوز أن يستعد الذهن القوي لفيضان كنه الحقيقة منه بمجرد تصور الخواص فلا يرد أنه كيف يمكن كون الخواص كاشفة لكنه الحقيقة مع أنه لا مناسبة عقلية بينهما، تؤدي إلى الكشف على أن هذا التقرير، إنما يحتاج إليه على مذهب الفلاسفة، وأما عندنا فالعلم بعد النظر الصحيح بمحض خلق الله تعالى بلا أعداد وتوليد، بل بطريق جري العادة كما مر فالأمر أظهر.
قوله : (بل يقول كونه أعرف يتوقف على كونه بديهياً توضيح لمراد المصنف فإن لزوم المصادرة لا يظهر من عبارته ظهوراً تاماً بخلاف عبارة الشارح، لكن فيه بحث وهو أن الأعرفية في نفس الأمر تتوقف على نفس البداهة، ونفس البداهة لا يتوقف على الأعرفية بل مستتبعة إياها وإنما للوقوف عليها هو العلم بالبداهة لا يقال : العلم بالبداهة يتوقف على العلم بالأعرفية اللازم
His statement: (It depends on its being self-evident) because what is meant by being more known is priority in conception. So if it were not self-evident, its knowledge would be prior to it in conception. Some erroneously thought that being more known means being more apparent in disclosure, so they denied its dependence on self-evidence and fell into what they fell. His statement: (And what you mentioned, etc.) is a refutation of the objection that his statement 'how can he concede etc.' is a refutation of a proven premise, and that is not permissible because refuting it is tantamount to refuting its proof. His statement: (Because it is possible that among the properties, etc.) This is because definitions and proofs are preparations for the emanation of the desired from the Effusive Principle; it is possible that a strong mind is prepared for the emanation of the quiddity of the reality from it merely by conceiving the properties. So it is not objected how properties can be disclosive of the reality when there is no rational connection between them leading to disclosure. In fact, this explanation is needed only on the philosophers' view. According to us, knowledge after correct consideration is by sheer creation of God, without preparation and generation, but by way of custom as passed, so the matter is clearer. His statement: (Rather, he says: Its being more known depends on its being self-evident) is a clarification of the author's intent. For the necessity of circularity does not fully appear from his statement unlike the commentator's statement. But there is a discussion: being more known in reality depends on self-evidence itself, and self-evidence itself does not depend on being more known but rather entails it. To grasp it is knowledge of self-evidence; it cannot be said that knowledge of self-evidence depends on knowledge of being more known, which is necessary.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage combines several comments that clarify the circularity argument and the nature of 'being more known'. The first comment explains that 'more known' means prior in conception, so if existence is not self-evident, its knowledge would be prior to it, which is circular. Some misunderstood 'more known' as more apparent in disclosure, leading to error. The second comment refutes an objection that denying a premise is not allowed; the commentator argues that such denial actually rejects the proof. The third comment elaborates on how properties can disclose the essence: definitions and proofs prepare the mind for emanation from the divine Effusive Principle. A strong mind may receive the quiddity just by conceiving properties, and there need not be a rational connection; this is consistent with Ash'ari occasionalism where knowledge is created by God after reflection. The fourth comment clarifies the author's intent: circularity is not obvious from his words, but the commentator shows that being more known depends on self-evidence, not vice versa.

أعرفية
بديهي
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'more known' means 'more apparent in disclosure' (أظهرية في الانكشاف).
Correction: In this context, 'more known' means priority in conception (أقدمية في التصور), not mere apparentness. This is a technical term in kalam.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does 'being more known' (أعرفية) mean?
A) More apparent in disclosure
B) More widely accepted
C) Prior in conception
D) More complex

Key Takeaway
In kalam, 'more known' means conceptually prior; confusing it with apparentness leads to error.

Refuting Objections and the Role of Properties
Refutation and explanation of properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the objection that denying a premise is impermissible, and how properties can disclose the essence through divine emanation or custom.

Core Idea
Denying a premise is allowed if it refutes the proof; properties can disclose essence without rational connection.

The commentator addresses two interconnected points: first, a methodological objection about denying premises, and second, the metaphysical mechanism of how properties can reveal the essence. This lesson unpacks these arguments and shows how they fit into the larger debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the premise objection
The passage responds to the claim that denying a premise is not allowed. The commentator argues that such denial is actually a refutation of the proof itself. Find this part.
This is a methodological point about argumentation.
2
Understand the explanation of properties as disclosive
The commentator explains that definitions and proofs prepare the mind for emanation from the Effusive Principle. A strong mind may receive the quiddity just by conceiving properties, without a rational connection.
3
Contrast the two views: philosophers vs. Ash'ari
The passage notes that this explanation is needed on the philosophers' view. According to Ash'ari occasionalism, knowledge after correct reflection is by God's direct creation, through the way of custom. Compare both.
This highlights the theological context.

Clarifying the Circularity Dependence
Clarification of circularity and dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise nature of the dependence between 'being more known' and self-evidence, and why circularity is avoided.

Core Idea
Being more known depends on self-evidence, but self-evidence does not depend on being more known.

This final passage refines the circularity argument. The commentator shows that the author's intent is that 'being more known' depends on self-evidence, not vice versa. This prevents the charge of circularity. However, the passage ends with an unfinished discussion about knowledge of self-evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification of the author's intent
The commentator explains that the author's statement 'Its being more known depends on its being self-evident' is a clarification. The necessity of circularity is not obvious from the author's own words, but the commentator's phrasing makes it clear.
2
Understand the one-way dependence
The passage states that 'being more known' depends on self-evidence itself, but self-evidence does not depend on 'being more known' – rather, it entails it. This asymmetry avoids circularity.
This is the key logical point.
3
Reflect on the unfinished sentence
The passage ends with 'it cannot be said that knowledge of self-evidence depends on knowledge of being more known'. The text is incomplete; consider what the full argument might be.
The original source may continue on the next page.

Defending Definition by Description (رسم)
Commentary defends definition by description as capable of revealing essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why definition by description (رسم) can be a valid way to achieve knowledge of essential reality, particularly in the case of existence.

Core Idea
Specific properties can lead the mind to grasp the essence.

This passage addresses a common criticism of definition by description: that it can never convey the true essence (الكنه) of a thing. The commentator argues that this is not necessarily true, because some defining properties (الخواص) are so intimately connected to the essence that thinking about them naturally brings the essence to mind. The example of existence is used to illustrate this point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the commentator first dismisses a false claim about contradictories, then moves to the main issue: the objection that description does not convey essence.
Focus on the phrase 'الرسم لا يعرف الكنه' (description does not make known the essence).
2
Understand the commentator's reply
The commentator responds by stating that it is not necessary for definition to convey essence directly; instead, some specific properties may lead to mental representation of the essence. This is the core argument.
Look for the phrase 'لا يجب تعريفه بالكنه' (it is not necessary to define it by essence).
3
Apply to existence
Consider how existence might have a unique property that, when understood, reveals what existence truly is. This shows the validity of definition by description.
The commentator says existence has a specific property (خاصة) that enables this.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Notice that the argument proceeds by rejecting a general objection and then providing a counterexample. This is a common method in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم النقيضين جزءاً من الآخر فإن صفة الجزء ليست جزءاً من المركب، ولنا أيضاً أن نختار أن تعريف الوجود بالرسم ( قوله : الرسم لا يعرف الكنه قلنا : لا يجب تعريفه الكنه ) وإيصاله إليه ( وأما أنه لا يفيده ) أي الكنه ( شيء من الرسوم ) أصلا ( فلا لجواز أن يكون من الخواص ما تصوره موجب لتصور كنه الحقيقة وأن يكون للوجود خاصة، كذا (قوله) في الوجه الثاني لإبطال الرسم
The first observatory - the first objective: On defining existence and non-existence. The two contradictories are part of each other? No, because the attribute of a part is not a part of the composite. And we also have the choice that the definition of existence by description (رسم) (His saying: 'Description does not make known the essence.' We said: 'It is not necessary to define it by essence.') and connecting it to it (And as for it not conveying it) i.e., the essence (anything from descriptions) at all (no, because it is possible that from the properties there is that whose mental representation leads to the mental representation of the essence of the reality, and that existence has a specific property, similarly (His saying) in the second aspect for invalidating description.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of existence and non-existence. It begins by rejecting the idea that two contradictories are parts of each other, explaining that the attribute of a part is not a part of the composite. The commentator then addresses an objection to defining existence by description (رسم). The objection claims that description cannot convey the essence (الكنه). The commentator responds that it is not necessary to define via essence; rather, some specific properties (خواص) when mentally represented can lead to the mental representation of the essence itself. The commentator defends this by pointing out that existence may have a specific property that enables such knowledge. This argument is presented as the second point in rebutting the invalidity of definition by description.

Key Terms
الرسم
الكنه
الخواص
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Definition by description (رسم) can never convey the true essence of a thing.
Correction: It is possible that some specific properties, when mentally represented, lead to the mental representation of the essence, so description can be effective.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why can definition by description (رسم) sometimes lead to knowledge of essence?
A) Because description always includes the essence.
B) Because specific properties may trigger mental representation of the essence.
C) Because existence is self-evident.
D) Because the composite defines its parts.

Key Takeaway
Definition by description is a valid method in kalam because specific properties can lead the mind to grasp the essence, as seen with existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator defends definition by description, saying specific properties can lead to knowing essence. Consider your own existence: what specific property of your own being—like your awareness or your ability to choose—leads you to reflect on the nature of your soul? How does that reflection call you to draw nearer to your Creator?
?
If specific properties can point to essence, then the attributes of Allah (like Mercy and Power) are properties that should lead you to know Him. Are you content to know Allah through His attributes, or do you feel a restless need to grasp His essence? How does this lesson challenge you to deepen your relationship with Him using the properties He has revealed?

The First Objection and Two Initial Replies
Iji presents an objection and two replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the self-evidence of existence and the two initial replies that defend its conceptual independence.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be dismissed as non-self-evident simply because it is an accident of essence.

Iji presents a powerful objection: if existence is an accident of essences, and essences are not self-evident, then existence must be acquired. He immediately offers two replies. The first denies that an accident's concept depends on its subject; the second concedes but notes that a particular essence may be self-evident in quiddity. Let's examine this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the two replies
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: objection → first reply (denial of dependence) → second reply (concession with necessary essence).
The objection uses the principle that the follower of the acquired is more fitting to be acquired.
2
Understand the reasoning behind each reply
The first reply says: an accident can be conceived without its subject, so existence can be independent. The second says: even if dependence is conceded, the subject may be self-evident, so the accident is not necessarily acquired.
3
Connect to the core idea
Ask yourself: how do these replies show that existence's status as an accident does not automatically make it acquired? The core idea is that accidents can be conceptually independent.
This connects to the broader debate on whether existence is self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم
العارض لا يستقل بالمعقولية لكن الماهيات ليست بديهية ( فلا يكون ) الوجود ( بديهياً أيضاً لأن التابع للكسبي أولى بأن يكون كسبياً ) والجواب لا نسلم أنه إذا كان عارضاً للماهية عقل تبعاً لها إذ قد يتصور مفهوم العارض دون ملاحظة معروضة) ومن يدعي أن تصور الوجود أول الأوائل في التصورات كيف يسلم أن تعقله تبع لتعقل غيره ( سلمنا لكن يكفي لتصور العارض ) تصور ماهية معينة وقد تكون ضرورية) فيعقل العارض تبعاً لهذه الماهية الضرورية فلا يلزم كونه كسبياً ( وقد يجاب عنه ) أي عن هذا الوجه ( بأنه يعقل) العارض (تبعاً للماهية المطلقة) الصادقة على الماهيات كلها
ماهية من الماهيات وقد فرض كسبية كنهها وأنه ينافي ما ذكره في الشق الثاني من أن كسبية المعروض تستلزم كسبية العارض لأنه يعقل تبعاً له.
قوله : ( لأن العارض لا يستقل بالمعقولية لاشتماله على المعروض الذي هو غير مستقل بالمفهومية لكونه إضافة وهذا الحكم منشؤه اشتباه مفهوم الشيء بما صدق عليه فإن العروض الذي هو إضافة معتبر في مفهوم العارض لا فيما صدق عليه.
قوله ليست بديهية أي بالكنه .
قوله : (بديهياً) أي بالكنه .
قوله : ( لأن التابع إلخ إذ له احتياجان احتياج لذاته واحتياج بواسطة ما يحتاج إليه وهذا الحكم منشؤه توهم أن ما يحصل عقيب الكسب فهو كسبي وليس كذلك فإن الكسبي ما يحصل بالكسب.
قوله : ( مفهوم العارض أي مفهوم ما صدق عليه العارض وكذا في معروضه لأن الكلام
فيما صدق عليه لا في مفهومهما . قوله: (وقد تكون (ضرورية أي بالكنه كالحرارة والبرودة فهو منع لقوله لكن الماهيات
ليست بديهية.
قوله : ( لأن التابع للكسبي أولى بأن يكون كسبياً مردود بما أشير إليه في مباحث النظر
من أن العلم بالبديهي قد يكون تابعاً للكسبي ومنه علم العالم بأن له هذا العلم الكسبي . قوله : ( إذ قد يتصور مفهوم العارض) فيه أن العارض إذا كان إضافة أو مستلزماً لها لا يتصور بدون المضاف إليه والظاهر أن الوجود من هذا القبيل فلا يتصور بدون المضاف إليه الذي هو معروضه فالأولى أن يجاب بما ذكرنا الآن أو بالجواب الذي أدعى فيه الاستدراك إذ لا استدراك على هذا التقدير فتدبر
•
قوله : ( وقد تكون (ضرورية أي بالكنه كتصور الحرارة وادعاء كسبية الجميع باطل أو نقول : معناه قد يكون تصور تلك الماهية المعينة بديهياً ولو بالوجه والتصور بالوجه يكفي في المتبوعية كما أشرنا إليه فلا يرد منع بداهة شيء من الحقائق .
The First Observatory - The First Purpose: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. The accident is not independent in intelligibility, but the essences are not self-evident, so existence is not self-evident either, because what follows what is acquired is more fitting to be acquired. The answer: we do not accept that if it is an accident of the essence, it is understood in dependence on it, since the concept of the accident might be conceived without noticing its subject, and whoever claims that the conception of existence is the first of first things in conceptions, how can he accept that its understanding is dependent on understanding something else? We accept, but it suffices for conceiving the accident to conceive a particular essence, and it may be necessary, so the accident is understood in dependence on this necessary essence, so it does not necessitate being acquired. And it may be answered—i.e., concerning this aspect—that the accident is understood in dependence on the absolute essence that applies to all essences, which is an essence among essences, and its quiddity is assumed to be acquired, and that contradicts what he mentioned in the second branch that the acquiredness of the subject entails the acquiredness of the accident because it is understood in dependence on it. His saying: 'Because the accident is not independent in intelligibility because it includes the subject which is not independent in conceptuality because it is a relation, and this judgment arises from confusing the concept of the thing with what it applies to, for the accidentalness which is a relation is considered in the concept of the accident, not in what it applies to.' His saying: 'are not self-evident' means in quiddity. His saying: 'self-evident' means in quiddity. His saying: 'because what follows etc.' for it has two needs: a need for itself and a need by means of what it needs, and this judgment arises from the illusion that what occurs after acquisition is acquired, but it is not so, for the acquired is what occurs through acquisition. His saying: 'the concept of the accident' means the concept of what the accident applies to, and likewise in its subject because the discourse is about what it applies to, not about their concept. His saying: 'and it may be necessary' means in quiddity like heat and cold, and it is a refutation of his saying 'but the essences are not self-evident.' His saying: 'because what follows what is acquired is more fitting to be acquired' is refuted by what was indicated in the investigations of theoretical reasoning that knowledge of the self-evident may follow from the acquired, including the scholar's knowledge that he has this acquired knowledge. His saying: 'since the concept of the accident might be conceived' includes that if the accident is a relation or entails it, it cannot be conceived without the related, and apparently existence is of this kind, so it cannot be conceived without the related which is its subject, so it is better to answer as we now mentioned or by the answer in which he claimed a rectification, since there is no rectification on this estimation, so reflect. His saying: 'and it may be necessary' means in quiddity like the conception of heat, and claiming that all is acquired is false; or we say: it means that the conception of that particular essence may be self-evident even if partially, and the partial conception suffices for being followed, as we indicated, so the denial of the self-evidence of any of the realities is not refuted.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the claim that existence is self-evident, followed by multiple replies. The objection argues: existence is an accident ('ârid) of essence; essences (mâhiyyât) are not self-evident (badîhiyya); therefore, existence, being a consequence of something acquired (kasbî), should also be acquired. The first reply denies the premise that an accident is understood only in dependence on its subject, arguing that the concept of the accident can be conceived independently. The second reply concedes but says a particular essence may be self-evident in quiddity (bik-kunh), so the accident can follow it without being acquired. The third reply appeals to the absolute essence (al-mâhiyyat al-mutlaqa) that applies to all essences. The commentary then clarifies each phrase: 'self-evident' means in quiddity; the judgment about the accident's dependence arises from confusing the concept with the referent; the necessary essence (like heat) refutes the claim that all essences are not self-evident; the argument that the follower of the acquired is more fitting to be acquired is rebutted by examples where knowledge of the self-evident follows from acquired knowledge; and the partial self-evidence of an essence suffices. The final point warns that if existence is a relation, it cannot be conceived without its subject, but the answer still holds.

Key Terms
عارض
ماهية
بديهي
كسبي
كنه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something depends on what is acquired, it must be acquired.
Correction: Dependence on the acquired does not necessitate acquisition if the subject is self-evident or if the accident can be conceptualized independently.

Checkpoint
In the second reply, why does existence not become acquired even though it depends on its subject?
A) Because the accident's concept is independent.
B) Because the subject may be self-evident in quiddity.
C) Because existence is not really an accident.
D) Because the objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The first two replies show that an accident's concept can be independent or follow a self-evident subject, so existence need not be acquired.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You've just read that some concepts can be self-evident even if they follow from something else. Are there truths about your own existence that you have been treating as if they need proof, when they are actually immediate? What would change if you accepted them as self-evident?
?
The objector argued that because existence is an accident, it must be acquired. Have you ever dismissed a deep truth simply because it seemed dependent on something you don't fully understand? How might that have kept you from certainty?
?
Iji's second reply says an accident may be understood in dependence on a necessary essence. Is your relationship with Allah like that—do you only know Him through His effects, or do you have a direct awareness? Reflect on whether you experience any aspects of faith as self-evident or as derivative.

The Third Reply and Clarifications on Self-Evidence
Iji gives the third reply and clarifies self-evidence and the concept/referent distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third reply that appeals to the absolute essence and the critical distinction between concept and referent in understanding accidents.

Core Idea
Accidents are understood through the essences they qualify, but those essences can be self-evident in their quiddity.

After the first two replies, Iji offers a third answer: the accident is understood in dependence on the absolute essence that applies to all essences. This is followed by a series of clarifications that refine what 'self-evident' means, distinguishing between conceptual dependence and referential dependence. Let's dive into these deeper points.

Guided Path
1
Read the third reply and the clarifications
Read the Arabic passage: the third reply (al-mahiyyah al-mutlaqa) and then the commentary phrases that explain why the accident's dependence does not imply acquiredness.
Pay attention to the phrase 'اشتباه مفهوم الشيء بما صدق عليه'.
2
Understand the distinction between concept and referent
The clarification notes that the judgment about the accident's dependence arises from confusing the concept of the thing with what it applies to. The relation (accidentalness) is in the concept of the accident, not in its referent.
This is a key epistemological point in kalam.
3
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on how these clarifications strengthen the argument that existence can be self-evident despite being an accident, by redefining what self-evidence means (in quiddity) and clarifying the nature of conceptual dependence.

Source Text

?

?

The Second Objection: Existence Defined Verbally
Al-Iji presents the second objection and its answer regarding verbal definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that existence is defined yet claimed self-evident is answered by distinguishing verbal definition from conceptual definition.

Core Idea
Self-evident concepts can be defined verbally without losing their self-evidence.

In this passage, al-Iji presents the second objection against the self-evidence of existence: if existence were self-evident, scholars would not define it. The answer distinguishes between defining a concept and defining a word. Verbal definitions clarify what is meant by a term, not the concept itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection: scholars do not define self-evident concepts, yet they define existence. Ask yourself why this would challenge existence being self-evident.
2
Study the answer
The answer states that the definition of existence is verbal (ta'rîf lafẓî), not conceptual. It aims to distinguish the meaning of the word 'existence' from other concepts.
Verbal definition does not provide the concept (taṣawwur) but rather affirmation (taṣdîq) of meaning.
3
Reflect on verbal definition of self-evident things
Consider how even self-evident concepts can need a verbal definition when the term is ambiguous or unfamiliar. This does not make the concept non-self-evident.

The First Observatory - The First Purpose: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. (And that they are self-evident, but there is a consideration because the essence insofar as it is essence, i.e., the meaning of the word 'essence', is among the accidents of particular essences, so the discourse returns to it by saying it too is not independent in intelligibility, but is understood in dependence on particular essences that are not self-evident, so it then needs one of the two previous answers, and a rectification is necessary in this answer.) The second aspect: it is said without doubt that (the intellects do not occupy themselves with defining self-evident concepts, just as they do not prove self-evident propositions. So if existence were necessary, they would not define it. The answer: its definition is not to give (its conception such that it contradicts its being self-evident, but rather its definition is to distinguish what is meant by the word 'existence' from among other concepts and to direct the soul to it specifically, so it is a verbal definition whose outcome is affirmation (taṣdîq), as has passed. Self-evident things can be verbally defined, for the self-evident, even if it is present in the mind intuitively, may be unknown in terms of being the meaning of a particular word and intended by it, so it is defined to know that it is its meaning and intended by it.) And it has been answered concerning the second aspect also (that no one has occupied himself with defining 'being in external reality' (al-kawn fi'l-a'yân) which occurred) according to. His saying: 'in dependence on the absolute essence etc.' because he considered in the inference its being an accident of it, or because its being an accident of particular essences entails its being an accident of the absolute essence, since if it were an accident of a particular essence, it would not exist without it in another essence. His saying: 'rather it is understood in dependence etc.' so it is not self-evident, because what follows the acquired is more fitting to be acquired. His saying: 'so it then needs etc.' by saying we do not accept that the absolute essence is understood in dependence on a particular essence, and even if accepted, it suffices for conceiving a particular necessary essence. His saying: 'so a rectification is necessary etc.' i.e., a rectification of the exposition concerning its being an accident of the absolute essence and that it is self-evident. His saying: 'the answer etc.' its summary is denying the implication in his saying 'so if it were necessary, they would not define it' based on why it is not permissible that it be a verbal definition, except that he presented it in the form of a claim to reinforce the denial, and it is extremely strong. His saying: 'its outcome is affirmation' i.e., that the word 'existence' is posited for that meaning. His saying: 'but there is a consideration because the essence etc.' that he did not make from the perspective of consideration that the absolute essence is among the secondary intelligibles that have no existence externally, so existence is only a follower of the particular, because mental existence occurs to it, and it is not necessary that the answerer be among the theologians so that his non-assertion of mental existence is refuted, but there is investigation: if the answerer does not accept the opponent's claim that the thing from among the particular essences is not self-evident in quiddity, he does not need in the answer to say that existence follows the absolute essence; but if he accepts it, then this statement does not occur as an answer because the absolute essence is a particular essence among essences, so reflect.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against existence being self-evident, and its answer, along with further commentary. The objection argues: scholars do not occupy themselves with defining self-evident concepts, yet they define existence; therefore existence is not self-evident. The answer: the definition of existence is not to give its conception (taṣawwur) but to distinguish the meaning of the word 'existence' from other concepts, i.e., a verbal definition (ta'rîf lafẓî) leading to affirmation (taṣdîq). Self-evident things can be verbally defined when the concept is known but its linguistic referent is unknown. Another answer is added: no one has defined 'being in external reality' (al-kawn fi'l-a'yân), which is the real object of definition. The commentary then analyzes earlier phrases: the absolute essence is not independent in intelligibility because it is a secondary intelligible; the objection's implication that the follower of the acquired is acquired is denied; the need for a rectification is discussed; and the final note questions whether the answerer accepts the opponent's premise about particular essences not being self-evident in quiddity, concluding that if he does not, the answer about the absolute essence is unnecessary, and if he does, the absolute essence itself is a particular essence, so the answer is circular.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
تعريف لفظي
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a concept is defined, it cannot be self-evident.
Correction: Verbal definition does not contradict self-evidence; it only clarifies the meaning of a word, not the concept's intuitive grasp.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does defining existence not contradict its being self-evident?
A) Because existence is not actually self-evident.
B) Because the definition is verbal, not conceptual.
C) Because scholars never define existence.
D) Because self-evident things cannot be defined at all.

Key Takeaway
Existence can be verbally defined while remaining self-evident in conception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed you understood a key concept (like tawḥīd or īmān) simply because you knew the word, yet when pressed, could not define what it meant? What does this lesson reveal about your need to clarify your own verbal definitions?
?
The objection in this passage arose because people confused defining a word with defining the concept itself. In your daily life, are there instances where you reject a truth claim because you misunderstand the wording, while the concept remains correct? How can you discipline yourself to distinguish between verbal confusion and genuine intellectual difficulty?
?
This lesson teaches that self-evident truths can still be verbally defined. What is one self-evident belief about Allah that you hold, yet have never articulated precisely in words? Will you take the time today to write a clear, verbal definition of that belief for yourself?

The Second Answer and Commentary on the Essence
Al-Iji presents a second answer and commentary on the absolute essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional answer that no one defines 'being in external reality' and the commentary on the absolute essence as a secondary intelligible.

Core Idea
The real object of definition is not existence in the mind but existence in external reality.

Following the first answer, another answer is given: no one has defined 'being in external reality', which is the real object of inquiry. The commentary then analyzes the earlier claim about the absolute essence being a secondary intelligible and questions the need for the answer. This deepens the discussion on self-evidence and definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer
The second answer states that no one has defined 'being in external reality' (al-kawn fi'l-a'yân). This shifts the focus from the word to the real entity.
2
Understand the commentary on the absolute essence
The commentary explains why the absolute essence (al-mâhiyya al-mutlaqa) is not self-evident: it is a secondary intelligible, understood only in dependence on particular essences.
Secondary intelligibles have no external existence; they are mental constructs.
3
Reflect on the final consideration (Fihi nazar)
The final note questions whether the answerer's position is coherent: if he denies that particular essences are self-evident, he doesn't need the absolute essence answer; if he accepts it, the absolute essence itself is a particular essence, creating circularity.
This shows the subtlety of the debate.

What Is Kalam?
beginner

The First Definition of the Existent: Al-Thabit al-'Ayn
Al-Jurjani presents the first definition of al-mawjud as al-thabit al-'ayn and explains the purpose of the word al-'ayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first definition of al-mawjud as al-thabit al-'ayn and the rationale behind including the word al-'ayn.

Core Idea
The existent is what is fixed in its own entity, not relative to another.

Al-Jurjani begins by introducing the first definition of existence and non-existence as given by those who consider existence to be conceptually acquired. This definition uses the terms al-thabit al-'ayn and al-manfi al-'ayn. As you read, notice how the inclusion of al-'ayn clarifies that the definition applies to the thing itself, not to its relations to other things.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noting the key terms الموجود, المعدوم, الثابت العين, المنفي العين.
2
Understand the definition
Identify that al-mawjud is defined as 'al-thabit al-'ayn' and al-ma'dum as 'al-manfi al-'ayn'. Reflect on what each phrase means: fixed in entity vs. negated in entity.
3
Examine the benefit of al-'ayn
The text explains that the word al-'ayn is added to specify that the definition refers to the thing in itself (fi nafsihi) and not to something existing for another (li-ghayrihi). Ask yourself: Why is this distinction important in ontology?
This nuance prevents confusion between essential existence and relational existence.
4
Connect to the broader discussion
Understand that this is the first definition among several that will be presented. It sets a foundation for distinguishing between existence and non-existence at the most fundamental level.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم ثم من قال بأنه (أي الوجود) يعرف (حقيقة لكونه كسبياً عنده) ذكر فيه عبارات الأولى أنه أي الموجود هو الثابت العين والمعدوم هو المنفي العين وفائدة لفظ العين التنبيه على أن المعرف هو الموجود في نفسه والمعدوم في نفسه لا الموجود لغيره
The First Observatory - The First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. Then whoever said that it (i.e., existence) is known (in reality because it is acquired by him) mentioned in it statements: The first: that al-mawjud (the existent) is al-thabit al-'ayn (the fixed in entity) and al-ma'dum (the non-existent) is al-manfi al-'ayn (the negated in entity), and the benefit of the word al-'ayn is to draw attention to the fact that the defined is the existent in itself and the non-existent in itself, not the existent for another.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the first definition of the existent (al-mawjud) and the non-existent (al-ma'dum) as stated by a certain proponent who holds that existence is conceptually acquired. The definition states: al-mawjud is 'al-thabit al-'ayn' (the fixed in entity) and al-ma'dum is 'al-manfi al-'ayn' (the negated in entity). The benefit of adding the word 'al-'ayn' is to specify that what is being defined is the thing in its own essence (al-mawjud fi nafsihi, al-ma'dum fi nafsihi), not the existent relative to something else (al-mawjud li-ghayrihi). This nuance ensures that the definition applies to the thing itself, not to its relations. The passage sets the stage for further discussion.

Key Terms
الموجود (al-mawjud)
المعدوم (al-ma'dum)
الثابت العين (al-thabit al-'ayn)
المنفي العين (al-manfi al-'ayn)
الوجود (al-wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the benefit of adding the word 'al-'ayn' in the definition of al-mawjud?
A) To indicate that the existent is fixed in the mind
B) To specify that the definition applies to the existent in itself, not for another
C) To exclude the non-existent
D) To indicate that al-mawjud is a substance

Key Takeaway
The existent is defined as what is fixed in its own entity, and the phrase al-'ayn ensures this definition refers to the thing itself, not to its relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you see it as fixed in itself (thabit al-'ayn) or as dependent on what others think of you? How might this definition of existence challenge the way you derive your identity?
?
If something can exist 'for another' (li-ghayrihi) without existing in itself, what does that imply about the ultimate reality of created things? Does this distinction give you a new awareness of your absolute dependence on the Creator for any reality you have?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
The Second and Third Definitions and the Critique of Obscurity
Al-Jurjani presents two definitions of al-mawjud and criticizes them as definitions by the more obscure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third definitions of al-mawjud and why al-Jurjani criticizes them as definitions by what is more obscure (al-akhfa).

Core Idea
True definitions must be clearer than the thing defined, not more obscure.

Al-Jurjani continues his survey of definitions of existence. He presents two more definitions: one based on division into agent/patient or originated/eternal, and another based on knowability. However, he immediately critiques them as 'definition by the more obscure' (ta'rif bil-akhfa), because the general public already know existence but do not know these technical terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the passage and find the second and third definitions. The second defines al-mawjud as that which is divided into agent/patient or originated/eternal. The third defines it as that which can be known and reported about.
2
Understand the critique
Notice that the commentator labels all these definitions as 'definition by the more obscure'. Why? Because the average person understands 'existent' but not terms like 'agent', 'patient', 'originated', 'eternal'.
This is a logical principle: definitions should use clearer terms.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider: if definitions must be clearer than the defined, these definitions fail. They are not false, but they are not helpful for explaining existence to a layperson.
This critique challenges the usability of these definitions in theological education.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم يصح أن والمعدوم عن غيره ولا ما هو أعم منهما (الثانية) أنه المنقسم إلى فاعل ومنفعل)، أي مؤثر ومتأثر ( أو ) المنقسم ( إلى حادث وقديم والمعدوم ما لا فاعل ومنفعل، أو إلى حادث ،وقديم أو ما به يصح أن يعلم الشيء ويخبر عنه ( وكله ) أي كل ما ذكره هذا القائل ( تعريف ) للشيء بالأخفى كما لا يخفى فإن الجمهور يعرفون معنى الوجود والموجود، ولا يعرفون شيئاً مما الخارجي، وهذا التعريف يشمل الموجود الذهني أيضا . قوله : ( والفاعل (الخ) في كون الموجود مأخوذاً في مفهوم الفاعل والمنفعل خفاء، نعم إنهما لا يكونان إلا موجودين. قوله : ( الثانية أنه المنقسم إلى فاعل ومنفعل هذا أولى مما نقله في شرح التجريد من أن الوجود هو الفاعل والمعدوم هو المنفعل لأنه مبني على ما اختاره المتقدمون من تجويز التعريف الناقص بالأخص لأن المعلول الأخير الذي هو منفعل محض موجود وليس بفاعل والممتنعات معدومات وليست بمنفعل على أن في إطلاق المنفعل على المعدوم مطلقاً بعداً كما لا يخفى . قوله : (أي يصح أن يعلم ويخبر عنه هذا التعريف للموجود المطلق المتناول للذهني والخارجي وحينئذ لا يرد عليه المعدوم المطلق لأن المعدوم المطلق لا يصح أن يعلم ويخبر عنه وإلا لكان موجوداً في الذهن لا معدوماً مطلقاً وأما التعريف الأول فهو للموجود الخارجي . قوله: (أو ما به ينقسم الخ إنما لم يقل أو انقسام الشيء أو صحة أن يعلم كما هو المناسب لقوله فيقال الوجود ثبوت العين لأن هذين التعريفين للموجود مأخوذان من الأحوال العارضة له باعتبار وجوده فمبدأ اشتقاق المشتق المذكور فيهما لا يكون حينئذ معرفاً لمبدأ اشتقاق الموجود أعني الوجود كما في تعريفه بالفاعل ألا يرى أن الموجود وإن كان هو الفاعل لكن الوجود ليس هو الفعل أعني التأثير بل المعرف للوجود ما به ذلك الحال المعبر عنه باللفظ المشتق عنه . نعم قد يكون تعريف الموجود بلفظ مشتق مرادف له فحينئذ يكون مبدأ اشتقاقه معرفاً لمبدأ اشتقاق الموجود كالثابت العين. قوله : ( فإن الجمهور يعرفون معنى (الوجود قد يمنع كون المعنى الذي يعرفه الجمهور كنه الوجود الذي كلامنا فيه قوله : ( والفاعل موجود له أثر قيل : ضعفه ظاهر لأنا لا نسلم أن معنى الفاعل موجود له
The First Observatory - The First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. It is valid that [؟] and the non-existent from other [؟] and not what is more general than them. (The second) that it is divided into agent (fa'il) and patient (munfa'il), i.e., efficient and affected, or divided into originated (hadith) and eternal (qadim), and the non-existent is not like that. (The third) that it is that which is known and reported about, i.e., that which is valid to be known and reported about, and the non-existent is not like that. So these statements are definitions of al-mawjud, and from them definitions of al-wujud are known. So it is said: al-wujud is the fixity of the entity (thubut al-'ayn), or that by which a thing is divided into agent and patient, or into originated and eternal, or that by which it is valid for a thing to be known and reported about. And all of it, i.e., everything this proponent mentioned, is a definition of the thing by what is more obscure (al-akhfa), as is not hidden. For the general public know the meaning of existence and the existent, and they do not know any of what is mentioned in these statements. Also, al-thabit (the fixed) is synonymous with al-mawjud, and al-thubut (fixity) with al-wujud, so it is not valid to define it by it as a real definition. And al-fa'il (the agent) is an existent that has an effect in another. His saying: (The third that it is that which is known etc.) The two previous definitions are specific to external existents, while this definition includes mental existents as well. His saying: (And al-fa'il etc.) regarding that al-mawjud is taken in the concept of agent and patient is obscure; yes, they are only existents. His saying: (The second that it is divided into agent and patient) This is more appropriate than what he transmitted in Sharh al-Tajrid than that existence is the agent and the non-existent is the patient, because it is based on what the earlier scholars chose of allowing an incomplete definition by the more specific, because the last effect, which is pure patient, is existent and not an agent, and the impossibilities are non-existents and are not patients, besides that applying 'patient' to the non-existent absolutely is far-fetched, as is not hidden. His saying: (i.e., that it is valid to be known and reported about) This definition is for absolute al-mawjud that includes the mental and the external. Accordingly, absolute non-existence does not apply to it, because absolute non-existence cannot be known and reported about, otherwise it would be existent in the mind, not absolutely non-existent. As for the first definition, it is for the external existent. His saying: (or that by which it is divided etc.) He did not say 'or the division of the thing' or 'the validity of being known' as would be appropriate to his saying 'so it is said: al-wujud is thubut al-'ayn', because these two definitions of al-mawjud are taken from the accidents that occur to it by virtue of its existence, so the source of derivation of the derived term mentioned in them is not then a definer of the source of derivation of al-mawjud, i.e., al-wujud, as in its definition by al-fa'il. Do you not see that al-mawjud, even if it is al-fa'il, yet al-wujud is not the act (al-fi'l), i.e., the effecting, but rather the definer of al-wujud is that by which that state expressed by the derived term occurs. Yes, it may be that the definition of al-mawjud is by a derived term synonymous with it, then the source of its derivation would be a definer of the source of derivation of al-mawjud, like al-thabit al-'ayn. His saying: (For the general public know the meaning of existence) It may be objected that the meaning the general public know is not the essence of existence that is the subject of our discussion. His saying: (And al-fa'il is an existent that has an effect) It is said: its weakness is apparent because we do not accept that the meaning of al-fa'il is an existent that has...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents two additional definitions of al-mawjud (the existent) and critiques them. The second definition states that al-mawjud is that which is divided into agent (fa'il) and patient (munfa'il), or into originated (hadith) and eternal (qadim). The third definition states that al-mawjud is that which can be known and reported about. The commentator then critiques these definitions, saying they are definitions by what is more obscure (al-akhfa) because the general public already know the meaning of existence but do not know these technical terms. Moreover, 'al-thabit' is synonymous with al-mawjud, so it cannot define it properly. He then discusses each definition in detail. The third definition includes mental existents, while the first two are for external existents. He argues that the second definition is better than a version from Sharh al-Tajrid that says 'existence is the agent and non-existence is the patient', because the last effect is existent but not an agent, and impossibilities are non-existents but not patients. He also discusses the derivation of terms: the definitions using 'divided into...' or 'valid to be known' are based on accidental states of the existent, so the source of derivation (e.g., 'division') does not directly define existence itself. However, if the derived term is synonymous with al-mawjud (like al-thabit al-'ayn), then its source can define existence. He ends with a possible objection that the general public's understanding may not be the true essence of existence, and a comment on the definition of al-fa'il.

Key Terms
فاعل (fa'il)
منفعل (munfa'il)
حادث (hadith)
قديم (qadim)
تعريف بالأخفى (ta'rif bil-akhfa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any true statement about a thing can serve as its definition.
Correction: A definition must not only be true but also clearer than the thing defined. The passage shows that definitions using obscure terms are invalid as real definitions.

Checkpoint
What is the main criticism al-Jurjani levels against the second and third definitions of al-mawjud?
A) They are circular
B) They are definitions by what is more obscure
C) They are too broad
D) They are based on false premises

Key Takeaway
The second and third definitions of existence fail because they use terms that are less known than the concept they aim to define.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you explain Islamic concepts to others, do you ever use technical terms (like qadim, hadith, fa'il) without realizing that they might confuse the listener? How can you ensure your explanations remain clear and accessible, just as al-Jurjani insists that definitions must be clearer than the defined?
?
Are there areas of your own faith where you have prioritized complicated scholarly language over the simple, clear understanding that the 'general public' possesses? What would it look like to humble yourself and return to that clarity?
?
This lesson shows that even great scholars can produce definitions that obscure rather than clarify. Where in your life might you be guilty of overcomplicating simple truths to appear knowledgeable? Will you commit to simplicity and clarity, even if it feels less impressive?

The Third Definition: Scope and the Case of Absolute Non-Existence
Al-Jurjani expands on the third definition, highlighting its inclusion of mental existence and its handling of absolute non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the third definition of al-mawjud includes mental existence and why absolute non-existence cannot be known or reported.

Core Idea
The third definition covers both mental and external existence, and absolute non-existence is unknowable.

Al-Jurjani examines the third definition more closely. He notes that while the previous two definitions only apply to external existents, the third definition encompasses mental existents as well. He then addresses a potential objection: if 'existent' is that which can be known, then absolute non-existence cannot be known, because to know it would make it mentally existent.

Guided Path
1
Notice the scope of the third definition
Read the passage and see how al-Jurjani says the third definition includes mental existents (al-mawjud al-dhihni), unlike the first two definitions which are restricted to external existents.
This is an important expansion of the concept of existence.
2
Understand the argument about absolute non-existence
Al-Jurjani argues that absolute non-existence cannot be known or reported, because if it could be known, it would exist in the mind and thus not be absolutely non-existent.
This preserves the consistency of the definition.
3
Compare with the first definition
Note that the first definition (fixity of entity) is for external existents only, while the third is more comprehensive.
This shows the third definition's advantage.

Commentary
This passage presents two additional definitions of al-mawjud (the existent) and critiques them. The second definition states that al-mawjud is that which is divided into agent (fa'il) and patient (munfa'il), or into originated (hadith) and eternal (qadim). The third definition states that al-mawjud is that which can be known and reported about. The commentator then critiques these definitions, saying they are definitions by what is more obscure (al-akhfa) because the general public already know the meaning of existence but do not know these technical terms. Moreover, 'al-thabit' is synonymous with al-mawjud, so it cannot define it properly. He then discusses each definition in detail. The third definition includes mental existents, while the first two are for external existents. He argues that the second definition is better than a version from Sharh al-Tajrid that says 'existence is the agent and non-existence is the patient', because the last effect is existent but not an agent, and impossibilities are non-existents but not patients. He also discusses the derivation of terms: the definitions using 'divided into...' or 'valid to be known' are based on accidental states of the existent, so the source of derivation (e.g., 'division') does not directly define existence itself. However, if the derived term is synonymous with al-mawjud (like al-thabit al-'ayn), then its source can define existence. He ends with a possible objection that the general public's understanding may not be the true essence of existence, and a comment on the definition of al-fa'il.

Key Terms
موجود ذهني (mawjud dhihni)
معدوم مطلق (ma'dum mutlaq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence only applies to physical objects.
Correction: The third definition shows that existence includes mental entities as well, as they can be known and reported about.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the third definition of al-mawjud include that the first two do not?
A) Eternal existents
B) Mental existents
C) Impossible objects
D) Divine attributes

Key Takeaway
The third definition broadens existence to include mental beings, and absolute non-existence is excluded by its own nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often worry about future events that have not occurred—things that are absolutely non-existent. Al-Jurjani argues that such non-existence cannot be known. How does this challenge your habit of fearing the unknown? Will you surrender that anxiety to Allah today?
?
Consider a belief you hold that has no external reality but exists only in your mind—for example, a suspicion about someone's intentions. The third definition counts this as a mental existent. How will you evaluate its truthfulness before Allah, knowing that you can report about it?

Derivation and Definitional Circularity in the Definitions
Al-Jurjani critiques the definitions based on derivation issues and responds to objections about common knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that definitions using derived terms (like 'divided') do not define existence itself, and why the public's understanding may not capture the essence.

Core Idea
Definitions must match the thing's essence; derived terms based on accidents fail to define existence.

Al-Jurjani continues his critical analysis, focusing on the linguistic and logical issues. He argues that when defining al-mawjud using terms derived from accidental states (like 'divided into...'), the source of derivation does not directly define existence. He also addresses the objection that the general public's concept of existence may not be the true essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the derivation issue
Read the passage where al-Jurjani discusses why the definitions using 'divided into...' or 'valid to be known' are problematic. The terms are derived from accidental states, so their source does not define existence itself.
He contrasts this with defining existence by 'agent' (fa'il), which also fails because existence is not the act.
2
Understand the synonym exception
Al-Jurjani concedes that if the derived term is synonymous with al-mawjud (like al-thabit al-'ayn), then its source can define existence.
This is a subtle point about derivation.
3
Consider the objection about common knowledge
The passage ends with an objection: the public's understanding of existence may not be the true essence. This challenges the earlier critique.
This objection suggests that the general public's concept might be superficial.

The First Observatory - The First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. It is valid that [؟] and the non-existent from other [؟] and not what is more general than them. (The second) that it is divided into agent (fa'il) and patient (munfa'il), i.e., efficient and affected, or divided into originated (hadith) and eternal (qadim), and the non-existent is not like that. (The third) that it is that which is known and reported about, i.e., that which is valid to be known and reported about, and the non-existent is not like that. So these statements are definitions of al-mawjud, and from them definitions of al-wujud are known. So it is said: al-wujud is the fixity of the entity (thubut al-'ayn), or that by which a thing is divided into agent and patient, or into originated and eternal, or that by which it is valid for a thing to be known and reported about. And all of it, i.e., everything this proponent mentioned, is a definition of the thing by what is more obscure (al-akhfa), as is not hidden. For the general public know the meaning of existence and the existent, and they do not know any of what is mentioned in these statements. Also, al-thabit (the fixed) is synonymous with al-mawjud, and al-thubut (fixity) with al-wujud, so it is not valid to define it by it as a real definition. And al-fa'il (the agent) is an existent that has an effect in another. His saying: (The third that it is that which is known etc.) The two previous definitions are specific to external existents, while this definition includes mental existents as well. His saying: (And al-fa'il etc.) regarding that al-mawjud is taken in the concept of agent and patient is obscure; yes, they are only existents. His saying: (The second that it is divided into agent and patient) This is more appropriate than what he transmitted in Sharh al-Tajrid than that existence is the agent and the non-existent is the patient, because it is based on what the earlier scholars chose of allowing an incomplete definition by the more specific, because the last effect, which is pure patient, is existent and not an agent, and the impossibilities are non-existents and are not patients, besides that applying 'patient' to the non-existent absolutely is far-fetched, as is not hidden. His saying: (i.e., that it is valid to be known and reported about) This definition is for absolute al-mawjud that includes the mental and the external. Accordingly, absolute non-existence does not apply to it, because absolute non-existence cannot be known and reported about, otherwise it would be existent in the mind, not absolutely non-existent. As for the first definition, it is for the external existent. His saying: (or that by which it is divided etc.) He did not say 'or the division of the thing' or 'the validity of being known' as would be appropriate to his saying 'so it is said: al-wujud is thubut al-'ayn', because these two definitions of al-mawjud are taken from the accidents that occur to it by virtue of its existence, so the source of derivation of the derived term mentioned in them is not then a definer of the source of derivation of al-mawjud, i.e., al-wujud, as in its definition by al-fa'il. Do you not see that al-mawjud, even if it is al-fa'il, yet al-wujud is not the act (al-fi'l), i.e., the effecting, but rather the definer of al-wujud is that by which that state expressed by the derived term occurs. Yes, it may be that the definition of al-mawjud is by a derived term synonymous with it, then the source of its derivation would be a definer of the source of derivation of al-mawjud, like al-thabit al-'ayn. His saying: (For the general public know the meaning of existence) It may be objected that the meaning the general public know is not the essence of existence that is the subject of our discussion. His saying: (And al-fa'il is an existent that has an effect) It is said: its weakness is apparent because we do not accept that the meaning of al-fa'il is an existent that has...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents two additional definitions of al-mawjud (the existent) and critiques them. The second definition states that al-mawjud is that which is divided into agent (fa'il) and patient (munfa'il), or into originated (hadith) and eternal (qadim). The third definition states that al-mawjud is that which can be known and reported about. The commentator then critiques these definitions, saying they are definitions by what is more obscure (al-akhfa) because the general public already know the meaning of existence but do not know these technical terms. Moreover, 'al-thabit' is synonymous with al-mawjud, so it cannot define it properly. He then discusses each definition in detail. The third definition includes mental existents, while the first two are for external existents. He argues that the second definition is better than a version from Sharh al-Tajrid that says 'existence is the agent and non-existence is the patient', because the last effect is existent but not an agent, and impossibilities are non-existents but not patients. He also discusses the derivation of terms: the definitions using 'divided into...' or 'valid to be known' are based on accidental states of the existent, so the source of derivation (e.g., 'division') does not directly define existence itself. However, if the derived term is synonymous with al-mawjud (like al-thabit al-'ayn), then its source can define existence. He ends with a possible objection that the general public's understanding may not be the true essence of existence, and a comment on the definition of al-fa'il.

Key Terms
مشتق (mushtaq)
مبدأ اشتقاق (mabda' ishtiqaq)
ثابت العين (thabit al-'ayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any derived term can be used to define its source.
Correction: The passage shows that only if the derived term is synonymous with the defined term does its source provide a valid definition.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, why does the definition 'that by which a thing is divided into agent/patient' fail to define existence?
A) It defines the existent, not existence
B) 'Division' is derived from an accidental state, not from existence itself
C) It is circular
D) It is too broad

Key Takeaway
Definitions using derived terms must ensure that the source of derivation is the same as the thing defined, otherwise they fail to capture the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you mean it in the same way as the common person, or have you striven to understand the reality of His existence as the scholars define it? What is one concrete step you can take this week to deepen your understanding of Allah's attributes?
?
Have you ever been content with a mere verbal affirmation of a core Islamic belief without striving to grasp its true meaning? How might this complacency affect your faith, and what will you do today to move beyond surface-level knowledge?

The Three Definitions of the Existent and Their First Critique
Enumeration of three definitions and initial critique of obscurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proposed definitions of the existent and the critique that they define by what is more obscure.

Core Idea
Defining existence by division or knowability is obscure or circular.

This lesson introduces the three definitions of the existent that are common in kalam: as that which is divided into agent/patient, into originated/eternal, or as that which can be known and informed about. We then examine the first major critique: that these definitions are 'definitions by the more obscure' (ta'rif bil-akhfa), because the general public already knows existence better than these technical terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with definitions
Read the Arabic passage carefully to identify the three definitions of the existent and the corresponding definitions of existence.
The passage also gives the definition of the non-existent as the opposite.
2
Understand the critique of obscurity
Notice the commentator's claim that these definitions are 'definition by the more obscure' because ordinary people already grasp existence without these terms.
This is a methodological critique: a definition should clarify, not obscure.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this critique matters: if definitions are not clearer, they fail their purpose. This sets up further critiques.
The passage also hints at circularity, which will be explored in the next lesson.

وجودهما
The First Observatory - First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. It is valid that [the existent is that which is known and informed about] and the non-existent is other than that, and nothing is more general than them. (The second) that it is divided into agent and patient, i.e., effecter and effected, or divided into originated and eternal, and the non-existent is not like that. (The third) that it is what is known and informed about, i.e., it is valid to know and inform about it, and the non-existent is not like that. So these expressions are definitions of the existent, and from them one learns definitions of existence: it is said: existence is the affirmation of the essence, or that by which a thing is divided into agent and patient, or into originated and eternal, or that by which it is valid that a thing be known and informed about. And all of it, i.e., everything this speaker mentioned, is a definition of the thing by what is more obscure, as is not hidden, for the general public know the meaning of existence and the existent, and they do not know anything of what is mentioned in these expressions. Also, 'the fixed' is synonymous with the existent, and 'fixity' with existence, so it is not valid to define it with it as a real definition. And the agent is an existent having an effect in another. His statement: (the third) that it is what is known (etc.): the two previous definitions are specific to external existents, and this definition includes mental existents as well. His statement: (and the agent (etc.)): that the existent is taken in the concept of agent and patient is obscure; yes, they are only existents. His statement: (the second) that it is divided into agent and patient: this is more appropriate than what he transmitted in Sharh al-Tajrid that existence is the agent and nonexistence is the patient, because it is based on what the ancients chose of allowing an incomplete definition by the more specific, because the ultimate effect which is pure patient is existent and not an agent, and impossibles are non-existents and are not patients, besides that applying 'patient' to the non-existent absolutely is far-fetched, as is not hidden. His statement: (i.e., it is valid to know and inform about it): this definition is for the absolute existent inclusive of mental and external; then the absolute non-existent is not directed against it because the absolute non-existent is not valid to be known and informed about, otherwise it would be existent in the mind, not absolutely non-existent. As for the first definition, it is for the external existent. His statement: (or that by which it is divided, etc.): he did not say 'or the division of the thing' or 'the validity of being known' as is appropriate for his saying 'existence is the affirmation of the essence', because these two definitions of the existent are taken from the accidental states of it by virtue of its existence; so the derivative's source of derivation in them is not then a definer of the source of derivation of 'the existent', i.e., existence, as in its definition by 'agent'. Do you not see that the existent, even if it is the agent, existence is not the act, i.e., effecting; rather, the definer of existence is that by which that state expressed by the derived word occurs. Yes, sometimes the existent is defined by a synonymous derived word; then its source of derivation is a definer of the source of derivation of 'the existent', like 'the fixed essence'. His statement: (for the general public know the meaning of existence): it may be denied that the meaning known to the public is the essence of existence we are discussing. His statement: (and the agent is an existent having an effect): it was said: its weakness is apparent, for we do not concede that the meaning of agent is 'an existent having an effect in another' and 'patient' is 'an existent having an effect from another', and 'the eternal' is 'an existent with no beginning', and 'the originated' here is 'an existent having a beginning', so it is not valid to take any of these in defining the existent. And the validity of knowledge and information is the possibility of their existence, so defining by them also is circular. His statement: (an existent with no beginning): for the non-existent that has no beginning is called eternal. His statement: (here): he only said 'here' because 'originated' may be used in the sense of 'renewed', including the non-existent that has a beginning. His statement: (and the validity of knowledge and information (etc.)): for their meaning is the possibility of knowledge and information, and possibility is not related to something either by virtue of its existence in itself or its existence for other, so its meaning is the possibility of their existence.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a critique of definitions of the existent (al-mawjud) and existence (al-wujud). The commentator begins by quoting several definitions from the Matn: the existent is that which can be known and informed about; it is divided into agent and patient; it is divided into originated and eternal; existence is the affirmation of the essence or that by which division is made. He then points out that all these definitions are problematic. The first problem is that they define the thing by what is more obscure (ta'rif bil-akhfa), because the common people already know existence but do not know these technical terms. The second problem is that terms like 'the fixed' (al-thabit) are synonymous with the existent, so the definition is circular. The third problem, illustrated in later glosses, is that the definitions already presuppose existence: for example, 'agent' is defined as 'an existent having an effect', so the definiendum (existent) appears in the definiens. Similarly, 'patient', 'eternal', and 'originated' all incorporate the notion of existence. The commentator also discusses the scope of the definitions: the first two definitions (based on division) apply only to external existents, while the third (being knowable/informable) includes mental existents. He addresses objections and clarifies that the absolute non-existent cannot be known. The passage concludes by stating that the definition using validity of knowledge and information is circular because it relies on possibility, which itself is a modal notion. The overall argument is that these definitions are inadequate for a real definition.

Key Terms
الوجود
الموجود
تعريف بالأخفى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Definitions of existence are straightforward and unproblematic.
Correction: The passage shows that these common definitions are problematic because they are obscure or circular.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the definitions of the existent?
A) Because they are false
B) Because they define by what is more obscure
C) Because they are too long
D) Because they contradict the Quran

Key Takeaway
The proposed definitions fail because they rely on terms that are either more obscure or presuppose existence.

?

Circularity and Presupposition of Existence in the Definitions
Critique of circularity and scope of definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definitions of the existent are circular because they assume existence in the definiens.

Core Idea
Every definition of existence already contains existence.

This lesson continues the critique by examining how each definition implicitly or explicitly uses the concept of existence. For example, defining the existent as 'agent' requires that an agent is an existent with an effect. Similarly, 'eternal' and 'originated' are defined in terms of existence. This circularity invalidates the definitions as real definitions. The lesson also covers the scope of the definitions and the circularity of the knowability definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on circularity
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the arguments that the definitions presuppose existence.
Pay attention to the definitions of agent, patient, eternal, and originated.
2
Identify circularity in agent/patient
The commentator points out that 'agent' means 'an existent having an effect', so existence is already assumed.
Similarly for 'patient'.
3
Identify circularity in eternal/originated
Eternal is defined as 'an existent with no beginning', and originated as 'an existent with a beginning'.
Thus both definitions contain 'existent'.
4
Examine the knowability definition
The definition using 'validity of knowledge and information' relies on possibility, which is also circular because possibility depends on existence.
This final point ties the critique together.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم يصح أن والمعدوم عن غيره ولا ما هو أعم منهما (الثانية) أنه المنقسم إلى فاعل ومنفعل)، أي مؤثر ومتأثر ( أو ) المنقسم ( إلى حادث وقديم والمعدوم ما لا يكون كذلك الثالثة أنه ما يعلم ويخبر عنه ( أي يصح أن يعلم ويخبر عنه، والمعدوم ما لا يكون كذلك، فهذه العبارات تعريفات للموجود ويعلم منها تعريفات الوجود فيقال : الوجود ثبوت العين أو ما به ينقسم الشيء إلى
