الخارجي، وهذا التعريف يشمل الموجود الذهني أيضا . قوله : ( والفاعل (الخ) في كون الموجود مأخوذاً في مفهوم الفاعل والمنفعل خفاء، نعم إنهما لا يكونان إلا موجودين. قوله : ( الثانية أنه المنقسم إلى فاعل الشيء أو صحة أن يعلم كما هو المناسب لقوله فيقال الوجود ثبوت العين لأن هذين التعريفين للموجود مأخوذان من الأحوال العارضة له باعتبار وجوده فمبدأ اشتقاق المشتق المذكور فيهما لا يكون حينئذ معرفاً لمبدأ وجودهما
The First Observatory - First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. It is valid that [the existent is that which is known and informed about] and the non-existent is other than that, and nothing is more general than them. (The second) that it is divided into agent and patient, i.e., effecter and effected, or divided into originated and eternal, and the non-existent is not like that. (The third) that it is what is known and informed about, i.e., it is valid to know and inform about it, and the non-existent is not like that. So these expressions are definitions of the existent, and from them one learns definitions of existence: it is said: existence is the affirmation of the essence, or that by which a thing is divided into agent and patient, or into originated and eternal, or that by which it is valid that a thing be known and informed about. And all of it, i.e., everything this speaker mentioned, is a definition of the thing by what is more obscure, as is not hidden, for the general public know the meaning of existence and the existent, and they do not know anything of what is mentioned in these expressions. Also, 'the fixed' is synonymous with the existent, and 'fixity' with existence, so it is not valid to define it with it as a real definition. And the agent is an existent having an effect in another. His statement: (the third) that it is what is known (etc.): the two previous definitions are specific to external existents, and this definition includes mental existents as well. His statement: (and the agent (etc.)): that the existent is taken in the concept of agent and patient is obscure; yes, they are only existents. His statement: (the second) that it is divided into agent and patient: this is more appropriate than what he transmitted in Sharh al-Tajrid that existence is the agent and nonexistence is the patient, because it is based on what the ancients chose of allowing an incomplete definition by the more specific, because the ultimate effect which is pure patient is existent and not an agent, and impossibles are non-existents and are not patients, besides that applying 'patient' to the non-existent absolutely is far-fetched, as is not hidden. His statement: (i.e., it is valid to know and inform about it): this definition is for the absolute existent inclusive of mental and external; then the absolute non-existent is not directed against it because the absolute non-existent is not valid to be known and informed about, otherwise it would be existent in the mind, not absolutely non-existent. As for the first definition, it is for the external existent. His statement: (or that by which it is divided, etc.): he did not say 'or the division of the thing' or 'the validity of being known' as is appropriate for his saying 'existence is the affirmation of the essence', because these two definitions of the existent are taken from the accidental states of it by virtue of its existence; so the derivative's source of derivation in them is not then a definer of the source of derivation of 'the existent', i.e., existence, as in its definition by 'agent'. Do you not see that the existent, even if it is the agent, existence is not the act, i.e., effecting; rather, the definer of existence is that by which that state expressed by the derived word occurs. Yes, sometimes the existent is defined by a synonymous derived word; then its source of derivation is a definer of the source of derivation of 'the existent', like 'the fixed essence'. His statement: (for the general public know the meaning of existence): it may be denied that the meaning known to the public is the essence of existence we are discussing. His statement: (and the agent is an existent having an effect): it was said: its weakness is apparent, for we do not concede that the meaning of agent is 'an existent having an effect in another' and 'patient' is 'an existent having an effect from another', and 'the eternal' is 'an existent with no beginning', and 'the originated' here is 'an existent having a beginning', so it is not valid to take any of these in defining the existent. And the validity of knowledge and information is the possibility of their existence, so defining by them also is circular. His statement: (an existent with no beginning): for the non-existent that has no beginning is called eternal. His statement: (here): he only said 'here' because 'originated' may be used in the sense of 'renewed', including the non-existent that has a beginning. His statement: (and the validity of knowledge and information (etc.)): for their meaning is the possibility of knowledge and information, and possibility is not related to something either by virtue of its existence in itself or its existence for other, so its meaning is the possibility of their existence.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a critique of definitions of the existent (al-mawjud) and existence (al-wujud). The commentator begins by quoting several definitions from the Matn: the existent is that which can be known and informed about; it is divided into agent and patient; it is divided into originated and eternal; existence is the affirmation of the essence or that by which division is made. He then points out that all these definitions are problematic. The first problem is that they define the thing by what is more obscure (ta'rif bil-akhfa), because the common people already know existence but do not know these technical terms. The second problem is that terms like 'the fixed' (al-thabit) are synonymous with the existent, so the definition is circular. The third problem, illustrated in later glosses, is that the definitions already presuppose existence: for example, 'agent' is defined as 'an existent having an effect', so the definiendum (existent) appears in the definiens. Similarly, 'patient', 'eternal', and 'originated' all incorporate the notion of existence. The commentator also discusses the scope of the definitions: the first two definitions (based on division) apply only to external existents, while the third (being knowable/informable) includes mental existents. He addresses objections and clarifies that the absolute non-existent cannot be known. The passage concludes by stating that the definition using validity of knowledge and information is circular because it relies on possibility, which itself is a modal notion. The overall argument is that these definitions are inadequate for a real definition.

فاعل
منفعل
قديم
حادث
دوري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Agent and patient are simple concepts that do not involve existence.
Correction: The passage shows that 'agent' and 'patient' are defined in terms of existence, so they cannot be used to define the existent without circularity.

Checkpoint
Why is the definition of the existent as 'agent' considered circular according to the passage?
A) Because agent means 'existent having an effect', so existence is presupposed
B) Because agents are not always existent
C) Because the definition is too narrow
D) Because the term 'agent' is obscure

Key Takeaway
Any definition of the existent that uses terms like agent, patient, eternal, or originated already presupposes existence, making the definition circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to define your own beliefs about existence, do you find yourself using words that already assume what you're trying to prove? How does this lesson reveal the subtle circularity in your own thinking?
?
The author shows that defining existence by agent, patient, eternal, etc. is circular. In your spiritual life, are there concepts you take for granted without examining their foundations? What would it mean to uproot those assumptions?
?
You may think you understand what existence is, but could you give a definition that doesn't presuppose existence? If not, how does this affect the way you approach the reality of God's attributes? Might you be relying on circular reasoning in your aqeedah?

The Univocity of Existence: Shared Concept or Gradational?
Presentation of the position that existence is a univocal concept shared by all existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key position that existence is a shared concept, its proponents, and the distinction between gradational and univocal interpretations.

Core Idea
Existence is one meaning shared by all existents, but debated as gradational or univocal.

This lesson introduces a foundational claim in metaphysics: existence is not an equivocal term but a single concept in which all things participate. Different schools agree on this but disagree on how existence applies—whether with degrees or equally. The passage also explains why the investigation of self-evidence is placed before the investigation of sharing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the opening statement that existence is shared conceptually.
The phrase 'اشتراكاً معنوياً' is key.
2
Note the positions of different schools
The passage lists philosophers, Mu'tazila (except Abu al-Husayn), and a group of Ash'aris as holding that existence is shared. Mark each group.
The exception for Abu al-Husayn is important.
3
Understand the gradational vs univocal distinction
Philosophers say existence is mutashakkik (gradational), while others say it is mutawati' (univocal). Reflect on what each means.
Mutashakkik applies with different intensities; mutawati' applies equally.
4
Consider the methodological note
The commentator explains why scholars discuss self-evidence before sharing, even though sharing is more fundamental. The reason: conception precedes assent.
5
Examine the phrase 'shared' as elliptical
The final comment clarifies that 'shared' stands for 'shared in', and the claim is a universal affirmative.
This is a grammatical point that affects interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثاني : في أنه مشترك ] (في أنه أي الوجود مشترك) اشتراكاً معنوياً أي هو معنى واحد اشترك فيه الموجودات بأسرها ( وإليه ذهب الحكماء والمعتزلة ( غير أبي الحسين وأتباعه وذهب إليه جمع من الأشاعرة أيضاً إلا أنه مشكك عند الحكماء متواطئ عند غيرهم، وإنما قوله : ( في أنه أي الوجود الخ قد جرت عادة القوم بتقديم بحث بداهة تصور الوجود على بحث اشتراكه مع أن النزاع في بداهته ونظريته، فرع اشتراكه كما مرّ ولعل وجهه أن تصور الشيء مقدم على التصديق بأحواله فالبحث المتعلق بتصوره أحرى بالتقديم فكأنهم بنوا حكم البداهة والنظرية على اشتراكه في بادئ الرأي، ثم بينوا أن هذا الاشتراك الذي هو في بادئ الرأي ثابت في الواقع. قوله : (أي هو معنى واحد (الخ أشار بذلك إلى أن قوله مشترك على الحذف والإيصال والأصل مشترك فيه وإلى أن المدعى موجبة كلية .
[The Second Objective: On Its Being Shared] (On that is, existence is shared) with a conceptual sharing, i.e., it is one meaning shared by all existents (and this was held by the philosophers and the Mu'tazila, except for Abu al-Husayn and his followers, and a group of the Ash'aris also held this, except that it is gradational (mutashakkik) according to the philosophers, and univocal (mutawati') according to others. And only... His statement: (On that is, existence, etc.): The custom of the scholars has been to present the investigation of the self-evidence of the conception of existence before the investigation of its sharing, even though the dispute over its self-evidence and theory is a branch of its sharing, as has passed. Perhaps the reason is that the conception of a thing precedes assent to its states, so the investigation related to its conception is more appropriate for presentation. It is as if they based the judgment of self-evidence and theory on its sharing in the initial view, then they explained that this sharing, which is in the initial view, is established in reality. His statement: (i.e., it is one meaning, etc.): He indicated by that that his saying 'shared' is with elision and inclusion, the original being 'shared in', and that the claim is a universal affirmative.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on whether existence (al-wujud) is a shared concept (mushtarak). The commentator states the position: existence is one meaning in which all existents participate (ishtirak ma'nawi). This view is held by the philosophers (hukama') and the Mu'tazila, except for Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers; a group of Ash'aris also hold this, but with a nuance: according to the philosophers it is gradational (mutashakkik), meaning it applies to different things with different degrees (e.g., substance and accident), while according to others (like the Ash'aris who affirm it) it is purely univocal (mutawati'), applying equally. The commentator then clarifies a methodological point: scholars customarily precede the investigation of the self-evidence of the concept of existence with the investigation of its sharing, even though the dispute over self-evidence vs. theoreticity is a branch of the issue of sharing. The reason given is that the conception of a thing (tasawwur) comes before assent (tasdiq) to its properties, so the discussion of the conception itself takes priority. It is as if they first assume sharing in the initial view, then prove that this sharing is real. Finally, the commentator explains the phrasing of the Matn: 'shared' (mushtarak) is elliptical for 'shared in' (mushtarak fihi), and the claim is a universal affirmative (i.e., all existents share in existence).

Key Terms
مشترك (mushtarak)
اشتراك معنوي (conceptual sharing)
مشكك (mutashakkik, gradational)
متواطئ (mutawati', univocal)
بداهة (self-evidence)
تصور (conception)
تصديق (assent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'shared' means existence is exactly the same in all existents without any difference in intensity or mode.
Correction: The passage notes that philosophers consider it gradational (mutashakkik), meaning it applies with different degrees, while others consider it purely univocal (mutawati'). So the shared meaning does not imply absolute sameness of application.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the difference between the philosophers and other proponents of the univocity of existence?
A) Philosophers deny univocity
B) Philosophers hold it is gradational (mutashakkik), others hold it is univocal (mutawati')
C) Philosophers say existence is equivocal
D) Others deny the concept entirely

Key Takeaway
Existence is a shared concept, but philosophers view it as gradational while others view it as strictly univocal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the existence of a pebble, a plant, and your own soul, do you experience them as possessing the same 'existence' in different degrees (gradational) or exactly the same quality (univocal)? How does your intuitive sense align with the philosophers versus the Ash'aris, and what does that reveal about your own assumptions?
?
If existence is a single meaning shared by all things, including the Creator, does that not risk blurring the line between Creator and creation in your heart? How do you personally guard against a vague pantheism while still affirming that existence is one concept?
?
This passage says the debate over self-evidence depends on the sharing of existence. Have you ever built an argument on a foundation that you assumed was settled, only later to realize the foundation itself was contested? What in your faith life might be a 'shared' concept you have not critically examined?

Objection and Answer: The Rational Division of Existence
Commentator presents objection and answer concerning the rationality of the division of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against the rationality of the division of existence is answered by showing that non-existence is multiple.

Core Idea
The division remains rational because non-existence is as multiple as existence.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a critical objection: if the word 'existence' is equivocal (shared by multiple meanings), then the division of a thing into existent or non-existent seems to depend on linguistic coinage rather than reason. The answer reveals that non-existence is not a single concept but multiple, each tied to a specific existence, thereby preserving the rationality of the division.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is raised: the division of 'thing' into existent or non-existent is not rational because 'existence' is an equivocal term depending on coinage.
Notice that the objection challenges the foundation of the previous classification.
2
Understand the answer
The answer states that non-existence is multiple and distinguished by its relation to existence. Each type of non-existence corresponds to a specific existence, so the division remains rational.
This is the key move: multiplicity on both sides.
3
Examine al-Abhari's clarification
The commentator cites al-Abhari to show that a division based solely on coinage would be variable and absurd, but a division recognizing multiple non-existences is not.
Al-Abhari's point strengthens the answer.
4
Reflect on the extraneous premise
The passage ends with a note about an extraneous premise concerning hyle (matter). This is a subtle point; note that it does not invalidate the main answer.
This is an advanced nuance; don't get stuck on it.

ملاحظة أحد تلك المعاني ليس إلا باعتبار وضع لفظ الوجود لها، فللوضع مدخل في الحكم والحصر دائر معه ويختلف باختلافه . قوله : (لا نسلم أن العدم مفهوم إلخ) أي ليس لنا مفهوم واحد مسمى بالعدم إنما هي مفهومات ،متعددة، على حسب تعدد الوجودات ولفظ العدم مشترك بينهما اشتراكا لفظيا کالوجود . قوله : ( متعدد متمايز بحسب إضافته إلخ) والإضافة إلى الوجود داخلة في مفهومه، فيكون متمايزاً بالذات . قوله : ( والترديد إلخ) فقولنا : زيد إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً بمنزلة قولنا زيد إما إنسان أو ليس بإنسان . بمقدمة أجنبية هي امتناع وجود الشيء بوجود غيره، واعلم أن ادعاء الحصر في قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً بوجود خاص، أو لا يكون موجوداً أصلاً، ولو بواسطة مقدمة أجنبية يشكل بالهيولى فإنها قد تكون موجودة بوجود خاص تارة وبوجودين أخرى فتأمل جوابه. قوله : ( قلنا فحينئذ كان الحصر بملاحظة اللفظ الخ ) رد عليه بعض الأفاضل بأنه يجوز أن يكون الحصر بملاحظة إحدى تلك المعاني المختلفة من غير ملاحظة لفظ الوجود الذي يطلق عليها، فإن هذا المفهوم شامل للجميع وغير مناف للاشتراك اللفظي، وجوابه أن الكلام في التقسيم الذي وقع فيه التعبير بلفظ الوجود مراداً به معناه الحقيقي، إذ الكلام في الحصر المستفاد من قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً فلا إشكال أصلاً
The First Observation - The Second Purpose: That it is equivocal. A thing exists with a specific existence among existences, or it does not exist at all does not invalidate the division. We said: Then the division is by consideration of the word and its usages, so it is not rational but rather inductive, dependent on usage, varying according to its variation. And the answer: We do not accept that non-existence is a single concept; rather it is multiple, distinguished by its relation to existence. For if existence is the essence itself, then non-existence is the negation of the essence, and there is no doubt that essences are multiple, and each essence has a negation that corresponds to it. And the disjunction: His statement: (For if it is said, etc.) means that the rational division is only invalidated if by our saying 'existing' we mean a specific existence among multiple existences. But if we mean what the word existence is applied to, then no, because the meaning becomes: either existing with one of the existences or not existing at all, and there is no doubt of its delimitation. His statement: (Varying according to its variation.) It is transmitted from al-Abhari that this is the meaning of division: a thing is either existing with one of the meanings for which the word existence was coined, or not. And this changes if one supposes the word existence to be coined for fewer or more of those meanings, so that the state of the thing in being existent or non-existent changes merely by changing usages while it remains itself unchanged, and that is absolutely false, end. And by this is refuted what some virtuous person raised: that it is permissible for the division to be by consideration of one of those different meanings without consideration of the word existence that is applied to them, for this concept is inclusive of all and not contrary to verbal equivocality. Because on the hypothesis of verbal equivocality, considering one of those meanings is only by virtue of the coinage of the word existence for them, so coinage has a role in the judgment, and the division revolves with it and varies according to its variation. His statement: (We do not accept that non-existence is a concept, etc.) i.e., we do not have a single concept named 'non-existence'; rather they are multiple concepts according to the multiplicity of existences, and the word 'non-existence' is equivocal among them in the manner of verbal equivocality like existence. His statement: (Multiple distinguished by its relation, etc.) And the relation to existence enters into its concept, so it is essentially distinguished. His statement: (And the disjunction, etc.) So our saying: Zayd is either existent or non-existent is like our saying: Zayd is either a human or not a human. With an extraneous premise: the impossibility of a thing existing by the existence of another. And know that claiming the division in our saying: a thing is either existing with a specific existence or not existing at all, even via an extraneous premise, is problematic concerning hyle (matter), for it may exist with a specific existence sometimes and with two existences another time, so ponder its answer. His statement: (We said then the division is by consideration of the word, etc.) Some virtuous ones objected that it is permissible for the division to be by consideration of one of those different meanings without considering the word existence applied to them, for this concept includes all and is not contrary to verbal equivocality. And its answer: The discussion is about the division in which the expression by the word existence occurred, intended by its real meaning, for the discussion is about the division derived from our saying: a thing is either existent or non-existent. So there is no problem at all.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit concerning the claim that the division (hasr) of 'thing' into existent (mawjud) and non-existent (ma'dum) is not a rational (aqli) division but a linguistic one. The author (the commentator) presents the objection: if existence (wujud) is a term used for multiple distinct realities (i.e., it is equivocal/mushtarak), then the statement 'a thing is either existent or non-existent' does not yield a rational division, because the meaning of 'existent' would be different in each case, so the division depends on the coinage (wad') of the word. The answer is that we do not accept that non-existence ('adam) is a single concept; rather it is multiple, each defined by its relation to a specific existence. If existence is the essence itself, then non-existence is the negation (raf') of that essence, and since essences are multiple, negations are multiple. Therefore, the division between a specific existence and its corresponding non-existence is still a genuine rational division. The commentator then cites al-Abhari to clarify that the division based on the coinage of the word would be variable and absurd. He also refutes an objection by some virtuous person who claimed that the division could be based on the meanings themselves without regard to the word. The commentator insists that the discussion concerns the division as expressed by the word existence in its real meaning, so no problem arises.

Key Terms

عقلي
موجود
معدوم
وجود
عدم
اشتراك لفظي
وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is equivocal, the division into existent/non-existent cannot be rational.
Correction: The division remains rational because non-existence is also multiple, each corresponding to a specific existence.

Checkpoint
What is the main response to the objection that the division of 'thing' into existent and non-existent is not rational because 'existence' is equivocal?
A) The division is only linguistic and not rational at all.
B) Non-existence is also multiple, so each specific existence has a corresponding non-existence, preserving rationality.
C) The word 'existence' is not equivocal but univocal.
D) The division is inductive and varies with usage.

Key Takeaway
The division of things into existent and non-existent is rational because non-existence is as multiple as existence, ensuring each specific existence has a corresponding non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you think of your 'non-existence before birth' as a single obscure nothingness, or as a specific negation of your person? How does the precision of this division affect your gratitude for being brought into existence exactly as you are?
?
If non-existence is multiple, then the annihilation of a loved one is not just a generic 'ceasing to be' but the removal of a unique reality. How does this truth confront the way you value each individual's existence before Allah?
?
The passage argues that a division based on words alone would be changeable and absurd. Reflect on a time when you reduced a matter of faith to mere terminology. Are there areas of your belief where you rely on linguistic habits rather than rational certainty? What will you do to anchor your convictions in reality?

The three schools on existence and essence
Classifies the three schools on existence and essence with the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical possibilities regarding whether existence is identical to essence or additional, and the first proof of the school that holds existence is the reality itself.

Core Idea
Existence may be essence itself or additional — three schools, not four.

This passage classifies the possible positions on the relationship between existence (wujud) and essence (mahiyya). The author enumerates the logical possibilities and identifies the three historically held schools, then presents the first proof for one of them. Understanding this classification is key to later debates on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the logical possibilities and the three schools. Note that the author starts with the possibilities and then narrows them down.
The passage includes a gloss that clarifies the usage of terms like 'existence' and 'or'.
2
Identify the three schools
List the three schools: (1) existence is the essence in all beings (al-Ash'ari and Abu al-Husayn), (2) existence is additional in all beings, (3) existence is essence in the Necessary and additional in contingent. Note that the reverse (essence in contingent, additional in Necessary) is not held.
The first school is given a proof; the other schools are not elaborated here.
3
Examine the first proof
Read the first proof: if existence were additional to essence, the essence would be non-existent in itself. This shows that existence cannot be something added; it must be identical to the essence for things to be real.
This proof relies on the idea that what is additional to something else does not make that thing existent by itself.
4
Reflect on the rejected possibility
Consider why the fourth possibility (existence is essence in contingent, additional in Necessary) was never advocated. Think about how it would conflict with the nature of the Necessary Being.
This reflection helps understand the logical constraints on these positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها
في أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها أو زائد عليها وفيه مذاهب ثلاثة لأنه إذ لم يقل أحد بأن الوجود جزء الماهية فإما أن يكون نفس الماهية في الكل أي الواجب والممكن جميعاً أو زائداً عليها في الكل، أو يكون نفس الماهية في الواجب زائداً عليها في الممكن أو بالعكس وهذا الاحتمال الأخير لم يقل به أحد، فانحصرت المذاهب في ثلاثة أحدها للشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري وأبي الحسين البصري من المعتزلة أنه نفس الحقيقة في الكل أي الواجب والممكنات كافة لوجوه ثلاثة الأول لو كان الوجود زائداً على الماهية كانت الماهية من حيث هي هي غير موجودة
قوله : ( فإن الوجود إلخ) أي ما يطلق عليه الوجود لا الوجود المطلق، فيشمل مذهب الأشعري أيضاً ولذا وضع المظهر موضع المضمر. قوله : (نفس الماهية أو جزؤها إلخ) كلمة أو ليس للتقسيم ولا للترديد إذ لا مذهب في انقسامه وترديده، للعقليين بل هو لأحد الأمرين على ما هو أصل وضعه فهو قضية حملية محمولها أحد الأمور الثلاثة عند العقل وفي هذه القضية التي محمولها أحد الأمور الثلاثة عند العقل على سبيل منع الخلو مذاهب ثلاثة باعتبار تعيين محمولها.
The First Observatory – Third Purpose: That existence is the essence or part of it.
Concerning whether existence is the essence or part of it or additional to it, and there are three schools. Because, since no one said that existence is part of the essence, then either it is the essence in all—i.e., both the Necessary and the contingent—or additional to all, or it is the essence in the Necessary and additional to the contingent, or the opposite. This last possibility no one held, so the schools are limited to three. One is that of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri of the Mu'tazila: that it is the same reality in all, i.e., the Necessary and all contingents, for three proofs. The first: if existence were additional to the essence, then the essence in itself would be non-existent.
His saying: 'Indeed existence etc.' means that which existence is applied to, not absolute existence, so it includes the school of al-Ash'ari as well, and thus the manifest is placed in the position of the pronoun. His saying: 'The essence or part of it etc.' The word 'or' is not for division nor for alternation, since there is no school of its division and alternation for theologians; rather it is for one of the two matters according to its original usage. So it is a predicative proposition whose predicate is one of three things according to the intellect, and in this proposition whose predicate is one of three things according to the intellect, by way of preventing emptiness, there are three schools based on specifying its predicate.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage classifies the possible positions on whether existence (wujud) is identical to essence (mahiyya) or additional to it. The author first notes that no one holds that existence is part of the essence, leaving four logical possibilities: existence is the essence in all beings (both Necessary and contingent), additional in all, essence in the Necessary and additional in the contingent, or the reverse. The fourth position is not held, so there are three schools. The first school, attributed to al-Ash'ari and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, holds that existence is the reality itself in everything. Their first proof is that if existence were additional to essence, the essence would be non-existent in itself. The gloss then clarifies that 'existence' here means what is called existence, not absolute existence, and the word 'or' does not indicate division or alternation but is used in its original sense to pick out one of the three possibilities. The three schools arise from specifying which of the three possible predicates (essence in all, additional in all, or mixed) is the correct one.

Key Terms
نفس الماهية
زائد على الماهية
جزء الماهية
مذاهب ثلاثة
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think there are four schools on the existence-essence relation, with the fourth being a possible view.
Correction: The fourth logical possibility (existence is essence in contingent, additional in Necessary) was never advocated, so only three schools exist.

Checkpoint
How many main schools does the author identify regarding whether existence is identical to essence or additional?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The three schools on existence and essence reduce to existence as essence in all, additional in all, or essence in the Necessary and additional in contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is additional to your essence, then in yourself you are nothing. Do you live as though your existence is a gift from the Necessary Being, or do you take it for granted? How would your day change if you truly felt your non-existence without Him?
?
The reverse possibility—existence as essence in contingent and additional in Necessary—was never held. Does your heart naturally acknowledge that God alone is existence itself, while you receive existence? When was the last time you thanked God for the sheer fact that you exist?
?
The first proof says that if existence were additional, essence would be non-existent. This means you cannot even conceive of yourself without existence. Are you humbled by this dependency, or do you still think you are self-sufficient? What concrete action can you take today to remember that your existence is maintained by God every moment?

What Is Kalam?
beginner

Existence: Positive but Not Extra
The author states the thesis and clarifies that existence is positive but non-extra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence is considered a positive (thubuti) attribute but not an extra entity separate from essence.

Core Idea
Existence is positive in concept but not an extra thing in the world.

This passage tackles a central debate in kalam: Is existence a mere concept or something extra added to essence? The author states that existence is indeed a positive attribute, but its distinction from essence is only mental, not external. Before diving into proofs, we need to clarify what is meant by 'positive' and 'distinction'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the compressed style and the key terms: wujud, mahiyya, thubuti.
The passage begins by stating the thesis about existence being identical to essence or part of it.
2
Identify the three key claims
The passage makes three claims: (1) existence is a positive attribute, (2) its distinction from the subject is purely intellectual, (3) 'thubuti' does not mean external existence but only that negation is not part of its concept.
The author uses 'n'am' (yes) to introduce the clarification.
3
Understand the necessary judgment
The author refers to a 'necessary judgment' that what is positive exists externally. He argues existence does not fall under this judgment because 'thubuti' here is defined differently.
This prevents a misunderstanding that could lead to affirming an extra entity.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on why this distinction matters: it allows us to affirm that existence is real (not a mere concept) without making it a separate thing added to essence.
This is foundational for debates on the relationship between essence and existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها
صفة موجودة في الخارج بل امتيازه عن معروضه إنما هو في العقل وحده، نعم هو ثبوتي بمعنى أنه ليس السلب داخلا في مفهومه لا بمعنى أنه موجود في الخارج، فلا يكون مندرجاً في ذلك الحكم الضروري.
The First Observatory - The Third Purpose: That existence is identical to essence or a part of it. [It is] an attribute existing externally, but its distinction from its subject is only in the intellect alone. Yes, it is positive (thubuti) in the sense that negation is not included in its concept, not in the sense that it exists externally, so it is not included in that necessary judgment.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage defines the thesis under discussion: whether existence (wujud) is identical to essence (mahiyya) or a part of it. The author first clarifies that although existence is an attribute that has some reality, its distinction from the subject (the essence) is only a mental distinction, not an external one. He then explains that existence is 'thubuti' (positive) meaning its concept does not include negation; but this does not imply it exists externally as an extra entity. Therefore, the necessary judgment that 'whatever is positive exists externally' does not apply to existence itself.

Key Terms
وجود
ماهية
ثبوتي
امتياز عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'thubuti' (positive) necessarily implies that something exists as a separate entity in the external world.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'thubuti' in this context means only that the concept does not include negation, not that it exists externally as an extra thing.

Checkpoint
What does 'thubuti' mean in this passage?
A) That existence is a separate entity in the external world.
B) That the concept of existence does not include negation.
C) That existence is identical to essence.
D) That existence is a part of essence.

Key Takeaway
Existence is positive because its concept lacks negation, but it does not exist as an extra entity—its distinction from essence is purely mental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you ever imagine your 'essence' as something separate from the fact that you exist? How does this lesson challenge that habit of thought?
?
If existence is not an extra thing added to you, then what does it mean for you to 'be'? How should this reorient your gratitude to the One who grants you existence moment by moment?
?
This lesson warns against confusing a mental distinction with an external separation. In your daily life, where might you be treating a purely conceptual difference as if it were a real gap, especially in your understanding of yourself or your relationship with Allah?

Third Argument: Infinite Regress and the Answer of Denial
The author presents the third proof and its answer: denial of the premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the identity of existence and essence—that non-identity leads to infinite regress—and the response that the premise is not necessary.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is avoided by denying that existence must exist.

This passage presents the third proof for the identity of existence (wujūd) and essence (māhiyya). The argument is that if existence were extra to essence or a part of it, then existence itself would need another existence, leading to an infinite chain. The answer is a simple denial of the premise. The commentator explains the reasoning and clarifies the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument in Arabic
Read the first sentence of the passage: it presents the conditional and the conclusion. Notice the logical flow: if P, then Q leads to infinite regress.
The answer 'al-man'' is given immediately.
2
Parse the major premise
The commentator breaks down the major premise into two judgments: existence must exist, and its existence is other than itself. Follow this reasoning.
3
Understand the answer (al-man')
The answer is a denial of the necessity that existence would exist if it were extra or a part. It could be extra and non-existent.
This is a valid response in kalam: denying the premise without proof.
4
Consider the objection and its dismissal
The commentator mentions an objection about the part-whole relation, but dismisses it. See why the objection is irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث لو كان الوجود زائداً على الماهية أو جزءاً منها لكان له وجود آخر لامتناع اتصافه بالعدم الذي هو نقيضه، وحينئذ ننقل الكلام إلى وجود الوجود و تتسلسل الوجودات إلى ما لا يتناهى والجواب المنع
قوله : (لو كان الوجود إلخ) تقريره أنه لو لم يكن الوجود نفس الماهية لكان زائداً عليها أو جزءاً منها، وكلما كان أحدهما كان له وجود آخر أي موجوداً بوجود مغاير لنفسه زائد عليه، أو جزء منه أما الصغرى فظاهر، وأما الكبرى فيتضمن حكمين أحدهما كونه موجوداً، وذلك لامتناع اتصافه بنقيضه وثانيهما كون وجوده مغاير لنفسه. أما زائداً عليه أو جزءاً منه، وذلك لأن المفروض أن الوجود زائد على الماهية أو جزء منها في الموجودات والوجود من جملتها، ولا يخفى أن هذا الدليل يدل على عدم كونه زائداً أو جزءاً في الكل فلا يثبت به المدعى، أعني العينية في الكل وهذا الاعتراض غير الجواب الذي يأتي لأنه على تقدير تسليم تمامية الدليل والجواب المذكور منع لكون الوجود موجوداً أو كون وجوده مغايراً له .
قوله : ( والجواب إلخ) تقريره لا نسلم أنه لو كان الوجود زائداً أو جزءاً كان موجوداً، لجواز أن يكون زائداً ومعدوماً، وما توهم من أنه لا يمكن تجويز كونه معدوماً على تقدير الجزئية لأن عدم الجزء يستلزم عدم الكل فيلزم أن لا تكون الماهية موجودة، فليس بشيء لأن المستدل ادعى لزوم كونه موجودا على كل واحد من التقديرين أعني الزيادة والجزئية، كما عرفت فالمانع
The third argument: If existence were extra to essence or a part of it, then it would have another existence because it is impossible for it to be described by non-existence which is its opposite; then we transfer the discussion to the existence of existence, and existences would regress infinitely. The answer: denial [of the premise]. His saying: (If existence etc.) Its exposition: If existence were not the same as essence, it would be extra to it or a part of it, and in either case it would have another existence, i.e., it would exist by an existence other than itself, either extra to it or a part of it. As for the minor premise, it is obvious; as for the major premise, it includes two judgments: one is that it exists, because it is impossible for it to be described by its opposite, and the second is that its existence is other than itself, either extra to it or a part of it, because it is assumed that existence is extra to essence or a part of it in existents, and existence is among them. It is not hidden that this argument shows that it is not extra or a part in all cases, so it does not establish the claim, i.e., identity in all cases. This objection is not the answer that comes, because even if the argument is granted, the mentioned answer is a denial that existence exists or that its existence is other than itself. His saying: (And the answer etc.) Its exposition: We do not concede that if existence were extra or a part it would exist, because it is possible that it be extra and non-existent. And what is imagined that it cannot be considered non-existent on the assumption of being a part because the non-existence of the part necessitates the non-existence of the whole, so the essence would not exist, is nothing; because the arguer claimed that it must exist on both assumptions, i.e., extra and part, as you know, so the denier [can simply deny].
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the identity of existence and essence, followed by its answer. The argument: If existence were extra or a part of essence, then existence itself would need another existence (since it cannot be non-existent), leading to an infinite regress. The answer is simply denial (man'): we do not grant that existence would have to exist if it were extra or a part; it could be extra and non-existent. The commentator then elaborates: the arguer claimed that existence must exist on both assumptions, but the denier is not required to prove why; he can just deny the necessity. The commentator also discusses an objection that if existence were a part, its non-existence would entail the non-existence of the whole (essence), but this is irrelevant because the arguer's claim is that existence must exist, not that if it were a part it would be non-existent.

Key Terms
وجود
ماهية
زيادة
جزئية
تسلسل
منع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'denial' (man') is a weak or evasive answer in theological arguments.
Correction: In kalam, denying a premise is a valid response when the opponent's proof is not demonstrative; the burden of proof remains on the arguer.

Checkpoint
What is the main answer (al-jawab) to the third argument?
A) That existence cannot be a part of essence.
B) Denying that existence would necessarily exist if extra or a part.
C) Accepting infinite regress.
D) That existence is identical to essence for necessary beings only.

Key Takeaway
The third argument is neutralized by denying that existence must exist if it is extra or a part of essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical argument that seems airtight, are you willing to examine and deny its foundational assumptions, or do you feel compelled to accept the conclusion out of discomfort with uncertainty? What is one assumption in your own belief system that you have never questioned, and will you question it now?
?
The arguer assumed that existence must exist if it is extra or a part. The answer was simply 'denial'. In your own life, do you tend to accept plausible-sounding premises without scrutiny, especially when they come from authority or tradition? Can you identify one premise about your faith that you have always taken for granted but never critically examined? What would it mean for you to honestly deny that premise?
?
This lesson shows that a seemingly powerful infinite regress argument is neutralized by denying a single premise. What seemingly 'irrefutable' argument against your faith have you encountered that might actually rest on an unstated assumption you have never challenged? Are you willing to identify and deny that assumption, even if it makes you uncomfortable?

What Is Kalam?

Existence as a Secondary Intelligible: Blocking the Regress
The author denies the implication by positing existence as a secondary intelligible and distinguishing types of predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the claim that existence is a secondary intelligible prevents the infinite regress objection against the extra-mentality of existence.

Core Idea
If existence is a secondary intelligible, it need not exist externally, stopping the regress.

In this passage, the commentator responds to a key objection: if existence is extra to essence, then existence would need another existence ad infinitum. The answer is twofold. First, existence may be a secondary intelligible—a concept that has mental reality but no external existence—so it does not require an external existence. Second, even if we grant that existence itself exists, that existence could be identical to itself. This lesson focuses on the first part and the crucial distinction between derivational and essential predication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: the author first denies the implication, then explains why.
The passage uses parentheses for nested conditions.
2
Understand secondary intelligibles
Secondary intelligibles are concepts that exist only in the mind, like universals. Here, existence is proposed as one. Why would that block the regress?
If existence is not externally existent, it cannot be required to have another existence.
3
Distinguish ishtiqaq from muwata'a
The author says a thing can be described by its opposite derivatively but not essentially. This is a technical distinction in predication. A black thing is 'black' essentially, but a non-existent thing can be 'existent' derivatively if it is mentally conceived.
This shows that 'existence' as a concept can be non-existent externally yet still be used in predication.
4
See the conditional concession
The author then concedes that even if existence has an existence, that existence may be identical to itself, not extra. This anticipates the next lesson.
This is a second line of defense.

That is, we do not concede the implication, because existence may be among the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya), so it would not be existent but non-existent, and there is no impossibility in a thing being described by its opposite derivatively; the impossible is only that it be described by it essentially as has passed. And if it is granted that existence has an existence on that assumption, then the existence of existence may be itself, not extra to it nor a part of it. And similarly we say: the eternity of eternity itself, and the temporal origination of temporal origination itself, on the assumption that eternity and temporal origination exist externally, and similarly their likes, i.e., the likes of what was mentioned like the necessity of necessity and the possibility of possibility and other recurring types that will be mentioned later. For every attribute that attaches to another is extra to that other, but its subsistence for itself is not extra to itself. So we say, for example: every concept that is other than eternity is not eternal except by the addition of another thing, namely the concept of eternity; but as for the concept of eternity, on the assumption of its existence, it is eternal by itself, not by something extra to it that joins it. Similarly, essence is existent by an existence extra to it, while existence is existent by itself, not by something... That suffices him to say: we do not concede that if it were either [extra or part] it would exist, because it is possible that it be extra and non-existent, and he is not required to show the non-necessity for each one separately. His saying: (And no impossibility etc.) Because the denial of the demonstrated premise is not valid; he indicated that its denial goes back to the denial of its proof. By itself, not in the sense... His saying: (And if it is granted etc.) That is, if its existence is granted on the assumption of extra or part, then we do not concede that it exists by another existence, because that judgment applies only to what is other than existence; as for existence, it may be that the existence of existence is united with existence, for the union of attribute with subject is not impossible. Rather, the result that is attached to other existences by virtue of existence subsisting in them is attached to existence itself without another existence subsisting in it. For the existent with us is what manifests judgments and entails effects, not what is described by existence as is the linguistic convention; otherwise, the dispute about whether it is identical to essence or extra would be futile speech. His saying: (For every etc.) is a justification for his saying: then the existence of existence may be itself, meaning this possibility is based on a true universal premise. It was said: this universal implies that blackness is black by itself, but intuition denies it because blackness is blackness, not black; this is nothing, because if he means that it is not described by blackness, then it is granted but does not harm us; if he means that it does not entail the effects of blackness, then it is denied. His saying: (Then the existence of existence may be itself, and as for what is imagined that the existence of existence may be non-existent, [we say] individuals of a single nature are not necessarily all existent, as will come, so infinite regress does not follow, so there is no basis for it here, because the mentioned argument, if sound, indicates the existence of all individuals of existence, as is not hidden. His saying: (And as for existence, it is existent by itself; then if it is said: then every existence would be necessary, for there is no meaning to it...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the answer to the third argument by providing a deeper analysis. First, it states that existence may be a secondary intelligible (ma'qulat thaniya)—a concept that does not exist externally but is mentally abstracted—so it can be non-existent externally. The impossibility of a thing being described by its opposite applies only to essential (muwata'a) predication, not derivative (ishtiqaq) predication. Second, even if we grant that existence has an existence, that existence could be identical to itself, not extra. This is justified by a universal principle: any attribute that attaches to another is extra to that other, but its subsistence for itself is not extra. Examples: eternity's eternity is itself, temporal origination's temporal origination is itself. If a concept is other than eternity, it needs eternity to be eternal, but the concept of eternity itself is eternal by itself. Similarly, essence needs existence to be existent, but existence itself is existent by itself. The passage then addresses an objection: if existence is existent by itself, then every existence would be necessary (wajib). The response is cut off, but the argument likely denies this by distinguishing between necessary and contingent existence. The commentator also defends the universal premise against the counterexample of blackness not being black; he clarifies that blackness is not described by blackness but entails its effects.

Key Terms
معقولات ثانية
اشتقاق
مواطأة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if existence is a secondary intelligible, it is completely unreal and cannot be predicated of anything.
Correction: Secondary intelligibles have mental reality; they are abstracted from existing things. The passage clarifies that predication can be derivative, so existence can be a concept used in statements without being externally existent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is there no impossibility in existence being non-existent externally while being a secondary intelligible?
A) Because existence is a necessary being.
B) Because a thing can be described by its opposite derivatively, not essentially.
C) Because existence is a part of essence.
D) Because the infinite regress is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Existence as a secondary intelligible does not require external existence, so the infinite regress objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you treated a concept (like 'existence' or 'attribute') as a real, external thing, only to find yourself trapped in unnecessary doubts about Allah’s attributes? What would it cost you to admit that some concepts are only tools of thought, not realities outside your mind?
?
If existence can be a secondary intelligible with no extramental reality, what does that mean for your habit of demanding 'proof by existence' for everything in your theology? Are you willing to let go of that demand and trust the conceptual framework of the scholars, even when it feels abstract?
?
The author distinguishes between derivative and essential predication to solve a logical puzzle. How often do you confuse the way you describe something (e.g., 'God is merciful') with the reality of that attribute? Is your faith anchored in the essential meaning of such descriptions, or do you slip into treating them as mere mental labels?

The Self-Subsistence of Attributes: Eternity and Existence
The author states the universal principle and illustrates with examples of eternity, temporal origination, and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that an attribute's subsistence for itself is not extra, illustrated by eternity and applied to existence.

Core Idea
An attribute that attaches to others is extra, but its subsistence for itself is not.

After the initial defense, the commentator expands the answer by introducing a universal principle: any attribute that attaches to another is extra to that other, but its own subsistence is not extra. This principle is illustrated with examples: eternity's eternity is itself, temporal origination's origination is itself. The culmination is that existence itself is existent by itself, not by another existence. This lesson unpacks these examples and the underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the pattern: the author gives examples of attributes (eternity, origination, necessity, possibility) and then states the general principle.
The examples are symmetric: each attribute's own concept is itself.
2
Identify the universal principle
The key sentence is: 'For every attribute that attaches to another is extra to that other, but its subsistence for itself is not extra to itself.' This is the core logical claim.
This principle is used to justify that the existence of existence is itself.
3
Apply to the examples
The passage explains that for anything other than eternity, eternity must be added. But the concept of eternity itself is eternal by itself. Similarly, essence needs existence, but existence itself is existent by itself.
This parallels the earlier argument about secondary intelligibles.
4
Note the analogy with attributes
The analogy shows that the attribute's own reality does not require another attribute of the same kind. This refutes the infinite regress.
The discussion of blackness in the commentary further clarifies the principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Dispute is About Real Difference Not Conceptual
The author distinguishes conceptual from real difference in the existence-essence debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise point of contention in the existence-essence debate: it is about real, extramental difference, not merely conceptual distinction.

Core Idea
The debate is about reality, not concepts: existence and essence are identical in the external world.

This passage clarifies the exact subject of the dispute over whether existence is identical to essence or additional. The author argues that arguments for addition only prove a conceptual difference, but everyone agrees concepts differ; the real question is whether the external realities are distinct. By focusing the debate on extramental identity, the author sets up the case for the doctrine of identity (al-ʿayniyya).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the parentheses; they are part of the commentary structure.
2
Identify the two types of difference
Find the terms تغاير المفهومين (conceptual difference) and تغاير الذاتين (essential/real difference). Notice how the author contrasts them.
The author says the proofs only show the former, but the dispute is about the latter.
3
Understand the example of blackness and existence
The author uses blackness as an example. Conceptual difference is obvious: the concept of blackness is not the concept of existence. But what blackness applies to externally is exactly what existence applies to. So there are not two separate identities like blackness and body.
This illustrates real identity despite conceptual difference.
4
Recognize the author's claim and the investigation
The author states that this is the truth, and then introduces the investigation (tahqiq) which will give preponderance to the doctrine of identity (al-ʿayniyya).
The tahqiq is the section that follows, arguing for identity.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها أن هذه الوجوه التي استدل بها على كون الوجود زائداً على ماهية الممكن (إنما تفيد تغاير (المفهومين أي مفهوم الوجود ومفهوم السواد مثلا ( دون ( تغاير ( الذاتين ) أي ذات الوجود وذات السواد مثلا ) والنزاع إنما وقع فيه ) أي في تغاير الذاتين لا في تغاير المفهومين ) فإن عاقلا لا يقول : مفهوم السواد هو بعينه مفهوم الوجود بل ( يقول العاقل إن ( ما صدق عليه السواد ) من الأمور الخارجية هو بعينه ما صدق عليه الوجود وليس لهما ) أي للوجود والسواد ( هويتان متمايزتان ( في الخارج ) تقوم إحداهما بالأخرى كالسواد القائم ( بالجسم ) فإن للسواد هوية ممتازة عن هوية الجسم بحسب الخارج، وقد قامت الأولى بالثانية (و) ما ذكره من أن ما صدق عليه أحدهما هو عين ما صدق عليه الآخر، وأنه ليس لهما هويتان متمايزتان ( هو الحق) المطابق للواقع قوله : ( والتحقيق) أي بيان الحق من قولي الزيادة والعينية بعد الإحاطة بدلائل الطرفين والمقصود منه ترجيح مذهب العينية، وخلاصته أن التغاير من حيث المفهوم لا يقبل النزاع فلا يمكن حمل الاختلاف عليه فالاختلاف والنزاع إنما هو في التغاير من حيث الذات، والحق في ذلك مذهب الشيخ لدليل لاح له.
The First Observatory - The Third Objective: That existence is the same as essence or a part of it. That these aspects which are adduced to prove that existence is additional to the essence of the possible (only indicate the difference of (the two concepts, i.e., the concept of existence and the concept of blackness, for example) without (the difference of (the two essences, i.e., the essence of existence and the essence of blackness, for example). And the dispute is only about (i.e., about the difference of the two essences, not about the difference of the two concepts), for no rational person says: the concept of blackness is exactly the concept of existence; rather (the rational person says that (what blackness applies to) from external things is exactly what existence applies to, and they do not have (i.e., existence and blackness) (two distinct identities (in the external world) one of which subsists by the other, like blackness subsisting (in the body), for blackness has an identity distinct from the identity of the body in terms of the external, and the first subsists by the second). And what he mentioned that what one of them applies to is identical to what the other applies to, and that they do not have two distinct identities, (is the truth) corresponding to reality. His statement: (And the investigation) i.e., the exposition of the truth between the two statements of addition and identity after encompassing the proofs of both sides, and its purpose is to give preponderance to the doctrine of identity, and its summary is that difference in terms of concept is not open to dispute, so disagreement cannot be placed upon it; rather disagreement and dispute are only about difference in terms of essence, and the truth in that is the doctrine of the Sheikh due to a proof that is clear to him.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage defines the precise point of contention in the debate over whether existence is identical to essence or additional to it. The author first clarifies that the arguments for addition only prove a difference between the two concepts (e.g., the concept of existence vs. the concept of blackness), but the real dispute is about whether the two realities (the essence of existence and the essence of blackness) are distinct in the external world. No one denies that the concepts are different; the intelligent person asserts that what blackness applies to externally is exactly what existence applies to, so there are not two separate identities like blackness and body. The author then states that this is the truth and introduces the investigation (al-taḥqīq) which aims to establish the doctrine of identity (al-ʿayniyya). The summary is that conceptual difference is uncontroversial, so the disagreement must be about real, extramental difference, and the correct position is that of al-Ashʿarī.

تغاير الذاتين
الهويتان
مذهب العينية
التحقيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate about existence and essence is about whether their concepts differ.
Correction: The passage insists that conceptual difference is agreed upon; the real dispute is about whether the external realities (essences) are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the real subject of dispute regarding existence and essence?
A) Whether the concepts of existence and essence are different.
B) Whether the external realities of existence and essence are distinct.
C) Whether existence is a property of essence.
D) Whether essence can exist without existence.

Key Takeaway
The existence-essence debate is about whether their external realities are distinct, not about whether their concepts differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage says no rational person confuses the concept of existence with the concept of blackness—yet we often live as if our concepts of faith are enough. Where in your life have you settled for a conceptual understanding of truth instead of striving for a lived, existential realization that transforms you?

Mental Existence and Its Distinction from External Existence
Definition of mental existence and its distinction from external existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what mental existence is, how it differs from external existence, and why the distinction hinges on specificity of effects.

Core Idea
Mental existence is a shadowy existence that produces only conceptual effects, not the specific effects of external existence.

This lesson introduces the concept of mental existence, a key topic in Islamic philosophy. The author begins by affirming the indisputable reality of external existence, then raises the question of whether things also have a mental existence. The passage defines mental existence and clarifies that its distinguishing feature is the absence of the specific effects that belong to external existence. It then addresses several objections to this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definition
Read the passage from the beginning to the point where mental existence is defined as 'shadowy'. Notice how the author uses fire as an example to illustrate concrete existence.
The passage contrasts concrete existence with mental existence.
2
Distinguish by effects
Identify the key difference: mental existence does not have the specific judgments and effects of external existence. The commentary explains that judgments are non-agent aspects and effects are agent aspects.
This distinction is crucial for the rest of the argument.
3
Examine the objections
The text presents objections that mental existence also has effects like universality. Read how the author responds by noting that these effects are not specific to any one thing.
The response uses the concept of 'specificity' (اختصاص).
4
Understand the final conclusion
The passage concludes that all specific effects belong to external existence alone, thus maintaining the distinction.
This resolves the confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الرابع: في الوجود الذهني لا شبهة في أن النار مثلاً لها وجود به تظهر عنها أحكامها ، وتصدر عنها آثارها من الإضاءة والإحراق ،وغيرهما، وهذا الوجود يسمى وجوداً عينياً وخارجياً، وأصيلاً وهذا مما لا نزاع فيه، إنما النزاع في أن النار هل لها سوى ذلك الوجود وجود آخر، لا يترتب به عليها تلك الأحكام والآثار أولا ، وهذا الوجود الآخر يسمى وجوداً ذهنياً، وظلياً طبيعة الوجود وإن كانت نوعية لجواز أن يكون من لوازم أفرادها لأن التجرد، والقيام بالذات متقدم على التشخص، فلا يجوز أن يكون معللاً به. قوله : (بل هو أمر عارض إلخ فلاختلافها بالحقيقة يجوز أن يقتضي بعضها الزيادة، وبعضها التجرد . قوله : ( أحكامها إلخ) أي الأحكام المعلومة ثبوتها لها والآثار المطلوبة منها لكل أحد، كما يشير إليه قوله لا شبهة وقوله : وهذا مما لا نزاع فيه والبيان بقوله : من الإضاءة والإحراق وفي قوله يظهر ويصدر إشارة إلى أن المراد بالأحكام ما لا يكون فاعلاً له ، وبالآثار ما يكون فاعلاً له . قوله : ( عينياً ) أي منسوباً إلى نفس الشيء لأنه وجود للشيء في نفسه بخلاف الذهني، فإنه وجود لصورته وقوله أصيلاً أي ذا أصل وعرق وليس ظلاً وحكاية عن شيء. قوله : ( في أن النار لا يتوهمن من ذكر النار أن النزاع في الوجود الذهني للموجودات الخارجية فإنه لمجرد التصوير. قوله : (تلك الأحكام والآثار سواء ترتب عليه أحكام وآثار أخر ، أولا وبما حررنا لك في بيان معنى الوجود الخارجي والذهني اندفع ما قيل : إن أريد الآثار الخارجية لزم الدور، وإن أريد الأعم دخل فيه الوجود الذهني فإنه أيضاً مبدأ للمعقولات الثانية، ولا يحتاج إلى ما قيل: من أنه لا أحكام ولا آثار للوجود الذهني والمعقولات الثانية آثار للصور الشخصية القائمة بالذهن، وهي من الموجودات الخارجية ولا إلى ما قيل من أن المراد كونه فاعلاً للآثار والموجود الذهني، ليس بفاعل ولا إلى ما قيل المراد الآثار المختصة والآثار الذهنية مشتركة بين الموجودات الذهنية، ماله قوله : ( تظهر عنها أحكامها وتصدر عنها آثارها المراد بأحكام النار وآثارها جميع اختصاص بها، فاندفع ما يقال : الفرق بين الوجودين بما ذكره غير واضح، إذ كما يترتب على الوجود العيني آثار وأحكام، كذلك يترتب على الوجود الظلي مثل الكلية والجزئية، والجنسية والفصلية ونحوها بل : بعض ما يترتب على الوجود الخارجي يترتب على الوجود الذهني، كلوازم الماهية ووجه الاندفاع أن العوارض الذهنية ليس لها اختصاص بماهية واحدة، بل كل منها شامل لماهيات كثيرة لا يعد في العرف من خواص واحد منها وأما حديث لوازم الماهية فاندفع بقيد الجميع، إذ بعض الآثار وإن ترتب على الوجود الذهني، وهو لوازم الماهية فجميعها لا يترتب إلا على الوجود الخارجي.
The first observatory - the fourth objective: On mental existence. There is no doubt that fire, for example, has an existence by which its judgments appear and its effects issue, such as illumination and burning and others, and this existence is called concrete and external and original, and this is indisputable. The dispute is whether fire, besides that existence, has another existence by which those judgments and effects do not follow, and this other existence is called mental and shadowy. The nature of existence, even if specific, is such that it may be among the concomitants of its individuals, because abstraction and self-subsistence are prior to individuation, so it cannot be caused by it. For saying: (But it is an accidental matter etc.): due to the difference in reality, it is possible that some require increase and others abstraction. For saying: (Its judgments etc.): i.e., the judgments known to be established for it and the effects desired from it for everyone, as indicated by his saying 'no doubt' and 'this is indisputable', and the explanation by his saying 'from illumination and burning', and in his saying 'appear and issue' there is an indication that what is meant by judgments is what is not an agent for it, and by effects what is an agent for it. For saying: (Concrete): i.e., attributed to the thing itself because it is the existence of the thing in itself, unlike mental existence, for it is the existence of its form. And his saying 'original' i.e., having a root and origin, not a shadow and representation of something. For saying: (Concerning fire): let no one imagine from the mention of fire that the dispute is about mental existence of external existences, for it is merely for illustration. For saying: (Those judgments and effects, whether other judgments and effects follow from it or not. And by what we have set down for you in explaining the meaning of external and mental existence, what has been said is refuted: if external effects are intended, a circle occurs; if general is intended, mental existence enters, for it is also a source of secondary intelligibles. And there is no need for what has been said: that there are no judgments or effects for mental existence, and secondary intelligibles are effects of personal forms subsisting in the mind, and they are from external existences; nor for what has been said: that the meaning is that it is an agent of effects, while mental existence is not an agent; nor for what has been said: that what is meant is specific effects, while mental effects are common among mental existences. For saying: (Its judgments appear and its effects issue): what is meant by the judgments and effects of fire is all that is specific to it, so what is said is refuted: that the distinction between the two existences by what is mentioned is not clear, since just as effects and judgments follow from concrete existence, so also from shadowy existence follow such as universality, particularity, genericity, specific difference, etc. Rather, some of what follows from external existence follows from mental existence, such as the concomitants of quiddity. And the way of refuting is that mental accidents do not have specificity to one quiddity, but each includes many quiddities not counted in custom as properties of one of them. As for the talk of the concomitants of quiddity, it is refuted by the restriction of 'all', for although some effects follow from mental existence, namely the concomitants of quiddity, all of them do not follow except on external existence.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage defines mental existence and distinguishes it from external existence. It begins by affirming that fire has an external existence with characteristic effects (illumination, burning). The dispute is whether it also has a mental existence without those effects. Mental existence is then defined as a shadowy existence. The commentary clarifies that the nature of existence may have different concomitants, and that judgments and effects refer to what is specific to the thing. It also explains that 'concrete' means existence in itself, while mental existence is existence of the form. The text then addresses several objections: the distinction between the two existences is not clear because mental existence also has effects like universals; but the answer is that mental accidents are not specific to one quiddity, and the concomitants of quiddity do not all follow from mental existence. The passage concludes that all specific effects follow only from external existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental existence has no effects whatsoever.
Correction: Mental existence does produce conceptual effects like universality, but these are not specific to any single quiddity; all specific effects belong to external existence.

Checkpoint
What is the main criterion distinguishing mental existence from external existence?
A) Mental existence has no effects whatsoever
B) External existence is the source of all effects, while mental existence produces only conceptual effects that are not specific to any single quiddity
C) Mental existence is a shadow of external existence and has no reality
D) External existence is for material things only

Key Takeaway
Mental existence is distinct from external existence because it lacks the specific effects that are uniquely associated with each external thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In what ways does this lesson's distinction between mental and external existence expose the danger of living in your imagination rather than reality?
?
Consider the concept of 'God' that you hold in your mind. Is your mental concept of God identical to God's true external existence? What specific attributes might your mind add or subtract, and how would that affect your worship and reliance on Him? Will you take one concrete step today to seek knowledge from revelation rather than relying solely on your mental image?
?
The author argues that mental existence lacks the specific effects of external things. Where in your daily life do you mistakenly treat a mental concept—like the idea of a person or a situation—as if it had the same power and reality as the external thing? What would change if you consciously acknowledged the difference?

What Is Kalam?
